THE RED PAGE
Verses by J. le Gay Brereton
I wove the fragrant boughs around
A mossy rock, and made a shrine
—An altar upon holy ground —
And here I poured ecstatic wine,
And broke my daily bread, and found
That Heaven and Earth and Hell were mine.
I spilled some wine upon the clay,
And moulded it with rugged skill
And made the lamp which here I lay.
For lovers kneeling here shall fill
My lamp on many a distant day.
And haply keep it burning still.
THE GRAVE.
In the grey daivn I lie within my bed
Still as a frozen lake that pats no more
With murmuring delight the o'erhanging shore,
Yet grim thoughts heave obscurely in my head ;
For curtains I have earthen walls, and lead
Is colder than the woollen garb I wore.
But, O, that heart of mine is still as sore
As when I did not know that I was dead.
I knew her (0 my Life !) and she was fair,
And gave her beauty to the hills and sea.
The wonder of her voice to leaf and wave.
The brown earth lies between us ; does she care
That since she cast the first dull clod on me
My lonely heart is aching in the grave 1
PANIC.
Out of the night
Springs wide-eyed with lips apart
Fright,
And clenches with intangible hand
About my struggling heart.
He twists me round
Obedient to inaudible command
And drives me, hounds me headlong down the slope,
Splashing in haste across the sodden ground.
God help us—hark !
What infant fear or dying hope
Is erying, crying, crying in the dark l
CLING TO ME.
Cling to me, love, and dare not let me go:
Km me as though it ivere our time to die,
And all our comradeship had drifted by.
Who knows what face to-morrow 1 s dawn may show l
All the night opens round us, dear ; and 10,
Mysterious deeps wherefrom the unseen eye
Of formless dread is gazing sleeplessly !
Over our love what shelter can 1 throw ?
How long have ive been seeking, and how far,
With time and space betwixt us, till at last
Our instant life makes glory of our pain l
The awful Night is round' us. Star on star
Calls us to wander, when our moment's past,
—Perchance upon that desolate quest again.
DREAM SHAPES.
Steal softly, visions of the night,
And do not break her rest,
But breathe a slumberous deep delight
Within her quiet breast.
Strange dream, whose face and form are mine,
Lie silent at her side
In healing peace and thought divine,
While shadowy moments glide,
Each leaving, as it wanders by,
A gift of special grace.
And on the pillow ’twixt you lie,
0 dream, a little face.
Perpetual Motion
IN a recent article we mentioned the
impasse into which modern science
had come in its efforts to prove the
eternal existence of the universe. It is
accepted now that matter is indestruct-
ible ; but modern thinkers are not so
sure that energy will not wear out. There
is a feeling that the universe is losing
energy, inconceivably slowly, but inevit-
ably. If this tired feeling of the cosmos
cannot be checked, we must contemplate
an ultimate universe of dead matter
clumped together by gravity in one enor-
mous inert and frozen mass.
However, modern science will not take
that decision lying down ; and more than
one theory has been put forward to ex-
plain how the machine may be con-
structed so that, not one particle of energy
is ever lost. Svante Arrhenius, in his re-
markable Worlds in the Making (Har-
per), has a feasible suggestion of escape
from the theory of the inevitable “de-
gradation ” of energy. According to that
theory all the energy of the universe will
ultimately come to a “heat death,” or
thermal equilibrium : the cosmos will be
of one uniform heat throughout, and
therefore incapable of doing any work.
Arrhenius’ postulate is that energy is
“ degraded ” in bodies which are in the
solar state, and energy is “ elevated,”
raised to a higher level, in bodies which
are in the nebular state. He conceives
that the universe has always been in its
essence what it is now. Matter, energy
and life have varied only as to shape and
position in space.
We may imagine the interior of the sun
charged with compounds which, brought to
the surface of the sun, would dissociate
under an enormous evolution of heat and an
enormous increase of volume. These com-
pounds have to be regarded as the most
powerful blasting agents, by comparison
with which dynamite and guncotton would
appear like toys.
In other words, every star is a danger-
ous dynamite charge, and ought at once
to be brought under the legislation re-
lating to explosives.
The discovery of the radiation pres-
sure of light that light actually
repels particles and thus overcomes
the attraction of gravity—has given
Arrhenius his clue to his theory.
Owing to the fact that gravity attracts
in proportion to the mass of a particle,
while the light repulses in proportion to
the surface of the particle, it is obvious
that if a spherule is sufficiently small it
will be repulsed from the sun against the
pull of the sun’s gravity. As Snyder, in
The Cosmic Machine, which discusses this
theory, puts it:
The mass of a body decreases in propor-
tion to the cube of its diameter, but the
surface decreases in proportion to the square.
It follows, therefore, that with smaller and
smaller bodies the relations between the
force of attraction and of light pressure
progressively alter ; gravitation decreases
much more rapidly than the force of light.
It is evident that there will come a point
whon the pressure of light is actually greater
than the pull of attraction. At this point
the particle has become exceedingly small;
but it will then be shot away from a highly
luminous body, even against the tremendous
gravitational pull of the sun itself.
Such a particle would travel at im-
mense speed, reaching the earth from the
sun in a few hours. And it is from these
particles meeting in space and fusing
together that meteorites are probably
born. An analysis of the loose structure
of meteorites confirms this theory.
In this fact Arrhenius has found
the perpetual motion of the universe.
Gravity and light act as the forward and
the backward beat of the cosmic piston ;
and we need not, therefore, fear the ulti-
mate clumping together of all the particles
of matter, including us.
But the theory explains more. ' The
particles expelled by the pressure of light
from the sun and stars are electrically
charged, and thus, incidentally, are re-
cognisable from the earth as the cause
of the polar lights, and other electrical
phenomena.'
vf/
And here Arrhenius makes use of his
other theory of explosive suns. After
life has ceased on the earth the sun will
condense and cool, but will retain its
heat for an inconceivable period, full of
gas under such immense pressure that
the sun will resemble a vast dynamite
magazine. Then it will ultimately meet
another dynamite magazine; and the
consequences will be worse than Bray-
brook. And with the explosive force
generated certain molecules of the gas
will be shot clear away from the gravita-
tion attraction of the nebula formed by
the smash-up, where, owing to the enor-
mous distances, the restraining force of
gravity—itself the weakest of all known
forces—-is exceedingly feeble.
Thus the nebulae will lose their most rapid
molecules from their outer portions, and
they will therefore be cooling in these outer
strata. If, now, there were only 'nebulae of
one kind in the whole universe, these escaped
molecules would finally land on some other
nebula, heat equilibrium would, thus be
established between the different nebulae,
and the “ heat-death ” would be established.
But the nebulae enclose many immigrated
celestial bodies, which are able to condense
the gases from their neighborhood, and
which thereby assume a higher temperature.
The lost molecules of gases may also stray
into the vast atmosphere of these growing
stars. By such processes the clock-work of the
universe may be maintained in motion with-
out running down.
W
Under Newton’s law of gravitation the
celestial bodies should tend to draw together,
to unite in ever-growing masses. In the
infinite space of past time the evolution
should have proceeded so far that some large
suns, bright or extinct, could alone persist.
All life would be impossible under such cir-
cumstances.
Yet we have found space full of' dust,
nebulse, and dark and bright suns of
moderate size, and planets.
The explanation of these apparent devia-
tions from the necessary consequence of the
exclusive action of gravity will be found in
the action of the mechanical radiation pres-
sure of light, and in the collisions between
celestial bodies. The latter produce enor-
mous vortices of gases about nebular struc-
tures ; the radiation pressure carries cos-
mical dust into the vortices, and the dust
collects into meteorites and comets. The
scattering influence of the radiation pres-
sure, therefore, balances the tendency of
gravitation to concentrate matter. The vor-
tices of gases in the nebulae only serve
to fix the position of the dust, which
is ejected from the suns through the ac-
tion of the radiation pressure In the
more rarefied, gaseous, cold portions of the
nebulse we find that part of the machinery of
the universe which checks the waste of
matter, and, still more, the waste of force
from the suns. . . . Thus every ray emitted
by the sun is absorbed, and its energy is
transferred, through the gaseous particles of
the nebulse, to suns that are being formed.
The explosive substances, formed by
the cooling of the suns, and dissipated at
their collision, play the chief part in this
evolution.
The enormous stores of energy concen-
trated in these bodies perform the duty
of powerfully acting fly-wheels interposed in
the machinery of the universe in order to
regulate its movements and to make certain
that the cyclic transition from the nebular to
the star stage, and vice versa., will occur ... in
an eternal circle, in which there is neither
beginning nor end, and in which life may
exist and continue for ever and undimin-
ished.
vl/
But Arrhenius makes a vastly more
comprehensive claim for his theory.
By means of it he accounts for
the presence of life on this globe.
He is not concerned with the origin of
life ; he assumes that life, like matter and
energy, have always been parts of the
mechanism of the universe. But the old
question arises: how is life generated
from matter? Arrhenius does not worry
about the generation of life, but shows
in his theory how life conceivably might
be disseminated through space, carried
from one star to another. He calls this
theory “ Panspermia.” According to it,
life-giving seeds (germs) are always drifts
ing about in space. They encounter the
planets, and fill their surfaces with life
as soon as the necessary conditions for
the existence of organic beings are estab-
lished. This, in a newer and more
scientific form, is the exploded sugges-
tion that life might have been carried
to this globe by meteorites. (This
theory was enunciated by Kelvin
in 1871.) For a meteorite arrives
in flame; its speed raises it to incan-
descence by contact with the atmosphere
of the earth, thus destroying the ger-
minating power of all. possible seeds or
germs.
\t/
It is to his theory of the repul-
sive radiation pressure of light, which on
spheres of a certain minuteness is more
powerful than the attraction of gravity,
that Arrhenius turns ; and he finds that
there are germs of the required minute-
ness known to biologists. Supposing
spores could escape from the earth by
being carried on an ascending air current
to the higher strata of our atmosphere,
and then, taking up a charge of negative
electricity from the solar dust during an
electric discharge, driven out into the sea
of ether by the repulsive charges of the
other particles, Arrhenius advances argu-
ments, too long for detailing here, to
show that the germinating power of such
spores could be retained intact for long
periods by the intense cold of space. One
of these many spores might fall on a
world that is conditioned to support life.
And on arrival, if, as is probable, this
spore was adhering to a particle of
cosmic dust, it would fall into the atmo-
sphere of that planet at quite a moderate
speed, and thus not be burnt up like a
meteorite. But the spores that met a
new solar system would not fall into the
sun, but be turned back by the radiation
pressure, and having lost the enormous
velocity wherewith they travelled from
one star to the next, would accumulate
somewhere near that sun, ready to fall
on any planet that happened to come
that way.
In this manner life may have been trans-
planted for eternal ages from solar system to
solar system, and from planet to planet in
the same system.
Seems clumsy, doesn’t it?
J. le Gay Brereton
With the spring has come the wattle and
the whispered message of J. le Gray. It is a
feminine and elusive message, hardly more
than a mood. For J. le Gay Brereton, in
his volume of verse, Sea and Shy (Lothian,
Melb.) is as distant as the sky and'as damp
as the se i, and in his little lyrics tiny clouds
drift softly over the blue and melt into
nothingness, and gentle wavelets ripple
pleasingly into peace.. It is true that the
poet stridently remarks that his is
A harsh, not all discordant note;
but J. le Gay is never harsh. He polishes
his little pebbles so lovingly that they have
the look of a schoolboy’s bag of marbles.
And we long in vain for a chunk of jagged
rock. Yet his labor results in some ex-
quisite things exquisitely said :
Shadow, a terror unshaken, who smothers the
breath with affright,
Sits like a statue uncarved in the heavy black mass
of the night. ...
And the insolent pomp of the ape in a robe of
state
And the sliding silence of guile.
Brereton, having nothing new to say, says
it charmingly ; and the work in this book is
not disfigured by the inclusion of anything
slovenly or false. His “Progress of the
Year ” is, maybe, the only unpoetic thing he
publishes. His “maidens fifty-two,” of
whom he remarks :
Seven-branched the candlestick
Each pale novice bears,
is a little too mathematically precise to be
poetry. But it is in the mastery of language
that the poet makes his final appeal. His
cunning use of alliteration in such a phrase
as :
When he comes at last
To Autumn’s welcome gateway, past
The orchards where the peaches grow,
shows him, as it showed his master, Tenny-
son, at his best.
Towards the end of his little book of
lyrics, Brereton grows rather too domestic
for the taste of the general reader. The
section “ Oithona,” with such charming
verses as these :
When shall we kiss that blossomy face of thine,
Oithona, when across the starlit straits
Thou flit teat till the hither region shine
With fires of welcome, and, her hand in mine,
Thy longing mother waits ?
O parted, smiling lips, so tender red,
When shall our ears that oft in dreams have
heard
Quaint prattle and glad laughter, and were fed
As starving men in visions, taste instead
At last a waking word ?
will, however, be read with interest by
those of our readers who are expecting by
practical means to aid in the solution of the
population problem. It is, however, the
father, not the poet, speaking when he in-
forms us of Oithona that:
Her soul shall be untamed,
And she will front the sun brown-skinned and
unashamed.
On the whole, this is a contribution to
Australian verse of which Australia need not
be ashamed. Such careful work, so deli-
cately done, is a rare portent in our vague
Australian sky ; and such criticism as has
been given to it has been occasioned by the
slightness of a message so poetically worded.
~—-
The Book Monthly, making a referendum
of the popularity of reprints of standard
works—not copyright books—issued in
Dent’s “ Everyman ” series, Frowde’s
“World’s Classics,” Routledge’s “New
Universal Library,” and Cassell’s “People’s
Library,” arranges the best sellers of dead
authors in this order of popularity : Tenny-
son (who is named in all four series) ; Lamb,
Palgrave’s Golden Treasury, and George
Borrow (named in three) ; and the following
authors named twice ; Chaucer, Emerson,
Matthew Arnold, Macaulay (History of Eng-
land), Ruskin (Sesame and Lilies), Dickens
(A Tale of Two Cities), and Carlyle (Sartor
Besartus). This list may cause some heart-
burnings in the literary purgatory—but that
can’t be helped.

THE LIVER BLAMED.
Sincerity of conviction and directness of pur-
pose mark the action of Mr. Thomas Theak in
giving for publication the ensuing lines, which
were written at his home, No. 88 Botany Road,
Alexandria.
“I am one of the few people living in Sydney
who has never been out of it,” was Mr. Theak’s
initial remark, “not that it is anything to boast
about, but purely an uncdmmon circumstance.”
“Your business does not conduce to much
travelling, perhaps?” said the interviewer.
“I’m a painter by trade, and there has been
plenty of work to keep me here, without wasting
time looking for it in other parts. Yes ; I have
done fairly well from a business standpoint, but
from a health point of view I was not always so
fortunate. Let me give you, for example, the
time that I found it necessary to start taking
medicine, and I must have been pretty bad to
*take that on, I tell you. It was because I was
much weaker than 1 should have been, conse-
quent, I firmly believe, on working too hard. I
had the unpleasant experience of never being able
to relish my food, no matter how daintily pre-
pared, for my appetite had fallen away to nothing.
I was also troubled with most uncomfortable feel-
ings, following upon the taking of foods, the
stomach filling with gases, and causing pain, be-
sides being oppressed with a dreariness of mind
which made it too much trouble to devote my
thoughts to anything. I felt fit for nothing but
to go and lie down after meals, and a person in a
healthy state should not be like that, you know.”
“That condition is a sure indication that some-
thing has gone wrong with the liver?”
“Precisely; and that’s what I was suffering
from. I went on ailing for a good length of time,
going from bad to worse, and wondering what on
earth would cure me and make my life a bit more
bearable, when a son of mine who had been using
Clements Tonic advised me to try some. Well,
that was the best introduction I have ever had,
because Clements Tonic has been a firm friend of
mine ever since. But before I get too far ahead
of my story, let me tell you of the way I suffered
from headaches —so heavy and acute that I could
scarcely raise my head from the pillow, and mak-
ing me wish that I could leave this earth and all
its misery behind. A coated tongue and a vile
taste in the mouth started the day’s round of dis-
agreeable circumstances, and my sight got so dim
as to lead me to think that a skin was growing
over them. Then the giddy feelings—why, some-
times I almost dropped, through their sudden
visits, and my shoulders were so affected with
pain that it hurt me to move. Indeed, on one
occasion I was for a whole week and could not
raise my arms. The chest and stomach were also
out of gear, and on top of it all I was listless and
low-spirited to a marked degree. Each morning I
got up feeling that I did not care what happened,
always being more inclined to go back to bed
again than to dress myself. There was sufficient
evidence to show that my nervous system was feel-
ing the strain of my physical adversities, so you
will see there was a big contract in front of Clem-
ents Tonic when I made my first purchase of that
valuable remedy. But I found it very much up-
to-date, believe me. There was no taking of half-
a-dozen bottles, and feeling no better, as applies
to other medicines. Oh !no ; neither do I say that
I felt all right after taking one bottle of Clements
Tonic ; yet I can truthfully assert that I felt some-
what relieved, and that by judiciously sticking to
that treatment I conquered in the end. Yes,
Clements Tonic gained a complete mastery over
each of my ailments, and left me in the happy
position of being able to eat and drink whatever
I liked, without experiencing any ill effects after-
wards. Its power over the nerves is extraordi-
nary, its appetising influences are unique, its value
as a promoter of sleep is unrivalled, and for curing
indigestion and building up one’s strength I’m
positive there is nothing to come within coo-ee of
Clements Tonic.”
“ Well, we’ll print your remarks ? ”
“Do—in any form you like to mention, or de-
cide upon.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, THOMAS TJIEAK, of 88 Botany Road, Alexandria,
in the State of New South Wales, Commonwealth
of Australia, do solemnly and sincerely declare that
I have carefully read the annexed document, and that it
contains and is a true and faithful account of my illness
and cure by Clements Tonic, and also contains my full per-
mission to publish in any way my statements—which I
give voluntarily, without receiving any payment; and I
make this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the
same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the
Oaths Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Waterloo, this twenty-
seventh day of February, one thousand nine hundred
and eight, before me,
tl] W. A. POWELL, J.P.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Rhyme of Rolling Forties.
The salted sea
Delights not me
In picture, song, or story ;
I want no more
Of waves that roar
And swell in foaming glory.
A-near the Line
The weather’s fine;
But when you go
Where winds do blow,
And get amidst the hail and snow,
In May or June—yah ho ! yah ho 1
A sea trip then no sport is.
Disgusting tricks the ocean played,
When, from the Cape to Adelaide,
The good ship Boshter’s course was laid
Along the Rolling Forties.
One’s attitudes
In latitudes
That verge on number 50
Are apt to make
A body ache,
So bent are they, and shifty.
You spread your feet
To keep your seat,
Or slide and slip
Around the ship,
And sprain your leg, or bruise your hip,
And in your cabin “ get the pip ”
For tightly closed the port is ;
And, as the moments drag along,
Half right side up, and inside wrong,
You listen for the dinner gong,
And curse the Rolling Forties.
No blessed boat
That goes afloat
(Though none be trim and tauter)
Shall bear my weight
To 48
In South Pacific water.
For there I froze
And nursed my woes
On water that
Was never flat,
But rose and fell
Like hill and dell,
And moaned as though not feeling well.
When parsons threaten me with H—ll
Emphatic my retort is.
H—ll’s latitudes are warm, no doubt,
But small discomforts one can flout,
Who has for days been knocked about
Among the Rolling Forties.
VIC.
O.K.
Spring Morning.
The hours speed blue and sunny,
Busy the wild bee thrums,
Now manna-dew and honey
Spill o’er from old red gums.
Spring reawakens, spreading
Her garment wattle gold ;
She chants Earth’s hymn of wedding,
Lambs frisk within the fold.
Warm morning winds come flocking,
Birds twitter in the nest;
The mother croons, a-rocking
Her baby at the breast.
The Bush gleams fresh and dewy,
At last by Chatting Creek
A swagman, humping bluey,
Finds what he tramped to seek.
To Earth, grown old and crabby,
The joy of youth she brings,
' The bloom, the bud, the baby—
All tender, wistful things.
She smooths out Winter’s wrinkles ;
And from her charmed horn
With holy dew she sprinkles
The Resurrection Morn.
VIC.
LOUIS ESSON.
Vanity Fair.
0 Vanity Fair, Vanity Fair !
My heart goes back to you.
For I only found you dear and rare—
And I only found you true.
What matter to me your brazen marts,
Your jests and fickle ways,
For me you’re the city of light young hearts.
And dear delicious days !
O Vanity Fair, where lies your blame?
Our life is but a span ;
And love and joy, and maybe shame,
Make up the life of man.
For we are dead—a long time dead—
And swift are the bright hours gone,
Though short the days of joy ahead,
We’ll follow the piper on.
O Vanity Fair, how oft we laughed,
How many songs we sung ;
The toasts we drank, and the wine we quaffed,
When all the world was young !
1 drink to those delicious days,
1 drink to the girls I kissed,
And ever I sigh for the vanished ways,
And the maids I may have missed.
0 Vanity Fair, with your skies so blue—
A banner above your head—
My heart goes back to the lure of you,
And the young years, dear and dead.
O Vanity Fair, Vanity Fair !
For me the grey years start,
But over the seas of spice and myrrh
The long winds bear my heart.
N.S.W.

The Multiplying and Vanishing Billiard Ball Trick.
Unique Billiard Ball.
ħ
By far the best and most effective billiard
ball trick ever invented. This is a superior
method to any other billiard ball trick ever
yet performed. The pecularity of it is that
the balls can be given for examination as
they are produced, to show they are solid.
The performer having turned up his sleeves
and shown his hands empty, gives a minia-
ture billiard ball, about one inch in diame-
ter, for examination. The audience being
satisfied, he takes it in his left hand and
passing his right hand over it shows two.
Again a mystic pass, and there are three,
which he shows are quite solid. The audi-
ence now see, to their astonishmet, four
balls. These gradually vanish one after the
other, till there is only one left, which is
again passed round for examination. An
adequate description cannot be given of this
most delightful effect. If is one of the
prettiest little sleights seen for a long time.
The billiard ball set is made in *• ivorine,"
and can be made in any size. The illustra-
tion will give our customers some idea of
the effect that can ;»o obt.i, ,vith this complete set. The sleeves are turned
back whilst performing this trick the whole time, the hands shown empty, and
at any minute, whilst the trick is beiug- performed, the halls can he passed for
inspection.
This trick requires no sleight of hand, and can he performed by any person.
The balls can be made to multiply or vanish at the will of the operator.

Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods o f address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad vertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. 'To isend them, to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is riot a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the gropes
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, if
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead or
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, OCTOBER 15, 1908.
THE YASS-CANBERRA CAPITAL: A
NEW DEVELOPMENT.
The ballot in the House of Representatives for a
Federal Capital produced a remarkable and unex-
pected result. At the first ballot Dalgety received
27 votes, and nine other sites acquired 45 votes
among them—an average of five suffrages apiece.
Lake George couldn’t raise a single vote. The
boasted Canberra got one; Tumut, which was
once chosen as the capital, collected three votes;
Lyndhurst had half-a-dozen. But as one dead
site after another dropped out, votes concentrated
on a new locality described as “Yass-Canberra,”
and in the last ballot it beat Dalgety by six votes.
Of course this resolution isn’t final, for the Senate
has yet to have its say ; but assuming that
the Senate endorses the selection, and Yass-
Canberra is decided on by both Houses, then a
new and interesting set of complications will
arise. For the new site is large and vague. It is
generally described as a triangle touching Yass at
one point, Canberra at another, and Lake George
at a third. This means the surrender by N.S.W.
of a fairly-large mass of territory, and inside this
territory there is space for several wild-cat fights
as to the location of the capital city itself. These
fights have still to come off, and they promise a
fair amount of excitement.
+ * * *
Furthermore, the Yass-Canberra crowd cadged
for votes by certain representations and half-
promises that may lead to a lot of
worry later on. There was a burning desire to
shift the capital site from Dalgety for two reasons.
One was the desire of the State Frights party to
assert the supremacy of the N.S.W. State Legis-
lature over the Parliament of all Australia by
driving the latter body to give up, at the bidding
of the State Legislature, the place it had origin-
ally selected. The other was the craving to bring
the capital closer to Sydney, so that its trade
might further congest that already congested port,
and concentrate more population in a district
which is already ninety times more thickly peopled
than the average of the State. In its haste to do
these things, the Yass-Canberra crowd took
chances. One of Dalgety’s strong points is the
splendid Snowy River and its tributaries—a
cataract of mountain water, tumbling eternally
down from the Kosciusko range, bringing with it
an unlimited supply of drinking material and
water power, and so situated that, with a capital
at Dalgety, it must necessarily be located in
Federal territory, and under Federal control.
Another great advantage is the handy and con-
venient Federal port of Twofold Bay. Against
these the Yass-Canberraites, in their anxiety to
grab votes, set the Barren Jack reservoir as a
source of water supply and water power, and
Jervis Bay as an even better harbor than Two-
fold Bay. And by doing this they gave two sort-
of-hvpothetical I 6 U’s, which will require a very
considerable amount of redemption.
* * * *
In the first place, will the N.S.W. State Govern-
ment surrender its boasted Barren Jack reservoir,
and the streams that supply it, to the Common-
wealth ? If it won’t do so, then the Common-
wealth will never be more than a lodger in a
strange house, and the Commonwealth will pro-
bably object. There is water —though not too
clean water—in Barren Jack, but if it is to be ex-
clusively the State’s water, then the Federal city
will only get a drink on sufferance, and the first
time it gets into a condition of disagreement with
the State Government it may get no drink at all.
There is also a certain amount of water power to
be obtained from Barren Jack, though not so
much as could be acquired from the Snowy; but
if it is to be exclusively the State’s power supply,
then the Federal city will only use it by
the State Government’s permission and kind
forbearance, and whenever it chances to have
a squabble with Wade or his successors
it may find its electric light turned into
darkness and its power supply vanished.
It isn’t sufficient for the State Government to say
that only a shocking slanderer would suggest that
it would do anything of this sort. If it insists
on retaining possession of Barren Jack, then it
carefully insists on having the power to do both
these things, and it is in the position of having
strained every nerve to get the Federal city shifted
from a place where it would have its own water
and power supply to a spot where it will only have
these things at the State Government’s discretion.
And there is no reason why the Wade Ministry
should make such extraordinary efforts to get an
extraordinary and dangerous power unless it
intends to use that power when it feels inclined.
Barren Jack reservoir has been pushed forward so
persistently as one of the advantages of the Yass-
Canberra site that it is evident that the eligibility
of that site depends largely on Barren Jack. If it
doesn’t, then the whole business is something of a
confidence trick. And if it does, and the Aus-
tralian Government doesn’t get Barren Jack, and
has no more control over it than over the Sea of
Galilee, that will also be something of a confidence
trick. Should it be that the waters of Barren
Jack and of the streams which feed it must needs
be divided between the Federal and State
authorities, then it is obvious that either the
Commonwealth must own the supply and the
State must trust to its honor to do the fair thing,
or else the State must own the supply and the
Commonwealth must trust to it to be honest and
reasonable. There are two very solid reasons why
the former course should be adopted. First, if
the State cut off the Commonwealth’s water
supply it would reduce a big city to awful dis-
tress, while, if the Commonwealth cut off the
State’s supply, it would merely dry up a few
farms. Second, the State has plenty of other
water supplies ; the Commonwealth, if its capital
is planted on the Yass-Canberra triangle, will
apparently have no other supply worth mention-
ing.
* * * *
The question of a Federal port will also call for
quite a heap of consideration. In the advocacy
of, first, the Canberra site, and, second, the Yass-
Canberra site, a great deal was made of the fact
that Jervis Bay is a splendid harbor —better even
than Twofold Bay, the sea-outlet of Dalgety. But
if it transpires that the N.S.W. State Government
doesn’t propose to transfer Jervis Bay to the
Commonwealth, then all the votes that have been
gained by the booming of that harbor have been
gained under false pretences, and when this
fact is realised, there is a chance of trouble
which may yet knock the Y ass-Can berra
site into a cocked hat. On the other hand,
Yass-Canberra is further from the sea than
Dalgety, and a Yass-Canberra capital, which in-
cludes Jervis Bay, will necessarily involve the
surrender to the Commonwealth of much more
territory than the Australian Parliament asked
for on Monaro, and yet that area was pronounced
by Carruthers and Wade as absurd and un-
thinkable on account of its size. A Federal
city on the site now proposed, with a
Federal territory commencing at Yass on
one side and extending to and including
Jervis Bay on the other, and with the Barren Jack
reservoir and its feeders as a Federal water supply
under exclusive Federal control, would certainly
be a good enough arrangement to satisfy anybody;
and if that is the arrangement which is proposed
the N.S.W. State Legislature can’t be too rapid
in explaining the fact, in order to prevent the
Federal capital shindy breaking out again just
when it is supposed to have settled down.
* * * *
The proposed site for the Federal city lies off
any existing line of railway, and in that respect it
suffers from the same disability as Dalgety. In
fact, it suffers from a worse disability than Dal-
gety. When the Cooma railway in N.S.W. and
the Bairusdale railway in Victoria are connected,
Dalgety will be on a great main railway, extend-
ing from Rockhampton to Adelaide. But the
Yass-Canberra site lies away off an already exist-
ing main railroad. So the idea is to serve it by
means of a branch, or stump, or dog-leg line which
will have no special importance until it is ex-
tended to Jervis Bay, and which, even then, will
be only a branch and a side-show. And
it must be always borne in mind that
the ultimate tendency of the establishment
of a Federal capital and territory on the
Yass-Canberra site will be to cut off the Monaro
district from N.S.W. and add it to the Federal
territory. Monaro, if the Yass-Canberra capital
is established, will be bounded by Victoria on the
south, by the Federal area on the north, by the
sea on the east, and by mountains 7000 ft. high on
the west. There are only two possibilities for such
a place ; either it will ultimately become part of
Victoria or it will in the end become an addition
to the Federal property. Yet, despite all
drawbacks and disadvantages, there is no reason,
why the selection of the Yass-Canberra site should
not be the solution—for a time at least—of a very
difficult problem. Much depends on whether the
booming of Barren Jack as a Federal city water
supply, and Jervis Bay as a Federal port, were
honest recommendations or mere swindles and
stale confidence tricks. If they were put forward
honestly, then there is no reason why the Yass-
Canberra capital should not last until the inevitable
northward movement of population upsets all
present arrangements and lifts the capital into a
new region altogether.
JONAH AND THE WHALE.
I joined in risking: for this State [N.S.W.] the loss of
next door to a purely Revenue Tariff, which loss has,
from my point of view, unfortunately taken place. . . .
Surely few can truthfully deny that, for the sake of
union, N.S. W. has made the greatest sacrifices of any
State in the Commonwealth, notwithstanding its pro-
mised possession of the capital. . . . Permit me to add
that I object to be classified as a Tory. In the Mother
Country, of which I have the honor to be a native, it is
the Protectionists who compose the Tory party, not the
Freetraders. We Freetraders maintain ours is a broader,
fairer, more peaceful and happier fiscal faith than is Pro-
tection, with which, alas, you still seem to be enamored,
not to say infatuated. — Letter by Senator Walker to The
Bulletin (8/10/’GB).
At first glance the connection between the
Prophet above-mentioned and the Senator above-
quoted may seem remote, but it is really closer
than appears. In certain circles it is fashionable
to be two things—strictly orthodox and a believer
in Freetrade. A belief in the absolute accuracy
of Cobden and of Holy Writ are as much a matter
of course as a plug hat and a tidy Sunday
umbrella. Therefore the orthodox person believes
(theoretically) in absolutely free imports and free
competition and in giving no preference whatever
to local over foreign goods. Also he believes
(theoretically) in Jonah and the whale. But as a
matter of fact his faith is only an item of dull old
habit, and arises out of a profound incapacity for
independent thinking. That incapacity makes a
man'keep on believing (theoretically) in what he
doesn’t really believe worth a cent. He hasn’t, in
reality, a penn’orth of faith in a literal Jonah and
a literal whale. He wouldn’t give a shilling of
his own cash for all the whale in that amazing
story—whalebone, oil, ambergris and the entire lot.
And his belief in real Freetrade is just the same.
It is habit—the shell of something that remains
behind after the substance has departed.
* * * *
There was nothing more laughable in all the
Southern Hemisphere than the old N.S.W. Free-
trade party to which Senator Walker belonged.
It was the prize joke and hypocrisy of the century.
Even when it came nearest to Freetrade it heavily
protected, through the Customs, sugar, beer,
wine, tobacco, biscuits, confectionery, jams and
jellies, etc. These represented fairly big indus-
tries. It didn’t protect them for the sake of
revenue, but solely for want of any real confidence
in its own alleged doctrine. The essence of Free-
trade is to place the local product and the foreign
one on an absolute equality. Therefore, if it was
necessary to tax at the Customs the imported
articles above enumerated in order to obtain
revenue, the simple and obvious way to preserve
the blessings of Freetrade, and at the same time to
procure more revenue, would have been to levy
exactly equal Excise duties on the locally made
articles. But there wasn’t one alleged Free-
trader who believed sufficiently in his own Free-
trade to do that. The so-called Freetrade party
deliberately sacrificed much good revenue in order
to depart from its alleged principles. It
didn’t turn traitor to its principles for
money, but it gave away good money
for the privilege of being a traitor. And
this wasn’t the only matter in which it made it-
self a mighty jackass before the Lord. Being
convinced that its wheat, oats and hay industries
couldn’t live under real Freetrade conditions it
subsidised them, surreptitiously, but to an enor-
mous extent, by carrying the produce of these
industries by rail at rates varying from one-half
to about one-sixth or one-eighth of the real cost
of carriage; by carefully differentiating in the
matter of railway rates between N.S.W. produce
coming towards the coast and imported produce
going inland ; by differentiating, in many cases,
in the matter of wharfage rates between N.S.W.
produce exported and foreign produce imported ;
by subsidising the farming industry with total
exemption from local rates, and by many other
underhand devices. One great reason why
N.S.W. never had a real Local Government
system till Protection came along was that it was
necessary to exempt the farmers from local rates
as a sort of surreptitious bonus in order to keep
these bucolic persons afloat under alleged Free-
trade conditions. On top of all it was considered
necessary to give the N.S.W. Freetrade penny
paper 50 per cent. Protection by charging the
imported penny paper Jd. postage while the local
one was carried gratis.
* * * *
Amazing as was the alleged Freetrade tariff of
N.S.W. at the time when the Commonwealth was
established, it is worth while to remember that it
was quite a new tariff, and quite an unusual
departure towards Freetrade. Though it pro-
tected, openly or surreptitiously, some of the
leading industries of N.S.W., yet the Freetraders
did not dare to bring in even that instalment of
Freetrade until the Reid Government had set the
Federation movement going, and felt reasonably
sure that Protection—Federal Protection—was at
hand. When it became almost certain that
the Freetrade system wouldn’t get a long
enough trial to cover its adherents—the . party
that Senator Walker belongs to—with utter
disgrace, th 9 N.S.W. Freetrade party brought
in a tariff that was a wild parodv on
Freetrade, yet came nearer to it than anything it
had previously attempted. Therein it revealed
the fact that it only nominally believed in Free-
trade because it was the “ correct thing ” —just as
it was, and still is, the correct and orthodox thing
to believe that a real meat whale swallowed
Jonah, and that an undigested prophet lived in
the animal for three days. When it was fairly
certain that Freetrade would have a very short
trial, and that the Shoddy party would be able to
profess that its policy had been reluctantly torn
from it by a Federal Parliament dominated by
Protectionist States, one might have thought that
the N.S.W. Freetrade party would have taken
the chance and for once gone the whole hog of
its professed gospel. Yet it dare not go the
whole hog, even when it had reason to believe
that its nominal doctrine would only have
to stand the test of four or five years.
It began at the commencement of 1896 with a
mongrel tariff. It shortly afterwards took the
first step on the back track to Protection by re-
versing its decision to put the sugar industry on a
Freetrade basis. And in 3f years N.S.W. got so
tired of even this partial Freetrade that it threw
out the Reid crowd and called the Protectionists
back to office. As for the early “Freetrade”
tariffs—those of the Parkes and Robertson
period—they were a jest. A Freetrade tariff
that levied a huge Protective duty even on gal-
vanised iron buckets was beneath derision.
* * * *
N.S.W. was the only State which had, for a
lengthy period, an alleged Freetrade party in
office with a strong majority. If that party, of
which Senator Walker seems fairly proud, had
once—even once—gone the entire animal on its
alleged creed, and abolished all the surreptitious
railway and postal preferences, and balanced any
necessary Customs duties with corresponding
Excise duties, in order to keep intact the beautiful
principle of Free Competition, it would have
commanded respect. Every honest belief com-
mands respect. But there was never a single
influential voice raised in N.S.W. —not even that
of Senator Walker —for genuine Freetrade. All
the alleged Freetraders knew that real Freetrade
would bankrupt the great Tooth and Toohey
breweries, would wreck the wine industry, and
the tobacco industry, and the sugar indus-
try, and would shake the wheat industry
to its foundations. Their belief in real Freetrade
was just as sound as the literal faith in Jonah,
which faith they took with them on Sunday, when
they hunted out the plug hat and the silken gamp,
and went to church, and dropped a threepenny
that looked like a small full moon in the plate.
The Bulletin, in its earliest days, was dubious
and uncertain in its fiscal beliefs. One great reason
why it soon became uncompromisingly Protec-
tionist was because it couldn’t find, in all N.S.W.,
one prominent Freetrader who would have real
Freetrade at any price. It reckoned that when a
creed was so foul that its own warmest advocates
wouldn’t touch it, it was a good creed to leave
alone.
* * * *
N.S.W. still sends into the Australian Parlia-
ment a number of representatives of its bogus
“ Freetrade” party, though it is pleasing to note
that they are growing fewer at each election.
Their attitude, and the attitude of the papers
which back them, has created an ill-feeling against
the eldest State of the Commonwealth that will
not be eradicated for years. In the first place,
the bogus Freetraders of this little squad assume
airs of superiority as persons who possess the only
genuine fiscal gospel, and their pity for the be-
nighted Protectionist is large and obvious. If
they had shown their belief in their own professed
creed by putting it resolutely and honestly to the
test when they held a big majority in the old
N.S.W. Parliament, this attitude would inspire
respect; but for those Freetraders who surrepti-
tiously protected the State’s biggest industries,
and yet pose loftily in the presence of fiscal
opponents who at least live up to their creed,
there is nothing save contempt. Again, statis-
tics not only prove that N.S.W. has progressed
far more rapidly under the Commonwealth’s
openly Protectionist tariff than it did under its
old semi - Freetrade - surreptitious - Protectionist
system, but that it has also made better progress
under the openly and honestly Protectionist tariff
of the Commonwealth than any other State has
done. Therefore the whine of the Senator Walker
party about the great sacrifices it made in giving
up the Freetrade it never possessed, and, if its
actions count for anything, never really wanted to
possess, is a cause of disgust. Lastly, the attitude
of certain alleged Freetrade members and certain
alleged Freetrade papers excites still more disgust.
When the matter in question is Protection for an
industry in another State, William Wilks is a
Freetrader with an all-round dislike to Protection.
When it is a question of Protection for an
industry in his own electorate, the attitude
of William Wilks is expressed in the
words: “I am a Freeetrader, but if there
must be this nefarious Protection I demand a
share of it for my own constituents.” Therefore
by being an alleged Freetrader, the Wilks per-
son is able to vote for Protection for his own con-
stituents and no Protection for everybody else’s
constituents, and at the same time to wear an
aspect of great mental sorrow for all people who
believe in Protection. In the same way, the
S. M. Herald and Sydney Telegraph feel quali-
fied to (1) denounce Protection generally as a
curse and as the creed of persons of deficient in-
tellect ; (2) oppose Protection when it applies to
the industries of other States ; (3) demand Pro-
tection for the N.S.W. iron industry ; (4) declare
that it is only the jealousy of other States which
prevents the N.S.W. iron industry getting Pro-
tection ; and (5) describe Protection in general as
a conspiracy to benefit at the public expense “a
few tinpot Melbourne manufacturers.”
* * * *
N.S.W. still elects, through habit, certain
Freetraders, of whom Senator Walker is one.
But if it was awakened by the staggering news
that it would get real, all-round honest Freetrade
if it selected Senator Walker and his colleagues
again, and if it was explained to N.S.W. what
complete Freetrade means, it is safe to bet that
it would get six new Senators right away. And
if Senator Walker and his five colleagues had to
vote either for High Protection or complete Free-
trade—not only for absolute Freetrade at the
Customs but for the abolition of all the surrepti-
tious aids, bonuses and preferences which used to
take the place of honest Protection in N.S.W. —
with a knowledge that they would get the real
Freetrade they voted for, this paper is prepared
to bet a trifle that they would vote for High Pro-
tection. Real Freetrade, nowadays, is only a
pose, a lofty mental attitude, and an old habit—
the same as the habit of believing in Jonah and
his marine animal.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Mean Colonial Party and its Craving
for a Mean Australian Capital.
Mr. J. M. Purves (Messrs. Batt, Rodd and Purves)
said : “I am really very glad of the result of this ballot,
because I think, of all the positions that were voted on,
Yass-Oanberra is the fairest for all parties concerned—
i.e., it ought to suit everybody. I do not personally
know the exact locality’ very well, but I understand that
it is very good country, and, if the water question can be
properly dealt with, the place, it seems to me, is
very suitable. The climate, I know, is all that can
be desired, and there are other advantages; but
the Federal capital, wherever it may be, will never
be more than a country town of any’ extent. The set-
tling of the people who have to live there, and those who
will live upon them, will, of course, lead to a certain
amount of added cultivation in and around the township ;
but unless thev strike gold or some other mineral wealth
the town can never be of any importance. For instance,
look at Washington—it is dead to the world when the
American Parliament is not in session. When I was there
there was only’ one street of shops, and it consisted chiefly
of boarding-houses and a few private residences.”—
Interview (a fair sample of a large number ) in Sydney
Telegraph.
When the House of Representatives nominated
the Yass site, by the dirty and undrinkable
Murrumbidgee, for the Australian capital, the
Sydney morning papers—which are anti-Aus-
tralian all the time—at once set out to interview
the pallid bagmen who sit in offices, as to the
suitability of the arrangement. And these
natural empire-builders mostly approved. Proba-
bly the one quoted above was the most character-
istic of the lot. He voiced to perfection the un-
patriotic and anti-Australian craving of the
dealers and traders who sit in offices for a
mean capital, and their unpatriotic anti-Austra-
lian craving to assure the people of this country
that they are a poor lot Who Can’t Do Things.
The capital of Australia, according to the person
quoted above, “will never be more than a country
town”; also “unless they strike gold or some
other mineral wealth the town can never be of any
importance.” True, other countries have grown
great inland cities, even if their people didn’t
strike gold. Paris, Berlin, Vienna, Birmingham,


Huddersfield, Leeds, Manchester, Nottingham,
Salford, Breslau, Brussels, Buda-Pesth, Chicago,
Dresden, Milan, Moscow, Munich, Prague, Flor-
ence, Turin, Denver, Omaha, Damascus, Teheran,
Irkutsk, Warsaw, Madrid, and a few dozen more,
are all great —some of them very great —far in-
land cities which have grown without any
adjacent gold discoveries to prop them up.
Even Washington, which the amazing Purvks
describes as “only one street of shops” and
“chiefly boarding-houses and a few private resi-
dences,” is a city of well over 300,000 population,
and it is growing so fast that it bids fair to leave
Sydney behind ere very long. But, though other
nations can raise great and noble inland capitals,
the Mean Colonial party assures Australia
that it must not think of doing anything
of the kind. Its people, according to the
Mean Colonial party, are a poor crowd who can’t do
as other communities do; therefore, they must
be content with a mean capital, and the only city
in all the world which is the political centre of a
whole continent is to “never be more than a
country town,” and “ never be of any import-
ance.” And already the crowd which secured
the partial and temporary selection of the
Yass site is explaining how the conspiracy to
make the Australian city poor and mean is to be
carried out; and talks about limiting its com-
munication with the outer world to, at most, a
right of way for a railway to Jervis Bay and
the use of “a proportion of the sea front-
age” there. The curse of Australia is this
Mean Colonial spirit—the spirit which up-
holds the States always against Australia,
and regards Australia as an enemy ; which
calls eternally for imported goods as against Aus-
tralian products; which perpetually assures the
Australian people that they are a poor, wretched
lot and Can’t Do Things ; which thinks a sort of
dog kennel is good enough for the capital of Aus-
tralia because it is only Australia, the Place That
Doesn’t Matter. And of that spirit the Sydney
Telegraph is the prophet, and the people it is
so fond of interviewing at a crisis are the apt
disciples.
Federal Politics.
The “ Opposition ” in the Australian Parliament
has elected during the month to do its washing
in public. The state of its linen was not edifying.
It seems that there is a small Reid following, a
very small one, which is tormented by a doubt as
to whether it should not abandon Mr. Reid, who
will not attend to the work of leadership, and
follow Mr. Cook, who works with great energy,
but with so little wisdom that he could never
lead a party to victory. Then there is a Knox
section, which is suspected of having at least
one follower; a Forrest section, with one fol-
lower ; an Iceberg Irvine section, which so far
does not seem to have attracted a single Polar
bear to its frozen hummocks; a section which
is Wilks, and wants to make its Balmain seat
secure by becoming a Protectionist or a Laborite ;
and other sections too nebulous to mention.
Parliament, therefore, resolves itself into these
elements : a Government which has a policy but
no electoral organisation, and which consequently
will, at the next elections, be probably left
without a party; a Labor party which has a
policy and an organisation, and which clearly holds
a mortgage on the future if the organisation does
not get too strong for the party, and, by bolting
from the shafts, upset the whole show ; an Oppo-
sition which has no policy and no organisation,
but ha.s Reid and other incubi. The prospects are
not bright for the Opposition. It wants more than
a leader; it wants also a policy ; and then its only
chance would seem to be from a break-up in the
Labor Party, a break-up of which there is a small
threat on the horizon now. As for the Govern-
ment after next elections, it will probably not
count, presuming that it follows its present course
of allowing its case to go by default.

“Not Guilty’’-Then Who Is?
After a trial extending.over 13 days, the jury last
Friday decided that the drivers of the Bendigo
express which crashed into the Ballarat train at
Sunshine station last Easter Monday night, and
killed two score persons, besides injuring many
others, were not guilty of the manslaughter with
which they were charged. Except for counsel’s
addresses and the Judge’s summing up, the whole
of those days were occupied by the Grown wit-
nesses in giving their evidence —the accused,
beyond theirpleaof “not guilty” and their reports
to the Department, made no statement what-
ever—and on that evidence the jury acquitted
them. The stationmaster, Kendall, has yet
to be tried. But whether he be acquitted or not
makes little difference in fixing the responsibility
between the drivers and the Department; for the
error in signalling which he has admitted was not
pleaded by the drivers as palliation or excuse.
The jury, of course, gave no reasons, but it is
obvious that it must have accepted the drivers’
reasons why the collision occurred, viz., that the
brakes, after being applied, “came off” spon-
taneously, and when applied “at emergency”
failed to hold the train. In support of
this plea they had the evidence of Crown
witnesses —of the Westinghouse brake inspector,
that the excessive piston travel in some
instances would cause the brake application to be
light; of Professor Kernot, that the brake gene-
rally was “a feeble one, and by no means per-
fect” ; of expert Bagley, that, “had he been
train-examiner, he would by no means have
allowed the train to go out, and that, had the
rear carriages of the Ballarat train been new
instead of old ones, the collision would have been
much less disastrous”; and of many other witnesses,
that the practice in the Department is for drivers
to go past “ distance ” signals, though against
them, at a high rate of speed, and to rely on the
brakes to pull them up at the “home” sig-
nal ; also, that the train was behind time
(through the fool-practice of running long
trains that have to be pulled up twice at each
stopping-place), and that the Department expects
drivers to make up such lost time. From the
evidence —all of it the Crown’s evidence—and the
jury’s verdict thereon, the only conclusion
deducible is that the twelve, after carefully
balancing pros and cons for 4J hours, decided that
the masters, not the servants, were culpable;
that, in fact, the Department’s practice of re-
quiring men to do more work than they were
able, of expecting them to take risks, and of
supplying them with machinery incapable of
coping with those risks, was responsible for
the awful holocaust of Easter Monday. As to
what the next step will be no man can say,
though there is a loud suspicion that the under-
taker will be hastily requisitioned and the whole
matter buried with the victims —except the bill.
But, lest there be another Sunshine disaster
presently, it would be well for Victorians to recol-
lect that Adolphus Bent has already appointed
three commissioners, Justice Cussen being chair-
man, to inquire into the whole question of Vic-
torian railway management, with , special refer-
ence to the late smash, and, having recollected
that, it must be for Victorians to insist that the
pall shall not be drawn over the corpse until
someone has set forth exactly why and where-
fore the corpse occurred.
The Victorian Land Valuation Swindle.
Ip it were not that the Victorian country land-
lords are nearly always cadging from the Govern-
ment, it might have been regarded as a remark-
able coincidence that, on the very day the country
M’s.L.A. were compelling Bent to crawl down
and withdraw his Land Valuation Bill, other
representatives of the same faction were besieging
the Works Department for public money to make
and maintain their roads and bridges. In the
morning they were begging for doles to do need-
ful works ; in the afternoon they were denouncing
a measure the object of which was to ensure a
larger revenue to all shires, by an honest
valuation of country lands and properties.
The obvious fact was that the country landlords
do not want any such valuation. They don’t want
honesty ; they want charity. And in the struggle
to get it, eight shires were represented through
their members on this special occasion. Every
one of these eight got a grant from the Endow-
ment Fund in 1906 (the latest returns available),
and most of them got, in addition, a special grant.
Only three out of the eight rated themselves at
Is. 6d. in the £ ; the other five let themselves off
at Is. This is bad enough, but the outrageously
low valuations which form the basis of the rate
are even worse. Take the first shire on the
list—Avoca. Its total capital valuation is
given in the municipal statistics as £307.040.
There are 900 houses in it; estimate them
at £250 each homestead, and we get £225,000.
Deducting this amount from the total valuation,
there remains £82,040 as the value of the bare
land. As there are 289,920 acres in the shire,
that gives ss. Bd. per acre as the value at which
the land is rated. On account of that absurd
valuation the revenue from rates was only £1595.
Even upon this shameful valuation, if the same
rate had been struck (2s. 4d. in the pound) as the
working men of Brunswick and Footscray have to
pay, Avoca’s revenue from rates would have
amounted to £3722, or. an increase of more than
£2OOO per annum. Instead, however, of collect-
ing that amount, or any part of it, these land-
owners’ representatives, finding money needed to
make and mend their roads, go cadging to the
Government for public funds —funds which the
men of Colling wood and Footscray have to pro-
vide, though for their own local purposes these
men have already had to pay 2s. 4d. in the £.
Nor does Avoea stand alone in this brazen
effrontery; this list deals with the whole eight:—

If the land were valued on an honest basis it
would not, however, be necessary to collect any-
thing like 2s. 4d. in the £, even though Brunswick
and Footscray do have to pay that rate —the same
Brunswick and Footscray to which subsequently
the rich country landlords come for charity. The
most outrageous case is that of the wealthy Western
shire of Colac. Here a large proportion of the
land is worth from £2O to £5O per acre. Reckon-
ing the average as £l2 (which is almost for certain
a serious under-estimate), then the total real value
would be £8,964,420. Deduct £562,500 for dwell-
ings (2250 at £250), and £8,401,920 remains as the
value of the land; and a 2s. 4d.-in-the-£ rate
would mean over £50,000, which is what these
rich Colac landlords would pay if they realised
their professed desire for all to be treated
alike, and if they consequently paid on
the basis on which Footscray and Brunswick
pay. By persistent and shameless rapacity
this rich shire of Colac has from first
to last obtained from the general taxpayer some-
thing like £250,000; and, in order to get that
spoliation of the Treasury continued, the one hope
of the rich landlords is to prevent an honest valu-
ation. If that were made, the robbery would be
so plain that it would be difficult to carry it
through. So Bent was squeezed, squeezed hard,
and Bent did just what on his record one might
expect that he would do. But the crying need to
stop this shameless robbery remains.
Bent—the Vicar of Bray.
In Thomas Bent’s recent abandonment of a
Ministerial Bill, on which he had professed to
stake his Government’s existence, it is more than
possible to perceive the working of a passion
stronger even than Bent’s love of office ; and that
is the man’s fear of the aspersions that would in-
evitably follow if office were torn from him. The
threatened defeat of the Land Valuation Bill was
Bent’s chance—the chance of his life —to pose as
boss Victorian patriot and reformer of the coun-
try’s obstructive land laws. It gave him
an opportunity to corner the Labor Party, to-
gether with every other party in the Victorian
Legislative Assembly which claims to be Liberal.
It put him in the position to reverse his old
evil reputation for Conservatism, and, had his
administration been satisfactory, it must have
resulted in his restoration to office in the
near future. But that, it seems almost certain,
was where Bent’s doubts came in. The man
clearly seemed afraid to lose his control of affairs
for even a month. As leader of the Opposition
he would sit to be pelted with proofs, and crushed
with revelations. Victoria thinks it knows its
Bent. . As a matter of fact, his worst features
are unsuspected by three-fourths of the voters, and
actually known to only a few of the more
enlightened. Bent is not a Conservative on prin-
ciple—or what is called principle in political life.
Assured of a majority for the Land Valuation
Bill, he would have passed it. There is probably
no Radical measure that he would not pass to keep
himself from being completely exposed. But his
dread of getting shifted makes him the tool of the
Assembly that tolerates him, and for his short-
comings in most needed legislation, not Bent, but
the other fellows, must be blamed. They have
always at hand a bad means to good ends. Bent is
the available bad means. If a majority in
the House had good ends in view they could
attain them through the Premier—the haunted
bluffer whose air of burly disregard for what
people and newspapers say of him is only a cloak.
In this Victorian Assembly it is not the dog that
wags the tail, but the tail that wags the dog.
Every member with a paltry local purpose to
serve compels Bent to serve it. He is the Tom of
all parties, and on condition that he pleases them
they continue to give him carte blanche to please
himself in those little matters which place him at
their mercy. The man in the street remarks
philosophically that Bent is bound to be thrown
out at next election —as though he were nowadays
a stumbling-block in the way of progress. It is
the House that is the obstruction ; for he would
be prepared to march in the van of progress if the
Parliament gave the word of command. He is
merely a political Vicar of Bray, caring not at
all about professions of faith, if by recantations
he can preserve himself in the vicarage. And the
shame attaches to the House that listens to him
with its tongue in its cheek —of the flock that
calls him shepherd.

Tlie Commonwealth and Immigration,
A howl has lately been raised against the idea that
the Commonwealth should have anything todo with
the introduction of immigrants. Now, one or two
things ought to be clear enough even to the obfus-
cated intellect of a State Frighter. First, immi-
gration is a necessity for developmental purposes
—to bring land under cultivation and to build up
manufactures. An intelligent immigration system
might in 10 years do work which would take a
century if the country had to wait for a home-
grown population. But far and away above the
developmental aspect stands the question of
defence. Australia wants a ready-made popula-
tion to provide it with means of maintaining this
country for the white man. Beside the question
of defence, all others are small; and were it not
for the threat of Asia, the immigration question
might well be left over for later consideration.
Probably Wade and Bent themselves would
admit that the pressing necessity for population
here is to ensure that the shores are protected ;
and, if they were honest enough to answer, it would
be as well to ask them in what part of Australia
defence (and therefore the immigrant) is most
needed. Surely even they would not dare to deny
that a million men in the Northern Territory
would be a better protection for Australia
than many more than a million congested in
the south-eastern corner of the country. For
purposes of defence Australia wants population
in every portion; but particularly it wants
population in those spots where to-day BINGHI
is the sole representative of the man on
the land. While New South Wales and
Victoria could well do with the right class of im-
migrants, the politician who would send them to
those States, when he could just as easily distri-
bute them in the North, would have all his
ingenuity taxed to show that he was not a traitor
to his country, and a silly person who might one
day be pulling a rickshaw with a brown gentle-
man lolling in its plush cushion. And the only
power by which the North can be populated is the
Australian Government. The Commonwealth is
the only friend the North has, and if the North
remains unfilled, it threatens to be only a matter of
a little time when there will be neither east, nor
south, nor west for the white man. So the whole
matter of immigration should be in the hands of
the Commonwealth ; and while readily accepting
suitable immigrants for any part of Australia, the
Commonwealth should deliberately and systemati-
cally neglect the whole of the settled portions—
and especially New South Wales and Victoria—in
favor of filling the places which now appear on the
map as flat white blanks.

Where Anti-Sosli Spells Lunacy.
In anticipation of the coming Federal elections,
an anti-Socialist “organiser” has just started a
tour of the big N.S.W. towns. He is the same
old organiser who toured on behalf of anti-Sosh
three years ago, and did no perceptible good to
the cause. His mission is to tell once more of the
horrors of Socialism, of the Blight that will
descend on the district that consorts with Labor,
and of the prosperity and joyousness which are
bound to arrive if things are allowed to lag in the
old rut. In every big centre there are some people
ready to listen to “organisers,” however crazy their
propaganda may be. So, even in the deadest of
districts, the barracker for more deadness gets a
following. It is a phenomenon, as surpising as it
is depressing, that in many of the big towns the
people whom the anti-Sosh organiser attracts are
not the village idiot and the local landlords exclu-
sively, but a fair proportion of shopkeepers and
business men as well. It is pathetic in places such
as Picton, Bathurst and the like to see small
traders adding their cheers to those of
wealthy land monopolists when the hired orator
from Sydney denounces “ the criminal folly of a
progressive Land Tax.” Many of these calamit-
ous asses have been bred and born in the town
that the organiser is paid to dissuade from approv-
ing of a land tax. From their childhood they have
been watching it dwindle in prosperity and popu-
lation. They have on the tips of their tongues the
names of once-prosperous fellow-citizens who
filed their schedules or were sold up by the bank
after strenuous efforts to combat the deadness of
things. In their mind’s eye they can see, even as
they express their approval of further stagnation,
the “To Let” sign in the shop windows.
They know why their town, and in con-
sequence, their own business is wilting.
It is because the ring fence of big estates forbids
expansion, and bans settlement. They are there
to do business, but in the town there is a con-
stantly decreasing tally of people to do business
with, and outside the town the population remains
stationary. One man holds the broad, rich acres
that should be supporting three or four hundred
families, and the odds are he imports everything
but his monthly hair-cut from Sydney. If there
is any unit of this community who should grasp
the need for a progressive land tax it is the up-
country tradesman. It is the only possible hope
a good many of him has of keeping his head
above the financial waters. Yet each Federal
election finds him barracking for Anti-Sosh ; and
the peripatetic “organiser” on behalf of that
foolish and negative creed is rushed by him in
droves. The social side of the question is a big:
factor in this tragic state of affairs. The back-
blocks trader, like the city clerk, suffers from a-
hankering to be on what his women folk regard as
the “ respectable ” side of the political fence
wherefore a whole host of citizens are insanely
willing to see their own prosperity slowly
strangled to death for the joy of voting the same-
ticket as the adjacent “Squire.”
B. Smith’s Friends Tried in a New Role.
The wrecked Aeon’s officers and passengers, who
spent a few solitary weeks on Christmas Island,
were landed in Sydney by the Manuka last week,
and promptly yarns got into the papers glossing
over the conduct of the Chinese crew. They
didn’t rush the boats, it was intimated—and the
fact that it is necessary to mention such a thing is
in itself a fairly stiff indictment of Chinese crews.
It was quite true that these Chinese didn’t rush
the boats. But there was no reason why
they should have rushed the boats. There was
nothing to create panic. The vessel went on
the reef without a bump; it did the nasty trick
so softly that it might have been a roll of velvet
it had landed on top of. There was no sea, no
storm, no rushing in of water, the engines and
lights were kept going, and land was not more
than 200 yards away. Even the engineers had to
be told what had happened. That the Chinese
crew abstained from rushing the boats wasn’t
therefore anything to the men’s credit. Neither, for
that matter, was the way they got the boats into
the water, later on. So disorganised were they,
and so little did they know about the business
that, even with the perfect conditions prevailing,
passengers were more alarmed about the way the
Chinese were shaping when getting the boats out
than about anything else. Had there been panic,
it is pretty certain those boats would never
have been launched. And afterwards, on
shore, these good, reliable, docile workers simply
struck and refused to do a hand’s turn. Even the
captain admits that he was told —“No pay, no
work.” While the few white castaways were
struggling with the very tough job of repairing
and fitting out a boat to make the long journey to ,
Fanning Island, the Chinese did nothing. The
yellow carpenter did not even take his tools out
—they let the whites hammer and patch away as
best they could. Meantime they did a little in
the way of pillaging. One passenger, going in for
a swim at night, had his belt and his money
stolen; the belt was found later on ripped up.
So truculent were the Chinese crew that for a-
fortnight the whites had to take turns on watch—
for the Chinese had revolvers and knives, and had
swung them menacingly. Ah Sin has been
tried under a good many circumstances, and
proved most unwholesome and unsatisfactory.
Now he has been tried as the companion-
A Matter of Degree.
The Minister of Defence: To confound the Defence
Scheme with the European idea of conscription which
takes a man away from his social responsibilities and his
work, places him under military discipline, and drills him
into what might be called a fighting machine, is entirely
a mistake.
Mr. Bruce Smith (N'.S.'VV.): It is a matter of degree.
The Minister of Defence : Everything is a matter of
degree—and facts. —Parliamentary Report.
B. Smith would most undoubtedly be very, very
cross
If some rude person called him Jap, and yet, I’m
at a loss
To see how he could argue otherwise in that re-
spect :
A Jap is human—or a rumor’s rife to that effect.
And he talks and argues much the same as B.
So, if Smith is not a twin
To his cherished Yellow-skin,
Why it’s only just a matter of degree,
Just a trifling little matter of degree.
Now, a Jap is not a monkey, though he’s oft com-
pared with such,
And he doesn’t look unlike one, so it hardly
matters much.
A monkey has a fearsome phiz, and hands that
grab at things,
And he imitates his betters —all of which the
matter brings
To a very clear conclusion, seems to me,
Which you cannot fairly funk :
If a Jap is not a monk-
Ey, it’s patently a matter of degree.
And we needn’t mind a matter of degree.
Of native monkeys hereabout, of course, you
haven’t heard;
And a monkey’s not a native bear, which, clearly,
is absurd,
And yet, a bear clings to a tree with young bears
on its back
(My word ! we’re getting on a rather interesting
tack) —
Yes, it clings there like the cheerful chimpanzee.
So, if monkeys are not bears
Quite exactly—well, who cares?
When it’s nothing but a matter of degree.
You can hardly notice matters of degree !
Well, bears are very plainly never bunyips, at
first glance;
But wait a bit and give Smith’s reasoning a
sporting chance.
They are both Australian, purely ; there is no
denying that;
And they both live in the bush, so there you have
it, clear and pat.
And “bear” and “bunyip” both begin with “b.”
So, it’s clearly in the game
That if these are not the same—
Well,‘who’d quarrel with a matter of degree?
We ignore these little matters of degree.
I suppose ’twill be admitted that a bunyip is a
myth.
Without adopting arguments resorted to by
Smith ?
But we’ve never seen a bunyip, and the myths are
pretty rare;
So there seems to be a solid sort of fam’ly likeness
there.
And it’s pretty safe to reason, seems to me,
If a bunyip and a sprite
Aren’t about an equal height,
Well, at most, it’s but a matter of degree,
Just a simple little matter of degree.
So, overlooking certain petty matters of degree,
We are forced to these conclusions with undoubted
certaintee :
B. Smith’s a Jap, a Jap’s an ape (according unto
Smith),
An ape’s a bear, a bear’s a bunyip, and the last’s
a myth !
It’s as clear as any argument can be—
That if our dear friend Smith
Is not a simple myth
It’s an unimportant matter of degree,
Quite a microscopic matter of degree.
VIC.

The suggestion that King Edward should
choose a Capital site is, on the ground that one
wise counsellor is better than a multitude of
foolish ones, worthy of consideration. Better,
however, for the foolish ones to seek enough
Wisdom'from D.C.L. Whisky to enable them to
settle the question on its merits. On its merits
D.C.L. is everywhere recognised as the best in-
surer of Wisdom and the best counsellor of the
of white people cast away on a desert
island, and he has come out of that test
just as badly as out of any of the others. Fortu-
nately, the rescue was effected while there was
still plenty of food and no visible danger of
running short; otherwise, judging by Ah Sin’s
attitude when there wasn’t any danger, those who
remained of the Aeon’s castaways would have an
even more shocking opinion of Chinese as com-
panions on a desert island than they have now.

Year after year, at about this time, the Mel-
bourne daily papers scratch away at the Dust
Nuisance without indicating the true reason
why there is such a horrible abundance of dust to
kick up. The Age is the chief offender. That
misguided journal has some foolish theories con-
cerning dust, and some of its stupidest readers
encourage it to publish “admirable articles” on
the subject. All the Melbourne dust that the
Age dreams of is the product of metropolitan
roads; therefore nothing more is required to
abate the nuisance than a new method of road
making. If all streets and byways were coated
with kerosene mixture, or salad dressing, or
something, the dust would rise no more, and
then His Excellency the G.-G. might drive from
Government House to the Town Hall and
back without needing to be carefully washed
and brushed on his return. This wearisome Age
theory is persistently advanced in face of the
clouds of new dust that come sweeping into the
city from the neglected suburbs, and into the
neglected suburbs from the surrounding country,
every time a wind blows free. Melbourne was a
less grimy city than now when, in the old days,
its malodorous open drains traversed the main
thoroughfares. The broad flow of water took in a
lot of superfluous matter and removed it.
Obviously Melbourne is dustier nowadays because
insufficient labor is employed in dealing with the
deposits which formerly drained away “on their
own.” The present system of hosing the gutters
occasionally whilst the dust remains outside them
is the merest trifling with what is really a very
serious trouble.
