There is one Sydney court that the reporters on
the Evening Bloodstain never invade ; and, even
if the magistrate simply bubbles over with wit,
all the scintillations have to go unrecorded. This
is the “ Children’s Court,” founded a couple of
years ago, after South Australia had tried the ex-
periment and proved it good. It is a busy court,
too, with an average of well over 30 cases a week.
To this court the police are bound to take all
arrested offenders, under 16 years old; and
there, with no limelight, but with, at the
most, two or three elderly constables, and the
matrons of half-a-dozen shelters and reformatories,
the desperado of 13 finds it tremendously hard work
to maintain his pose as a fierce and hardened
sinner. Into this court the “neglecteds” and the
“ uncontrollables ” are swept up ; but, contrary
to a pretty general belief, they don’t form the
majority—don’t even form one-fifth of the total.
It’s a painful fact, but a fact nevertheless, that in
the past 12 months no less than 63 youngsters of
under 16 were up at this court charged with bur-
glary ; in fact, 40 of them were under 14 —includ-
ing three girls. The biggest section, however, are
just plain young • sneak-thieves, though 34 began
early in life in the “business” side of crime by
setting up as “ receivers of stolen property.” On
the whole, the experience one gets in the Children’s
Court is horribly depressing; yet the court
patently works on the right lines. First and
foremost, the youngster hasn’t got the least chance
of posing as a hero ; he gets no advt., no limelight,
no anything of the sort. He just falls into the
hands of people who make a special study of
reforming wild children, and who, moreover,
mostly have the merit of being able to hold their
tongues about it when a youngster has made a slip.
i + *
The visit of Uncle Sam’s battleships moved
London Morning Post to speak a vigorous word
for the coming Australian navy :
Australians are not likely to be deterred from embark-
ing- on this enterprise by the spectacle of what has been
achieved by the American people, who, at the time when
the colonies were first federated, numbered only about
the same as the Australian people to-day. The most for-
midable opposition hitherto confronting Mr. Deakin has
been, not hostility or apathy in Australia, butthe dogged
obstruction of the Admiralty in London, who cannot see
why, if French colonies subsidise the French navy with
cash, Australia and Canada should not subsidise the Eng-
lish navy. Observing that some of the smaller British
colonies accept the principle of cash subsidies, the Ad-
miralty apparently consider that the others may reason-
ably be expected to do the same. In other words, they
fail to understand that, given a certain set of circum-
stances, colonies grow into nations, and that the national
point of view is essentially different from the colonial.
For twelve months past Mr. Deakin has been trying to
obtain the co-operation of the Admiralty in working out
a scheme which, while laying the foundations of an Aus-
tralian navy under Commonwealth control, would ensure
a homogeneity of ships and training so as to promote
efficiency in a fighting alliance. Continued obstruction
on the part of the Admiralty can only mean, in the long
run, that the Australian navy will grow up on a smaller
scale and in a less efficient form than if hearty encourage-
ment had been offered from this end. No greater disserv-
ice could be rendered by any State department here,
either to the United Kingdom or to the Empire, than
to delay or prevent the beginnings of an Imperial naval
alliance, which alone can ultimately secure to the Em-
pire, including this country, a perpetual command of the
sea. All history shows that navies must be grown from seed,
and the seed is not prohibitively expensive. Mr. Deakin’s
proposals—which seem to involve within a year or two
an annual expenditure of about half-a-milhon sterling—
are proposals for preparing and planting the seed, with
the expert assistance of the Admiralty, in order that later
generation may enjoy a fruit not otherwise obtainable
at any price. But behind the reluctance of the Admiralty
there lurks undoubtedly the political apprehension that
a squadron owned, manned, and controlled by Australia
could not be relied upon implicitly to support the foreign
policy of the United Kingdom in all emergencies. This
political objection is, however, a matter for statesmen, not
for naval experts, the proper relation between the two
classes being that the statesmen provide the political
hypothesis upon which the naval experts have to base
their technical work. If English Cabinet Ministers dic-
tate, as they ought, the hypothesis of Imperial alliance—
not colonial dependence—the problem for the naval
experts is not how to secure one navy under one control
in time of peace, but how to promote the utmost efficiency
of allied navies.
♦
Urged by the Vic. Water Commissioners,
Minister Swinburne is alleged to be delving
deeply into his inner consciousness to find means
to compel landowners in irrigated areas to use the
fluid that the State has brought to their doors,
instead of sitting down and calmly waiting for
land values to rise. Yet time and time again
these simple ways of compelling the land-
owner to use his soil and the water have
been pointed out : (1) the levying of a general
rate on the area irrigated, whether the
water is used or not; (2) the imposition of a
land tax so that the owner must either use his
land or get rid of it; (3) the enforcement of
honest valuations and adequate local rates, so that
it won’t pay the owner to await the unearned
increment while he cadges subsidies from the
State to keep roads, etc., in repair. But Swin-
burne can’t adopt those plans without treading
on some loafing landlord’s toes; wherefore he
professes to have a brand-new problem set before
him, and he writhes in the agony of deep thought
and struggles hard to give the impression that he
is doing his best, and may hatch something tre-
mendous some day.

“C. N. Macintosh,” from Buenos Aires, S.
America:—
One hears a lot about the British mercantile marine.
Most Britons, when they hear or mention it, throw out
their chests and fancy they are speaking of ships manned
by men born in the British Isles. One needs to live out
here and watch, the steamers that sail under the British
flag to realise what the term really does mean. Out in
Australia, with your laws regulating colored crews, you
do not get a chance to see the full drift of things.
Under three years ago a large meat-carrying contract
was secured by the Anglo-Argentine Shipping Co. The
ships commenced to run with a great flourish of trum-
pets—all British,” vwis the word. On their first trip
they had everything-*fp to the knocker. Each carried a
British skipper, four British officers, a crew of 45
Britishers, and a bugler. Every man did the deck in
swagger uniform, while the “ style ” almost led one to
believe that this line was half-brother to his Majesty’s
navy. On their second trip the steamers still carried the
style, and the white British crew, but one officer less.
On their third visit the bugle boy had gone ; likewise the
swagger uniforms and half the “dog”; the crew of 45
had been reduced to 30, but still all were white Britishers.
On the fourth visit the captain was a “ white Britisher,”
and two of his officers likewise; but the crew was de-
cidedly mixed. Next visit, the British flag flew, as
before, but it waved over a British captain, two white
British officers, one foreigner, andsoyellow “Britishers”
from Hong Kong. What their next round of visits may
bring forth Heaven only knows ; but I am prepared for
anything.
That is a sample of the results of “a fair field and no
favor ” (or Freetrade as some call it), when up against
State subsidies and State bounties. Is it any wonder
that the Argentines (or people of any other nationality)
smile when they see the British flag waving over 15 or 20
Britishers and 50 Chinese, and then read the bombast
that one sees in the English papers !
“ England rules the waves.” She may think she does ;
but every day she is training more men for the Yellow
Men’s Navy, while the home-born Britisher finds the con-
ditions so uncongenial and pay so low that few men of
good type will ship. What, then, is the value of Britain’s
mercantile marine as a reserve in case of war?
Yes, it is time little Australia was learning the art of
self-defence.
+
The coasting steamer Hillmeads is a twin-screw
vessel, so, the other day, when one of the tail
shafts snapped, she was able to get comfortably
into port instead of bobbing helplessly about on
the high seas, as she would have had to do had she
been a single-screw boat. The remarkable
frequency with which the tail-shaft accident
happens is getting on the public’s nerves,
and people are beginning to think that
Commerce is rather too willing to put its trust in
one big steel rod, and allow the passenger to
keep dry as best he can. All the recent shaft
casualties in Australian waters have ended with-
out loss of life or serious damage to ship property,
so far as the public knows. Certainly, a few
boats have mysteriously disappeared off the face
of the waters, and it may have been a broken tail-
shaft in their case, and again it may not. Even
assuming, however, that the tail-shaft wasn’t the
criminal in these cases, it can only be regarded as
great good fortune that the accident in every
other case has occurred when the vessel has had
plenty of sea room. But some day the tail-shaft
disaster will occur when the circumstances are
less favorable, and then there'll be no ship to drag
home with a tow-line, but just a heap of crumpled
iron on a reef. When that happens, the question
of compelling all passenger vessels above a certain
tonnage to have twin screws may be taken up in
real earnest.
“ Y.” digs into the ribs of history
The threatened invasion of Scottish fishermen creates
no dismay among those who live by line or net fishing in
N.S.W. “Let ’em come,” is the common remark;
“unless they strike better conditions than ours they’ll
wish themselves back.” In a similar dismal condition of
the fishing industry in England the Government took the
matter up, and in time produced admirable results. It
associated the fishing industry with a scheme of naval
defence. The British methods of the time are, of course,
out of date now, but the connection of the humble fishing
boat and the warship was then, as it should be now,
perceived, and the fisherman was encouraged that the
navy might be benefited.
Here was how the British Government went to work.
The Privy Council in March, 1594, issued an ordinance,
“ very necessary to be placed in the houses of all men,
especially common victuallers,” instructing the English
people to eat fish. Lent was very largely observed,
and Fridays were generally fish days; but nevertheless
the consumption of fish was considered insufficient, and
Saturdays throughout the year were ordered to be fish
days. The ordinance disavowed any religious significance,
stating that its object was to develop fishing along the
coast, and thereby encourage seafaring “so that the navy
shall be replenished with men.” The Government penalty
for eating-flesh on Saturdays was ±3fine or three months’
imprisonment. Later the fine was reduced to ; but
as £1 then was equal to £5 now, the penalty was fairly
substantial. Any householder knowing and permitting
flesh to be eaten on Saturdays was fined 405., and victual-
lers and others who sold flesh on Saturdays were fined £5.
The Privy Council of that day condescended to argue,
and the first argument with which it backed up its
ordinance ran thus: “Our country is surrounded by
seas ; our best defence is the navy ; fishing is the nurse
of shipping, and if it flourishes men will be at all times
ready for service in the navy.” That argument
has a homely ring to Australian ears. The second
argument insisted on an array of villages along
the coast. “By stimulating the fishing industry,”
the Privy Council went on, “we replenish those
villages not alone with fishermen, but shipwrights,
smiths, ropemakers, sailmakers, weavers, and scores of
other craftsmen.’’ That, too, fits the Australian outlook
pretty closely. A third argument was addressed to the
small farmer class. It was pointed out that increased
consumption of fish would reduce the consumption of
flesh, and that large areas, instead of being devoted to
“ grazing beefs and muttons,” would be cut up into small
farm’s, and so the country would be studded with inland vil-
lages instead of grazing preserves. That is a fairly accurate
statement of the Australian closer settlement idea. There
followed a rage of pamphlets dealing with fishery-develop-
ment, the burden of most of them being the “ replenish-
ing of the navy”; and the outcome of the enthusiasm,
public and private, was the establishment of the fishing-
industry on a permanent and prosperous basis along the
coast of England.
The history of the navy shows that the connection
between fishing boats and men-of-war was fully as inti-
mate as the Government and private pamphleteers had
said would be the case, for, year by year, out of the fish-
ing villages and coast settlements recruits flocked to the
navy. That intimate connection is a natural one, and
in Australia should inspire all friends of naval defence to
be also friends of the local fishing industry.

One hundred and twenty or so dear creatures, from
all parts of the Commonwealth, made an early
start this week in St. James’ Hall, Sydney, to
talk about Socialism and other things they mostly
know nothing at all about. The first contribution
was a delightful essay on “Woman Under
Socialism,” in which the shocked ladies were in-
formed that under Socialism everybody would
have to put in his two or three hours a day :
Scholars, scientists, artists, and others would have to
devote a few' hours a day to assiduous physical toil, but
she would like to know how a few hours of assiduous
physical toil would affect such a person as a musician.
All of which, alas !is but too true. The railways
are a big bit of Socialism, and the post and tele-
graph office is another bit, and street cleaning is a
third fragment; and, as everybody knows, G.
Reid has to put in at least two hours out of every
24 crawling under engines and other things at
Redfern workshops, and B. Smith can be seen
any morning between nine and 11, racing along
with telegrams, and next week, if Hambourg
happened to be here, it would be his turn to
sweep up George-street between Martin-place and
King-street. With these horrors of Socialism
staring the ladies in the face, really is there need
to meet (with the help of vile.socialistic trains
and trams) to argue about their existence?

The Crossley Sydney season of six con-
certs ended with a matinee on Monday after-
noon and the singing by the star of “God Be
With You ” (till she and the audience meet again
understood). It is difficult to realise that 3200 per-
sons could make such a tumult and a shouting as
that last audience did in showingits appreciation of
the singers and players, and in demanding encores.
The season has been the unqualified success it
deserved, for the combination is the best of its
kind so far to visit Australia ; and, as for Crossley
herself, she has paid her native country the com-
pliment of coming back twice while in the prime
of her early summer-time, not waiting for the
evening calm and cool, to give us a skimmed-milk
voice. The company opens in Melbourne this
week, and later will warble its way through Ade-
laide, the West, and Maoriland, returning in
March to give Sydney more melody.
** * *
You can have your choice now. At Her
Majesty’s (Sydney) there is Carrie Moore to dis-
cuss between acts : further down the street the
other lady has just set out upon a stage career. It
is another case of yes-no ; and Sydney seems to be
the place where that sort of thing breaks out quite
naturally. Even over the Federal Capital the
House of Representatives has said Yass; and
there is no telling yet whether the Senate, which
always did like Monard, won’t say the other
thing. Still that’s wandering away from the
Merry Widow, and so far the public has shown no
sign of doing anything of the sort, for it’s still a
case of catching an early train and waiting on
the doorstep if you haven’t booked a perch before-
hand.
MISS KATHARINE GOODSON, a famous
pianiste, who appears at Sydney Town Hall
on 17th October.
“The Belle of Mayfair” continues to
walk, likewise to dance, in grace and beauty
nightly before the eyes of edified Criterion audi-
ences. Miss Ivy Salvin, late of Darlinghurst and
elsewhere, has now settled down definitely in the
more desirable quarter of Mayfair, and the change
has proved satisfactory all round. As Time is
incapable of corroding the Mayfair chorus, and as
the jokes that dwell in this part of the world are
for the most part proof against age-corrosion, the
odds are the Belle will stay at her present address
for some time to come.
** * *
After reviewing “Two Little Sailor Boys,”
at Sydney Palace, The Bulletin has doubts
about the wisdom of entrusting the defence of this
country any longer to the British navy. “Two
Little Sailor Boys ” might with more justice have
been called “The Admiral’s Imbecility,” for a
person of that rank dodders furiously through all
four acts, laying waste the emotions of the com-
pany at the bidding of a fiendish wife with a
metallic laugh and a weakness for stabbing people
in the back and throwing her offspring off bridges
into muslin and green limelight.
* * « *
The story of “ The Admiral’s Imbecility ”
doesn’t suffer from originality. It contains no
surprises, but it skips along gaily by way of
assault and battery, insult and murder, from
8 p.m. to 10.45, and is entertaining all the time.
Admiral Grey, R.N. (retired), has a grown-up
daughter and a daughter-son (later on, one of the
Two Little Sailor Boy-Girls). His household
consists of himself, his second wife (the lady with
the metallic laugh), and the daughter and son
aforesaid. The lady with the laugh owns a Past,
and, to prevent it colliding with the present and
telescoping the future, she incites her husband,
the lamentable Admiral, to bombard the
domestic arrangements. The lady’s previous
performances seem to be known to everyone but
the Admiral, and, at her bidding, he cheerfully
throws his family and friends out of doors and
casts suspicion over all. There is a good captain
in love with his daughter, and a villainous lieu-
tenant who hates the good captain and falls down
throttled immediately the good captain shakes
the front of his vest. A fearfully hoarse burglar
pervades the first three acts, and tyrannises over a
girlish waif until he is killed by being knifed in
the back by the Admiral’s amazing missus. One
Captain —of the Worcester regiment—is supposed
to have died gloriously in S’Africa after saving
the life of the Good Captain, but he turns up in
the third act in a muffled-up condition, like a new
sauce bottle hiding its label from the world. He
unwraps his identity when the proper time comes,
and pours himself over the situation like a bitter
but forgiving condiment. The action of the drama
is stopped every now and then to allow one of the
characters to bless one of the others; even
the Admiral’s wife, in the intervals between
requesting people to do their ineffective
worst and laughing the metallic laugh,
requests Providence to bless the child she
pushed into the muslin river in the first act.
Possibly the most impressive moment in the play
is in the last act, when she repairs to the house of
her dismissed maid-servant with the intention of
interviewing her step-daughter, who has taken
refuge there. The pert maid bars her way, and
says that that is a respectable house. A second
demand for admittance is met with a snappy re-
fusal and a reference to the safety of the spoons.
Only after a third attempt, and another crushing
retort, does the Admiral’s wife recoil; trembling
with rage, she demands in a deep contralto, “ Do
you mean to insult me 1 ”
■****■
Lilian Meyers glides stealthily through the
part of Mrs. Admiral in several very tine gowns ;
Mildred Grey, the Admiral’s daughter, is grace-
fully played by Lorna Forbes ; and Maud Chet-
wynd sparkles as the maid. Louise Carbasse and
Maisie Maxwell are the Sailor Boy-Girls ; Wilson
Forbes dodders as the Admiral; Conway Wing-
field is the good captain ; Arthur Cowley is the
villainous Lieutenant Redstone; and G. H.
Emery is well wrapped-up as the Worcester-sauce
captain. As an instance of how hard the play
hits the public over the heart, it may be mentioned
that, the night this paper attended, an earnest
voice from the gods loudly advised the waif to
resist the impossibly hoarse burglar to the last.
Such incidents are calculated to make the carping
critic wilt in his chair.
** * *
Sydney Tivoli programme still leans
heavily on the 12 Arabs of Abdullah, who toil at
spinning themselves. Osborn and Brooks, sketch
artists, have stepped into the place vacated by
the departed Meymotts, but otherwise the bill
bears a brotherly resemblance to that of last week.
Burt Shepard and his piano, Tod Lowen, Fanny
Powers, Irving Sayles, and the Barnards’ dolls
supply the balance of a moderately good show.
It’s about time the Tivoli meteorologist sighted a
big change approaching.

West’s Pictures are back at Sydney Glaci-
arium ; and, instead of a score of electrics gleam-
ing nightly upon a white frozen floor and a num-
ber of fair women and brave men—or, rather, ven-
turesome women and brave men —scurrying hither
and thither on murderous-looking boots, the big
hall is lighted as with a few glow-worms only,
while a packed and seated audience gazes upon a
motor race from New York to Paris. The pro-
grammed note that the race is an imaginary
one is more necessary than Edwin Geach
might think. The young farmer who has
grown up milking cows, separating milk, and
feeding poddies does not stop long enough (ac-
cording to good accounts) at his public school to
learn enough geography to know there is any con-
siderable fluid between Paris and New York.
The pantomime film, Under a Donkey’s Skin, is a
new laugh manufacturer, and for music in the in-
tervals there is de Groen’s band.
* # *
At Sydney Lyceum, Harry Lauder on the
chromomegaphone is still easily first favorite,
although his rivals, P. Dawson and S. Kirby, are
steadily winning favor. The star picture of the
piece is the splendid Roman Idyll, which rages
through an emotional story in many rare colors.
Strange how the people enjoy looking at picture
representations of what they have seen in real
life : the Sydney Eight-Hours procession evoked
quite a furore of applause when it set back the
clock and brought last week into the beginning of
this ! Which probably explains why the amateur
photographer is always kept busy photoing the
babies of his friends—the babies they see every
day.

At a Smoking Competition : I took two
friends and we climbed up three flights of stairs,
passed a genial doorkeeper, and arrived at a gal-
lery filled with chairs, and men, and blue tobacco
smoke. We leant over the railing and looked
down at.the competitors—36o of them, seated in
groups of six round sixty small tables, and all smok-
ing steadily. Some of them, with the conscious-
ness of a stiff job on hand, had their coats off;
others had newspapers, into which they peered
through the fast thickening fog. But the bulk of
them sat listening to the official on the stage,
which place, seen from the spectators’ gallery,
looked like the opposite edge of a drowsy volcano.
The official, in a voice muffled by the smoke, an-
nounced “The programme of items will now be
proceeded with. The first is” , and a man in
evening-dress fought a way to the mist-enshrouded
piano and sat down. His selection was a “rag-
time,” and the notes, as they fled out of the in-
strument, lost themselves in the haze. Presently
the pianist finished, and another entertainer
advanced to the crater’s edge and started to tell
humorous stories. By this time, 360 pipes going
steadily had dyed the atmosphere a beautiful blue,
and you felt you would like to carve out a block of
it, take it outside, and watch it melt. Close to
the walls, where it wasn’t quite solid, sinuous
wisps rose lazily until gripped by the draught
from one of the windows, when they streamed up
and out into the night to have a look at the stars
and tantalise the angels on duty. The evening
didn’t wear away—it just thickened. Somewhere
about 10.15, when the atmosphere was lying in
flat, blue flakes, and most of the pipes were cold
and empty, the newly-discovered long-distance
champion mounted the stage and took his last few
lingering whiffs. He managed to keep going long
enough to smash the German record, and then,
having had a long smoke for nothing, won £lO,

“ smoking competition ” should appeal to the man
of profound thought and few words who sits

A slightly altered version of “Camille,’
with Miss Anglin as the heroine, gives that
accomplished artiste a chance to emotionalise
with unusual vehemence, but is not otherwise a
specially interesting revival. Nor is it likely to
jarove so attractive as “ The Taming of the
Shrew,” which was removed to make room for it.
When the performance at Her Majesty’s (Melb.)
had been prolonged to 11.30 p.m. on Saturday
by a succession of tedious Waits, the audience went
away remarking that they were about full up of
Camille and her troubles. Miss Anglin, to be
sure, plays the part naturally as far as her appear-
ance can be reconciled with the text, and if she
were to take a dose of poison in the last act
her decease would seem reasonable. But the
theory of a fatal consumption in her case
needs something more than an occasional short
cough to commend it to public acceptance. Rising
from her death-bed to enfold the belated Armand
in a mad embrace, Camille is palpably plump and
hearty, and when she at last lapses into silence,
one feels that the lady is not dead, but dozing.
This air of robustness negatives much of the effect
of Miss Anglin’s performance from first to
last; and, even if she looked the part, she
couldn’t make it quite congenial to her, for
the French demi-mondaine is idealised by
the author, and even Sarah Bernhardt, who
appears to play Camille “ naturally,” de-
corates the character with histrionic graces that
are foreign to the uncompromising realism of Miss
Anglin’s style. She makes her strongest appeal
to sympathy in her scene with Duval, senr,, pre-
sented by Titheraclge as a hard-shelled English
parent of Wesleyan tendencies—kindly but frosty.
Yet. here, as elsewhere, her references to her
health are unconvincing.
• • « *
Prom the Armand of her devotion Miss
Anglin gets insufficient inducement to mawkish
sentimentality. Henry Kolker is everything that
Kyrle Bellew wasn’t, and, in the hysterical love-
sick capacity of Armand Duval, nobody can have
been more acceptable than Bellew was. Not
that Harcourt Beatty is an Armand to be
sneezed at. Kolker’s effusive insincerity in-
vites many sneezes. He is broadly Ameri-
can, oppressively gloomy, and quite middle-aged
m the part. Also he wants power (or he did last
Saturday) in the scene where he flings a few brass
farthings at his dissimulating idolatress and calls
the company to witness that he is repaying her
the wealth she lavished on him- Tempestuous
hysteria is not Mr, Kolker’s forte. Syd Stirling
(Count de Varville), Miss Susie Vaughan, Cyril
Mackay, Miss Evans and others will assist to
carry the tired drama to the end of its
brief revival, and on Saturday a week of “ Zira ”
will be inaugurated; after which “Twelfth
Night,” according to present arrangements, will
close the season in an odor of Shakspearian
sanctity. Melbourne has acquired a strong liking
for “Twelfth Night ” within the past few years,
and if it gets the opportunity of seeing Titheradge
as Malvolio the city may be trusted to prove suffi-
ciently grateful. Meanwhile, there is “Zira,” in
which Miss Anglin has one of her best parts.
** * *
At the Princess’s the demand for ‘ ‘ Morals
of Marcus ” showed an upward tendency during
last week, and if a fickle public persists in talking
about the merits of the comedy it will presently
attain the dimensions of a pronounced success or
a veritable triumph. Thus far the management
has been content to call it a Fascinating Play.
Few managerial declarations are so simply truth-
ful. “The Morals of Marcus” has a very strong
fascination for the numerous individual who is
ignorant of the story in book form. The incon-

siderable number of people who come along loaded

than those who come and get acquainted with the

dreadfulness, and it may be that he was merely
asking her the , latest Turkish conundrum, or in-
viting her to enter for an Oriental limerick com-
petition, as, for instance—“ There was a young
nymph of the Bosphorus, who painted her features
with phosphorus; when an amorous ‘ spark ’ took
her out in the dark” . After three visits to
“The Morals of Marcus” the present commenta-
tor has not arrived at a notion of Oarlotta’s Past,
but he will be always open to give her the benefit
of the doubt until she is proved otherwise.
* * * m
The acting has quickened since the first
night. The general effect is brighter. More
warmth might be infused into the dialogue be-
tween Marcus and Mrs. Mainwaring at the end
of the third act, and it is certain that their unemo-
tional conversation in the first act could be pruned
of some half-dozen lines—notably the reference
to Marcus Aurelius—that are neither smart nor
necessary. Also Antoinette, the servant, should
lay less emphasis on her intimation that she is
about to dress Carlotta up in a flaming red cos-
tume, so as to prepare the audience for the damsel’s
farcical-comedy entrance a few minutes later. For
the rest of it, the show improves on acquaintance.
Carlotta, by speaking less slowly, has lost none of
her cnarm, and the opening picture is an attrac-
tion in itself.

“ Maritana,” because ifc is “Maritana,”
draws people to Melbourne Royal who stayed
severely away what time “ The Lily of Killarney ”
was on the bill. The company, being more
Hibernian than Spanish, fitted the previous
opera better than the dear old thing it
is just now engaged with. In the present
instance two or three of the co. seem ill at ease in
their magnificence, and when nobody is singing
the performance grows unconsciously humorous.
Still, there are “Happy Moments” in “ Maritana,”
likewise “A Flower that Bloometh” and an air of
Myst-ereo, not to mention other time-honored
melodies that have survived in spite of thre«
generations of organ grinders. Don Caesar changes
his identity every evening— i.e., there are two of
him, and both of his voices will remain a credit to
his ancient race until the end of the season, pro-
bably. The sands of the season are passing in
swift but tranquil tide away through Time’s
unerring glass. On Derby Night the long-lost
dramatic company returns, and Miss Mclntosh
will appear in “She Stoops to Conquer.”

On Saturday afternoon a thin audience
welcomed Katharine Goodson at Melb. Town Hall.
The crowds were mostly listening to the music of
the bookmakers at Caulfield, or lining the banks
of the Yarra, where a number of rival college boys
were pushing spider-like boats over the water.
But if it was a thin audience it was
critical to a degree that is impossible in a
very fat assembly. Edward Scharf and his wife—
both accomplished pianists—big Maggie Stirling,
little Rita Hope, and Una Bourne, came to criti-
cise and remained to applaud. The new Russian
Consul, with the name that sounds like a hail-
storm, was m evidence with his wife; and Mrs.
Chief' Justice Madden, and her mother, and
other indefatigables were there. Marshall
Hall loomed up out of a funereal dais
draped in cold white cloth and lilies (the
dais, not Mr. M. Hall). The star sent a flutter
through the hall, when she at.last appeared
in a shimmering pale blue gown on which the
“ betrothal opal ” gleamed. As an artist, she
captured the admiration of Melbourne’s most
exacting music lovers, her most successful item
being Greig’s concerto in A minor, heard for the
first time here with the orchestral background. A
fortnight previously, Mark Hambourg, with a
more expensive edition of the orchestra, played
Tschaikowsky s concerto, and Miss Goodson dar-
ingly challenged comparison. While her render-
ing was strong, graceful, and expressive, it failed
to reproduce the barbaric note that dominates the
Russian’s music. But as a pianiste—remember-
ing the “e”—the gifted young performer is
delightful, and surprisingly magnetic.

marked off. Four subsequent concerts, on the 17th,

** # *
The lady who lapses oft into melancholy and
calls herself “ The Gayest of the Gay ” will utter
her despondent laugh no more after Friday at the
King’s. As Andersonian bellowdramasgo, “The
G. of the G.” was somewhat of a snorter, with a
large, complicated plot and many penitent girls in
it. For the sake of its lovely moral it might have
been given another week. No previous drama
within The Bulletin’s recollection has presented
so many cases of female reformation ; for when
Miss Duggan returns to the path of virtue
and undertakes nursing at the hospital, she
seemingly rings up her old giddy companions, and
persuades them to follow her lead. Anyhow, the
eight minor nurses who adopt the white apron of
a blameless life bear a suspicious resemblance to
the Lotties and Totties of the second act; and
the sight of them, ranged in meekness at the hos-
pital, suggests great possibilities in the restora-
tion of fallen sisters to a state of uprightness.
On Saturday, the old Drury Lane drama,
“ Human Nature,” is to be revived at the King’s.
Long years ago, when the Triumvirate ran the
Royal, this was a mighty drama, and the name
should draw the attention of many persons who
have grown older in the meantime.

The Great Marconis, the electrical per-
sonages who were affixed to the catalogue at
Melb. Opera House on Saturday, somehow fail to
dumbfound. They have a shopful of apparatus,
which spits and sputters, but does not horrify.
They emit anything from conflagrations to gas
flames from the place wnence coarse men eject bad
anecdotes. They pick sparks and other lllumin-
ants from their bodies, as if they were orchards of
electrical phenomena. Yet, with all their mani-
festations, they do not seem inhuman. Gregson
and Goodfellow gave a turn that was rather foolish
and purposeless, but a spice of novelty saved it.
Another first appearance was that of the Reno
Sisters, two damsels who have partially circum-
navigated the globe to exhibit legeentricities in
four changes of costume. Also Mignonette Kokin
appeared in new turns with great success.

subject when buying their tickets.
AT POVERTY POINT.
The mighty Butt has started to provide the British
press with quaint stories of what befell her in the Austra-
lian wilds. She passed this anecdote concerning the
woolly ways of Maoriland to the trusting editor of the
Musical Home Journal :—“ VVe found awaiting us at the
station the local carriage and pair, with the local under-
taker as footman. He was garbed in the usual funereal suit
of black, but he had substituted a white tie, in order to
relieve the situation somewhat, and, evidently with a
desire to dispel any morbid impressions, he commenced
to whistle a selection of the most cheerful tunes he knew
while he held the door open for us and helped us into the
carriage.” In Australia, she told the London Sunday Sun,
a Society lady” wrote to her pointing out that “the
concert dresses which she had been wearing would prob-
ably be useless to her,” and suggested, therefore, that
one should be sent to her (the Society dame), “asshe was
going to a party the following week and had nothing suit-
-6 j 'year.” The whistling undertaker-footman and
the old clo -seeking and outsize Society female are alike
weirdly improbable, but the British public has learned to
expect these curious folk from returned W’anderers, so
Clara heroically meets the demand.
Annette Kellermann lately swam 11J
miles in Boston Harbor—from Charles-
town Bridge to within half-a-mile of
Boston Light—in 6hr. 24min. The
whole distance from Bridge to Light
is about 12 miles ; the latter portion
of the journey is through a rough
open sea, and thus far only one man
has tackled it successfully. Last year
30 crack swimmers failed to do it in a
competition for a prize Cup. All things
considered, Annette’s performance was
a big ,one, and but for night coming on,
she might have struggled along to
the winning point. Anyhow, the
*' splendid failure ” served to boom her
Mermaiden show at Boston Wonder-
land. As a talker to the press Annette
is improving all the time. She tells
the reporters that dancing and swim-
ming are kindred arts. “All last
winter I was learning different kinds
of dancing in Paris. My swimming
makes it come most easily to me, and
so good dancers will find that their
ability will soon make the finest of swim-
mers of them.” Annette is the muscular
daughter of a Frenchwoman—a musical artist—and her
education as a dancer began when she was a child. Her
doctrine is, however, subject to a heavy discount when
applied to ordinary swimmers and comparatively fragile
danseuses. 0
The Meynell and Gunn Dramatic Co., with Miss Mclntosh
and Harcourt Beatty in the lead, returns to Melbourne
Royal on October 31, and plays Oliver Goldsmith’s comedy
“ She Stoops to Conquer.” Another chance for students
of the antique in dramatic work. To the present Mel-
bourne generation “She Stoops to Conquer” is prac-
tically unknown. The Brough and Boucicault co. tried
it with moderate success many years ago, Mrs. Brough
doing the “stoop” as Kate Hardcastle, and Brough
appearing as Tony Lumpkin. Also Kyrle Bellew and
Mrs. Potter are said to have staged the quaint old comedy,
but, if they did, the performance made no abiding im-
pression on the sands of time. The forthcoming revival
will come as a novelty.
“Sceneshifter”; “The Lily of Killarney,” the lyrical
version of Boucicault s “Colleen Bawn,” recently revived
at Melbourne Royal, introduces the cave scene with the
transparent stage-water effects. Until the piece was first
produced in London, in 1860, transparent stage-water had
never been seen, and a few yards of blue gauze was a
mightier draw than the finest acting in the world. It
filled the London Adelphi for hundreds of nights, it
poured coin into the treasuries of provincial managers,
sent people to the theatre who had never been before
and made the fortune of the author. Yet the author was
actually not the inventor of the wonderful thing ; the
idea first occurred to an old stage carpenter who’ was
working on the job.

preparing for a night at the Princess’s on Tuesday
Oct. 20.
“ Theorbo ” : With ‘ ‘ Maritana ” staged one may refresh
by noting Vincent Wallace’s extraordinary career. Born
1814, a native of Waterford, Ireland, his father was a
regimental bandmaster, afterward bassoonist in the
orchestra of Theatre Royal, Dublin, where his son Wel-
lington played the flute and Vincent the violin. Vincent
was a prodigy with piano, violin, clarionet and guitar.
He was organist of Thurles Cathedral at 16 years old •
then leader at the Dublin concerts. He married in 1834
and came to N. S. Wales in 1835, having separated from
hiswife. In N. S. Wales he engaged in sheep farming
Governor Bourke was enchanted with his piano-playin"
and gave him 100 sheep for concert tickets. Yet Wallace
went off to Tasmania, then to Maoriland, where a
chief’s daughter saved his life. He utilised this in
an opera called “The Desert Flower.” Next he went
whaling in the steamer Good Intent. The Maoris in the
crew mutinied, and killed all the white men except
Wallace and two others. Next Wallace was off to India
where he enchanted the Begum of Oude. In India he
found his way even as far as Nepal and Kashmir. Left
India at a day’s notice for Valparaiso ; crossed the Andes
to Buenos Aires ; recrossed to Santiago and Lima where
he realised £lOOO by a concert. Next he turned up in
Mexico, where he composed a fine Mass. He invested in
pianos and tobacco, and became bankrupt. After that he
went to London, arriving in 1845; “Maritana” was pro-
duced the same year, and made a great hit, on the
strength of which he went to Germany, Paris and the
United States of America. He returned to England in
1853. Seven years later he produced “Lurline” with
immense success. Died at a chateau in the Pyrenees
Left an indigent widow, known as Madame Stoepel a
pianist. r >
‘‘Melb ’’O er y discursively): A morning paper says
that Maritana was first seen here about 1864. It was
produced at Coppin’s Olympic (Melbourne) in 1856 bv a
small company from England, shortly after Brooke took
his farewell of that theatre as Petruchio, before migrating
to the Royal. The cast of “ Maritana,” in 1856, included
Julia Harland, wife of Hoskins, as Maritana, Farquharson
as Don Jose, and Sherwin as Don Caßsar. Conductor,
Linley Norman. Hoskins, of whom Titheradge is a
reminder, came to Australia with this company, and
used to act in farces after the operas. He gave up
a fine position at Sadler’s Wells, with Phelps. His
Lucio, in “Measure for Measure,” had made a hit,
and his portrait, in this character, will be found in one of
the finest illustrated editions of Shakspeare. Julia
Harland’s last appearance on the Melbourne stage appears
to have been in a small part in the burlesque of “ Black-
lived Susan,” when acted by Kate Denin at the Hay-
market about 1866. Farquharson was immediately
engaged by Lyster on, arriving with his company about
1000, and featured as Marcel in “The Huguenots.” He
was great also in “Elijah,” with the Philharmonic, but
he was an unreliable man. It is alleged that once, with
the Phil., he only stood up, and Sara Flower, the power-
ful contralto, sang his music. Sherwin travelled with the
Carandinis. Rather a tinpotty tenor. Dost an arm out
shooting. Squires was good as Don Caesar, but that char-
acter must always be associated in the Australian mind
with Armes Beaumont.
“Lyre ” : There is a common delusion that “ Maritana ”
was composed in Sydney. Wallace did not obtain the
libretto till he arrived in London, in 1845, after his travels,
let the music must have been simmering in his mind for
many years. “Maritana” is probably a hastily-con-
cocted cento of ballads he had composed before. When
he reached London the play of “ Don Cmsar de Bazan,”
by Dennery, was at the height of its popularity. It was
first acted in Paris by Lemaitre, then in London by Wallack.
Fitzball, the dramatic author, suggested that Wallace
might scrape together music for an opera on the theme.
It was put together with the rapidity of Handel’s
Messiah,” which only took six weeks. Yet the copious
ballads all bear the stamp of long meditation. A man
does not runoff such things. They do not verv strictly
belong to the opera, nor any opera. I think Wallace’s
n “ mr * lne ”> s essentially finer, and more of an opera, than
Maritana.” Australia loved it in Lyster’s time. Re-
member the “Take this Cup of Sparkling Wine,” and
Sweet Spirit, Hear My Prayer,” the gem of the
Aladdm burlesque when Julia Matthews was Aladdin.
Wallace thought “ The Amber Witch ” his best opera.
Frances Alda, nie Davis, is a singer not long ago well-
known to Sydney playgoers as Francie Adler. She comes
of a musical family, for her mother was Leonora, eldest
daughter of Martin and Fanny Simonsen. Recently she
sang with considerable success in opera in Paris, and now
she has appeared before what is generally conceded to be
the most critical audience in Europe, at La Scala, Milan.
Ihe opera was Charpentier’s “Luisa,” not exactly new,
for it was produced seven years ago at the Teafcro Lirico,
Milan, and neither on that occasion nor on this with very
much success. L’lllustrazionk Italiana, one of the best
illustrated papers published in Italy, thus speaks of
her: “Among all distinguished herself Mrs. Frances
Alda, new to our public, an Australian who has
all the grace of a true Parisienne. In the part of the
protagonist, she is perfect. She sings with security and
suavity, and is a skilful and intelligent actress. She was
accorded the greatest applause of the evening, particu-
larly after the romance in the third act.” There is no mis-
take about Australia being a voice factory.

The chaplain held services on Sunday, but all were toft
tired to attend.
“Aha!” thought the parson: “Now I have got
'em. They are surrounded by the sea. Nothing
can save them.” But he was mistaken, for

“ F.M.”: The production of “ The Taming of the
Shrew,” at Melbourne Her Majesty’s, is interesting as
Shakspeare invariably must be. It is funny and it is
human. Yet there is one terrible fault. The Shrew’s
face and shoulders are altogether beautiful, and she has
a presence and gesture that call for sympathy instead of
scorn. The Tamer puts too much whip into the thing,
and when we think of that w hip and those \vhite shoul-
ders in the same think, any man with any sense of com-
passion in him must shudder. It is too brutal altogether,
and it should not appear brutal. This is Miss Anglin’s
faulr. She is so awfully nice when she should be sour and
haggard. In the country-road scene, when Petruchio
cracks his whip over the woman who has collapsed from
hunger, sleeplessness and exhaustion, the cruelty of it is
too much. And it is all because Katharine always retains
the sympathy of the audience. By the bye, why is there
so much surprise expressed in the papers because “ The
Shrew” is produced wdthout “The Induction”? Is
Shakspeare ever produced nowadays without first being
put through the sausage machine? So far as the written
play of “ The Shrew ” goes, the funniest line in it is in the
stage directions. Hortensio goes out to teach Katharine
how to play the lute, and then the directions read:
“Enter Hortensio with his head broke”! That is a
delightful way of putting it.
A SIMPLE PRESCRIPTION.
SAID TO CURE RHRUMATISH, KIDNEY TROUBLE
AND BLADDER TROUBLES.
We know that the readers of this paper appre-
ciate good advice, and we are glad, therefore, to
be able to publish the following from a prominent
physician.
It is astonishing to know the prevalence of
kidney trouble and other diseases, such as rheu-
matism, bladder and liver troubles, which are
almost invariably caused by weak kidneys. It is
said on good authority that fully one-third of the
people in Australia who have reached the age of
maturity are more or less afflicted with kidney
trouble in some form. The most dangerous
feature of this disease is the fact that many
people have it without their knowledge, and con-
sequently neglect it until it has reached a danger-
ous stage.
The least symptom of- kidney trouble, such as
backache, must be given prompt attention, and a
good reliable treatment should be taken, until
every trace of the disease has been eliminated.
We are glad to be able to give our readers a pre-
scription which we have seldom known to fail in
any case. It contains no harmful ingredients, is
pleasant to take, not expensive, and can be
supplied by any good chemist. It is as follows :
Tincture Gentian Compound, one ounce;
Syrup Rhubarb, one ounce ; Liquid Barkola Com-
pound, one ounce ; Syrup of Ginger, five ounces.
Take a teaspoonful after each meal, and one at
bedtime.
We are glad to publish this prescription, as we
know it will help our afflicted readers who give it
a fair trial. If taken continuously for a few
weeks it will be found to be an almost absolutely
certain cure for rheumatism, kidney and bladder
trouble, backache, and all allied complaints.
Note.—Other papers are privileged to copy the
above.

SHOPWALKER: “Is there anyone waiting on you
Miss?”
MISS (blushing): “Fes, that’s my young man.

The ancients at the Liverpool (N.S.W.)
tubercle farm, officially known as the Liverpool
Asylum for the Infirm, took an extra interest in
the papers the other morning ; for the announce-
ment was then made that the old quarrel between
Superintendent Dr. Beattie and G. E. Brodie had
ended in the latter’s retirement from the job of
Inspector-General of Charities. Brodie was pre-
viously an inspector of accounts, and had fair
chances once of being made Auditor-General; but
instead some amazing blunderer turned him on to
the work of bossing the State charitable institu-
tions, and he promptly made himself famous by
reducing the poor little gratuities paid to the
inmate attendants from 3fd. to 2|d. or so a
day—and some of the attendants have to look
after cancer patients and do other most
disagreeable work of that kind. By this master-
stroke of finance the total amount of gratuities
paid in all Government asylums for the old and
infirm was reduced from £3776 11s. Sd. in one year
to £2917 3s. 3d. in the next, and the other
£859 Bs. sd. helped to make the “Reform
Government’s surplus. Then Brodie began a
great battle with Dr. Beattie over some
mineral water which he alleged Beattie had im-
properly allowed to be consumed ; and when
Beattie showed that it was taken medicinally on
the prescription of two doctors, and amounted
to a whole ten-shillings’ worth anyway, the
Public Service Board reckoned the farce had
gone far enough. So the ancients who
have to attend to the cancer patients and the
other disagreeable jobs are to get back their 3fd.
per day, and Dr. Beattie can have another bottle of
mineral water if it is needed, and the square Mr.
Brodie is taken out of his round hole in the
Charities Dept, and dropped into the Taxation
Dept., where he ought to be a tremendous suc-
cess if he pursues the odd penny with the old
vigor. In his place Dr. Baton —the pawky
kindly Scotch gentleman (and a real gentleman
he is) who for years past has been looking at
prisoners in gaol and deciding whether they are
insane or not —becomes Inspector-General of the
tubercle farm and the other asylums.
* * * *
It was rumored a while ago that Geelong
(Vic.) was waking up; but a recent decision of
the local council shows that there was no truth
in the story. The sanitary inspector, observing
three scoops of rotten barracouta standing along-
side a fence, looked into the neighboring yard of
a milk vendor, and in that yard, right where cows
were milked and milk carts and cans were kept,
he found decomposing fish and other abomina-
tions lying in pits till the pigs should need a
little light refreshment. The inspector then got
the dairy-supervisor to investigate, and, both
being of opinion that the case called for heavy
steps, he asked the council for instructions to pro-
secute. Also, he assured the city grandfathers
that typhoid had already broken out in families to
whom this man had supplied milk. One venture-
some councillor proposed that the officer should
be authorised to go ahead, but the majority,
having yawned three times, decided to gently
request the gentleman to clean up his place—any
time within a week would do. So Geelong is still
Geelong, after all.
V * * *
N.S.W. Railway Commissioners decided,
the other day, to light the North Shore Suburban
line tunnel. Evidently, they consulted an oculist
on the subject, and the oculist was thoughtful
for the growth of his own business. A few small
electric lights have been attached to the sides of
the tunnel at long intervals, and the effect as the
train runs through is of a series of bewildering
flashes. A few months of travelling through that
tunnel twice a day will ruin many good eyes.
The N.S.W. law allows divorce in cases
where there has been desertion for three years and
upwards; and if either party receives from
an ungrateful country an award of three
years’ imprisonment, or has been frequently
sentenced, it counts as enough desertion to
justify the breaking-up of the happy home
in an official manner. Out of this, the other
day, arose a point in Sydney Divorce Court
which looks capable of providing Costs on
a very healthy scale. For in this case,
though the husband had received three sen-
tences of 12 months each, they all landed
upon him on the same day, and the sen-
tences were concurrent, with the result that
he only “did time’’for one year. The Divorce
Act didn’t apparently legislate for this
very obvious possibility, and the Divorce
Judge, in dismissing the suit, gave the peti-
tioner a hint to pursue the point and Costs
through the Courts of Appeal.
As a rule, the most formidable thing about
the old inhabitant is his memory. Awaken it
with a chance remark and it arises and wanders
relentlessly into the Past, to gather the weed of
Reminiscence and uproot the bones of Romance.
But a quite unusual ancient, because his memory
had completely run down, happened along to the
Carlton (Vic.) Court last week, in pursuit of an
old-age pension. First of all he stated that he
could not remember the Christian or maiden
name of his mother. He had a sort of idea he had
been married in Tasmania many years ago, but he
was drunk at the time, and could not swear to it,
as he had never bothered to ask for the certificate.
Somebody had once told him that he had a wife
and child, and that they had both died ; but he
could not give any particulars about the deceased,
owing to there being the usual moth-hole in his
memory at that point. Then, having amazed the
Court with the list of things that had leaked out
of his mind, he remarked proudly that the one
thing he was certain about was his age, which was
67. The Commissioner looked at him, and in-
stantly decided that he was wrong. Directions
were, however, issued that the Tasmanian authori-
ties be communicated with and asked if they could
supply any further information re the Great For-
getter, whose sole recollection, or thereabouts,
was the spree he had had when he married his for-
gotten bride.
HER DIGNITY.
ENID (doing a lesson about one of Britain’s famous Admirals): “Mamma, how do
you, spell Collingwood ?”
MELBOURNE PARENT : “ Tell Miss Masharn I don’t send you to her school to learn about a
place like Collingwood!”
Mr. Stiggins scored a great victory in S.
Aus. last year, and induced the Parliament to
pass an Act for the suppression of the House that
Flossie lives in. The measure has been at work
for about 12 months, and in his annual report the
Inspector-General of Police (who was the head of
the training college for teachers before he got the
other job) tells what has happened. Fines amount-
ing to £94 15s. have been inflicted, and the House
that Flossie lived in has been closed up. Never-
theless Flossie, he admits, hasn’t been extirpated
or even reformed ; but when she’s at home she
isn’t at home, in a Sassiety way of speaking. It is
difficult, says Colonel Madley, to say where Flossie’s
place of business is under the new conditions ; but
he mentions that “loose women have been seen in
dark lanes, rights-of-way, and the park lands,”
and—well, the Colonel has his suspicions. Also
you never quite know what you’re liable to
stumble against in the private parlors of Adelaide
pubs nowadays. In fact, one publican stumbled
against unexpected things so often that he had the
doors taken away from his parlors and public sit-
ting-rooms, and another, the Inspector-General
says, “ keeps a man constantly visiting his
parlors.” So if Flossie has lost her job (more or
less), a new industry has been created—the Man
above Suspicion who Circulates among Pub
Parlors.
*■*■,*■*
Vic. Salvarmy has got badly burnt in
an attempt to escape a microscopic fine. A
while back, a male and female belonging
to the Sale corps were exhorting people to
certain ablutions, when the Law requested
them to move on. They wouldn’t, so the
Law, in the person of Harris, P.M., smote them
to the extent of 10s. for street obstruction.
Thereupon they began to appeal. They asked
Justice Hodges for an order nisi to review the
magisterial decision, and his Honor threw them
out. They proceeded to the State Full Court on
a similar errand, and were_ again ejected. Un-
daunted, and now imagining themselves to be
burning comets with the of the .world upon
them, they requested the High Court to review
the first decision, and were once more dismissed,
with Costs against them. In fact, Costs had been
playing band selections all the time. The Harmy
in Vic. will have a heavy bill to pay, the result,
seemingly, of a determination to shed any quan-
tity of coin to maintain a principle. Still they
probably had to shed any quantity of principle to
obtain the coin, so the account is about square !
The Powerful court-martial went the
limit in sentencing the man-o’-warstnan who
threw ship’s goods overboard to two years. The
offender thought in his foolishness that J. Bull
would expel him from the service, and so enable
him to settle down with an uncle in Australia.
But Bull wasn’t doing things that way. The
convict will be fired off his ship, not in Australia,
in all likelihood, but at the other end of the world,
and neither Australia nor his uncle will want him
when that time comes. The captain of a, man-o -
war can give up to 90 days’ “hard ” in a civil gaol,
and that was the worst the unhappy ass on the
Powerful expected. He forgot that naval law,
like land law, has a good deal of elasticity. It was
felt that he should be transformed into a “hor-
rible example”; and so the senior officers of the
ships in Sydney harbor were constituted a court-
martial.

From one of the ancients :
A petition is being circulated in Parramatta (N.S.W.)
asking for the removal of the asylums for the infirm from
that town. The petition alleges that “ the citizens of
Parramatta” Drotest against the presence of these
asylums; but‘before the Government agrees, we old
people hope it will find out how the petition was engi-
neered. The document hypocritically says the removal
is in our interests, and on that false ground many signed
it. It would he cruel to exile us, as is proposed, to a place
where our friends could never visit us, where many of us
would have no personal communication with the outside
world, where there would be no shops from which to
procure little comforts not supplied in the rations—in
fact, to condemn helpless people to expatriation simply
because we are poor and are supposed to have no friends
in Parliament.
* * * *
Right in the heart of Adelaide the in-
tensely practical S. Australian Government has
built a school that will accommodate no more
than 24 children. Away out in the backblox S.
Australia has what it calls “provisional schools,
mostly hitherto taught by people who haven’t had
any training as teachers —a job, in fact, which has
often gone to well-educated failures in other walks
of crust-making. Lately it was concluded that
the educated failures might at least be given a
scrap of training before being sent out; so this
“ provisional” school has been set up in Adelaide,
and put in charge of a woman (many of the pro-
visional teachers are women) who is reckoned an
expert in keeping six classes going at one time ;
and the candidates who are waiting for the poor
and usually temporary job of provisional-school
teaching will attend at the tiny little model to see
how the miracle is done.
SPRING ON THE BLOCK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Let other singers rave of Spring
Where wattle floods come rolling down
The range’s slope, but I shall cling
To seasonable joys in town.
No lush greens grow in Oollins-street,
Nor do the parrots hither flock ;
No bush bloom odors, cloying sweet,
Surge up to meet you on the Block ;
But Spring arrives, a jaunty queen,
And sudden all the town is gay.
In flowing robe, with merry mien,
I met her only yesterday.
Blue heaven lay along the roofs,
The little girls were all a-wing
Like butterflies. Here, here were proofs;
My heart cried out: “It is the Spring 1 ”
She’s tall and lithe, her hair’s aflame
With wall- flower and with marigold ;
She puts sobrieties to shame,
And makes the meekest buds grow bold.
All loveliness of bird and bloom
Goes gaily with her. Wide and free
She scatters lustre and perfume,
And dainty, dear audacity.
Let others sing of fields where Spring
Has squandered jewels in the grass,
For I love best this subtle thing,
Fresh prinked and braided from the glass.
Her lip may be a touch too red,
Her cheek be somewhat over-bright;
Perchance, as certain prudes have said,
She wears her stays a trifle tight.
But she’s Queen Glorious. I sing
Her rapture in no mincing vein,
Content if this reward it bring—
A Block nymph of her merry train.
Blue eye or grey, twin lips that cling,
A mouth of laughter—these for me 1
Thou’rt prodigal; but, Lady Spring,
Thy servant asks no more of thee.
via.

“ MEN’S
OUTFITTERS,’
The Oldest Inhabitant goes on remember-
ing ancient pubs and ancient landlords
The present Liquor Act has done what its promoters
outside Parliament never intended it should ao. It has
caused the demolition of many of the old pubs, which are
being replaced by palatial buildings. Instance the West-
minster, in George-street West. The house which is to
cake its place is to be a replica in miniature of the British
Houses of Parliament. Another landmark to go stood on
the N.-W. corner of King and Oastlereagh streets, a great
theatrical house. It was built in the early Ws. One of
the first landlords was the late Edwin Gee, who kept
it as the Montpelier. Then came Nat Smith, who called
it the Royal Navy. It then became the Oontinental, kept
by Albert Kirschbaum, who was followed by Eastway,
James Gordon Jackson and J. J. Power. The last made
a.fortune in the house, and, having toured the world,
came back and entered Parliament as a Labor member for
West Sydney.

“Narrabeen” tells of a romance that
went wrong
Don’t talk to me about romance in real life. I had
been expecting someone to call to see me for ages. I did
out the drawing-room every day, called on all the neigh-
bors that had flower gardens, dolled up poor old mater in
a tight black velvet bodice that she loathes. And he
never came. Then we had the spring cleaning. I
polished and shook and dusted and swept until the
beeswax and turpentine got into my soul. I tote down
everything that was up, and took up everything that was
down, and at last worked myself into a first-class fit of
hysterics. Then I retired from the field of battle, and—
oh, yes, he came. I ought to have been wandering in the
garden (I had arranged it a hundred times), with some-
thing fleecy thrown carelessly over my head. Then there
ought to have been a quick step, and the scent of a good
cigar—no, fragrant cigar—that would be hurled away
directly he saw me. And the fact ? Well, I had on my
worst blouse, one that never suited me, and I shuffled to
the door in old tennis slippers. And there was Fred—
with a most awful cold in his head, and he put the butt
of a cigar carefully in my one pot of musk, and growled,
and slapped his pocket, and said, “ I’ve lost my handker-
chief somewhere.”

Melbourne, or part of it, witnessed a
shameful piece of humiliation and meanness the
other day, when a man who had lately been sen-
tenced to four years’ gaol for a crime in Ballarat
was taken from Spencer-street railway station to
Pentridge on the twopenny tram, handcuffed to
an armed constable. The man was sentenced to
four years’gaol—he wasn’t sentenced to be made
an exhibition of through the streets of the city.
And the Government that does that sort of thing
talks about its great interest in the “reforma-
tion ” of prisoners 1
SEEN IN PASSING.
JIM: “ Who was that?"
FLO: "My husband. I thought you knew him.”
Things are not what they used to be in
“the West.” Five years ago and thereabouts, if
a young woman turned up at Perth, 17 men and
a fraction would most likely line up within a fort-
night, each with his heart in one hand, a nugget
in the other, and an offer of marriage in his mouth.
In 1903, even in Perth, there were only 82 females
to every 100 males, and on the goldfields the
females were almost too few in the bundle to
count. But now, in Perth, there are 101 of the
sex that wears poultry on its head to every 100 of
the others, and the district of North Perth, which
in 1903 only had 92 Bellas and Janes to every 100
Bills and Johns, now, alas ! has no less than 160
of them; wherefore the pursuit of a husband is
becoming as desperate a proceeding as it is in
East Melbourne or any other part of the Vic-
torian State of big landlords and elderly maids.

“Viator”: Putnam Weale’s last book,
“The Coming Struggle in the Far East,” is
written after Manchurian travel, which was in
the early part of 1907. So he is the latest,
formerly he was pro-Japanese. Now he is anti-
Jap. Says Japan is preparing trouble even for
England. Harbin has 80,000 Russian inhabitants.
Yet in 1897 it had only two white men. What
builders these Russians are !
It is more astounding than
Johannesburg and very much
more significant, for Harbin,
like Chicago, is planted on
substantial resources. Not
only is it in railway and tele-
graphic communication with
Russia, but it is built along-
side the grand river Sungari,
1000 miles long, a waterway
for traffic somewhat compar-
able to the Yangtse. The
region is richly agricultural
for a vast space round Harbin.
Before the war, great flour
mills were established there.
The Russians never had to
fetch flour, meat, vegetables,
tea or fodder along the
Siberian railroad. Yet the
genius of. Director-in-Chief
Khilkoff was taxed to the ut-
most. He had to deal with a
single line, with copious
sidings. It carried as many
as 10 trains per day each way,
total 32. Over a million Rus-
sian soldiers were taken. The
Russians were preparing to
send 700,000 more, or 200,000
more than the army that
Napoleon took to Moscow.
The number of horses sent
from Russia amounted to
nearly 250,000. Weale makes
out that the Japs were sure to
have been beaten. Linievitch
had constructed something
like Wellington’s lines of
Torres Vedras. Harbin was
the depbt. The photos show
quite magnificent edifices. The
business future of Russian
Manchuria is incalculable, and
millions, too, of free citizens are going to Siberia.
Weale went south from Harbin, transferring from
the sft. gauge Russian railroad to the shabby
little 3ft. 6in. Japanese.

Providence isn’t always on the side of the
Big Drum. At Bourke (N.S.W.) a generous towns-
man presented the local Sal. Army with a block of
land in the centre of the town whereon to erect
barracks. He committed suicide afterwards—and
the Army prayed. Then a Darling River hurri-
cane came along between a prayer and a collection
and blew the edifice over. It was rebuilt; but
had scarcely recovered its perpendicular before
another windstorm tossed it on its side again.
The Army then gave it up for a bad job,' said
another prayer and removed the ruins, leaving
only the floor. The local youths have converted
this into a skating rink, and thereon in the moon-
lit nights they smoke cigarettes and make love to
the river maidens. And the disciple of General
Booth now holdeth the collection and basheth the
Large Drum in the office of the old Central Aus-
tralian —once owned and edited by W. N
Willis. W.N. isn’t dead.
Even after that veteran battler in strange
places, Capt. John Strahan, had given up the
wreck of the Chinese liner Australian as a bad
job, a Port Darwin firm decided lately to have a
dig at it. The ancient skipper, who has been re-
ported drowned so often that Thk Bulletin
wouldn’t believe it now even if Neptune and three
mermaids swore affidavits to that effect, made
over his rights to some Northern Territory store-
keeping people; and they went round lately in
the Wai Hoi to the reef near Vashon Head, where
the big vessel lies, looking from a distance as
though she were riding at anchor. But she
is fast aground from bow to Stern, and
after inspecting her fore and aft and side-
ways, Port Darwin did the same as John
htrahan, and likewise gave her up as as a bad job.
So by this time probably everything that is likely
to bring in a profit of tuppence is being torn from
f l "’ , lnc^u ding the polished satin wood decorations
of the saloon, and later on, if they can get the
water out, a raid will be made on the engine-room.
As for the cargo, the water in the holds is so foul
that that section will have to be given a wide
berth.

This evening (Thursday) the Vicar-
General, Archdeacon Gunther, of Parramatta,
will preside at a meeting in the Chapter
House, Sydney, when the question of a memorial
on the site of the first church in Australia
will be considered. The first church built
by first-parson Johnson was of the “wattle-and-
dab ’ order, and stood, approximately, on the
small triangular space at the junction of Hunter,
Bbgh, and Castlereagh streets. It was built in
1/93 at a cost of £67 12s. lljd., though another
account places the sum at 3d. more. The church
was burned down on October 1, 1798, by “ some
wicked and disaffected persons ” who resented
Governor Hunter’s general order to attend church
on Sundays.

Now that Melbourne Hospital is alleged
to be of loud repute regarding the wages it pays,
sundry bodies are getting inquisitive. As an
example, Coburg (Vic.) Council bluntly requested
the hospital to supply a list of its employees,
with details of their hours and state of affluence.'
lhe hospital committee looked at the application
with an air of mild surprise for a considerable
time, then someone named Jeffries got up and
erupted :
I think this request is deserving of something more
than an ordinary reception—the reception it should get
is a hot one. We have been publicly charged with being
sweaters” by a municipality which has, during the
last twelve months, sent us 195 patients, and contributed
to the hospital funds the munificent sum of £3, and its
sister municipality, Brunswick, sent us 1120 patients, and
contributed nothing at all. . . . Personally, I am g-lad
that these questions are asked, for, though we need not
necessarily give the information required, we have the
opportunity of drawing the attention of those who ask
them to the fact that, if there is any sweating, they are
the sweaters, for in the meanness of their support they
are actually sweating the hospital, and it’s a pity they
can tbe prosecuted for it. F * 3
Things in Coburg are now dead quiet.
A rifle club afc Ballarat; (Vic.) is leaching its
members to shoot in a historical building—the old
house at Bakery Hill, where Seekamp first printed
the Ballarat Times and wrote the fiery articles
that stiffened the diggers to resistance, and where
Beter Lalor swore his men to “be true to the
southern Cross.” The riflemen—most of them
Canadians, by the way—have at least a place
with inspiring records to spur them on to hit the
bull s eye.

“Fourth Lubber” puts on his sea-boots,
and speaks :
I always had an idea a chantey was a joyous sort ot
ditty. This impression had been gleaned from sea novels
and from the fierce, spray-dashed verse written by dry
poets who stay ashore and sing about the joys of being
afloat with brine in your whiskers and cockroaches in the
forecastle. On a recent Sunday morning a friend and I
rowed down Sydney Harbor as far as Bradley’s Head, to
have a look at the morning before the excursionist arose
and threw orange-peel on it. A tug kicked its way
up the harbor, like an ant towing a great four-
masted cockroach. The cockroach went up harbor and
let g-o both anchors with a roar and rumble of heavy
chain. Almost immediately the capstan was made into
a sort of rimless wheel by having six or seven capstan
bars stuck spoke-like in it, and the crew of twenty-odd
men, two or three to a bar, started to walk round and
round on the fo’castle, their object apparently being to
wind up some of the slack cable. They looked like low'-
sentenoe prisoners taking exercise in a circle in the gaol
yard. Ilound and round they went—not skipping merrily
as I had expected, as does the young goat on the moun-
tain, hut trudging silently with their chests pressing on
the massive bars. For about five minutes they circled
as ghosts might trek around the stove of Hell.
Suddenly a voice started a hoarse sort of lament, and
the circular procession responded with a united groan of
'‘Off V IUo-oh-oht ” They sang 59 verses, yet we could
distinguish nothing save the prolonged moan of the
chorus. It was the saddest wet-Sunday lamentation I
ever encountered. Are all chanteys like that—just the
broken-hearted cry of the wet forecastle and the ship-
biscuit, and the soul-harrowment of the corned beef ?
* * * *
Ifc may be true that in the springtime
fancy lightly turns to thoughts of love ; but the
Victorian Registrar has found out by long experi-
ence that, in his State at any rate, the springtime
isn’t when the population turns towards the par-
son with the marrying fee in its hand. There
were 194,871 weddings in Victoria in the 25 years
1881-1906, and 2686 per 10,000 of them were in
the autumn quarter. Winter is the coldest time
of the year for the marrying parson ; only 2337
per 10,000 of the year’s weddings happen then ;
then summer, 2403 ; and spring, 2574. Latterly
the popularity of the autumn wedding has been
even above the average; last year, for instance,
285 of every 1000 weddings happened then, and
only 249 in the spring. There’s no telling for
certain why these things are so. No doubt in a
lot of cases Bill (and Susan) wait till after
harvestin’; in a lot more cases they hurry things
on because Easter is a good time for honey-moon-
ing. But, anyhow, the figures can’t lie, and poets
are enjoined to amend their spring odes accord-
ingly.
CHRISTCHURCH IN THE EVENING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A sound of water by the dusty street,
An open window and a floating song,
And on the evening air a breath of flowers.
Where the dark willows bend above the stream
The lengthened lamp-lights tremble, and the noise
Of echoing footsteps far and farther fails
In whispering silences beyond the bend
Of the wide-sweeping river. Long, straight streets
Stretch on faint-lighted in the growing gloom
Over immeasurable distances
Shut in by trees and houses. Far away
A church clock Chimes the hour, the seven clear strokes
Fainting with silver cadence on the night.
O’er all there breathes a solemn witchery,
And each raw newness of the glaring day
Is toned with velvet shadow. Surely here
The spirit of some ancient city walks
At even-time : and all her palms are dead ;
Ten thousand years ago her music failed ;
Her crumbling palaces are swathed in sand.
But when the lamps are lighted she draws nigh
On golden sandals, and the air is filled
With the soft, subtle magic of her breath.
MAORILAND.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
THE PHARISEE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Upon a corner of the city street,
Where all day long the multitudes press by,
He stands, imported boots upon his feet;
Imported broadcloth on his back ; a high
Silk hat to crown his brow. Self-righteously
His glance is turned towards the passing crowd—
Paint, toiling women, trudging hopelessly,
Child-slaves of Croesus, old men bent and bowed,
Meek publicans, he counts each but a clod,
Removed by kindly fate beyond his ken ;
And lifting up his eyes to Heaven—" Thank God,"
He cries, “ that I am not as other men.”
N.S.W.
