The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The House of Representatives having said
“ Yes,” it is now practically settled that a “small com-
mission ” shall be appointed to inquire into the whole
question of insurance in Australia, and report not later
than June 30 of next year. It is far better to put a com-
mission on to the job than a committee of Parliament; for
in Australia there are very few independent actuaries
even outside the House, and none at all within it, and it
will need an actuary of large capacity to handle the
witnesses who will naturally speak for the several life
insurance offices. Every life office has one or more
actuaries connected with it, and presumably there isn’t
one of them who doesn’t believe his method is sound.
The commission will therefore have to be strong enough
to set expert witnesses right where they are wrong.
If there is to be a searching inquiry into the
affairs of every office, it is very doubtful if the job can be
done by the end of June next. Yet nothing less than
that inquiry is called for. The majority of the life offices
are on the mutual principle, and the policy-holders are
therefore interested in knowing what are the weak points
in their organisation. If there are weak points, the
policy-holders will be the people who will suffer. There is
so far no regular State examination of insurance offices in
Australia; and until one thorough examination is made,
the public really will not know to what extent an exami-
nation is necessary. Above all things it is therefore to be
hoped that the affairs of every individual office will be
investigated.
That the result will be very serious for some
offices is highly probable. But that is the very reason
why the investigation should be made—and why it should
be made, too, by men whose report will be tantamount
to a judgment of the High Court. For if the position of
some offices is such that the public will get a shock when
it finds out what that position is, the public can’t too
soon have its eyes opened. Supposing that anybody who
liked had been allowed to practise medicine, it might be
very disastrous to some of the men if an inquiry were
made into their qualifications ; but not to make
an inquiry would very certainly prove a lot
more disastrous to the public. And life insurance is a
matter as to which the public can practically make
no personal inquiry. At the most it says—“ What sort of
bonuses does the office pay ? ” Yet that may be an abso-
lutely rotten test. Bonuses are based entirely on estim-
ates—and everybody knows what sort of things estimates
are. If an office likes to estimate or assume that it will
get 4J per cent, interest, it can declare big bonuses
straight away ; but if a policy-holder lives very long the
chances are that he or his beneficiaries will never draw
them. And there are a hundred other much more deli-
cate little tricks. For instance, an office can assume that
every industrial policy existing will remain in force for a
certain period—a very daring assumption, as anybody
who knows anything at all about industrial insurance
will admit.
The fact, then, is that, if a proper inquiry and
report were made, it might be very difficult thereafter
for some offices to get any business ; in other words,
people are only going to them now because they don’t
know their real weakness. And that is where the State
will be able to come in ; for it should take over the
weakest of these offices, and on that foundation form a
Commonwealth Assurance Department. This would
require no capital; the offices that should be taken over
would be of the mutual type, with perhaps one or two
unimportant exceptions. Proper valuations would, of
course, have to be made, and existing policy-holders
might not perhaps be guaranteed quite all that they are
now led to expect they will get; but the position, so far
as they are concerned, would steadily improve on account
of the great saving of expenses that would at once
follow upon the amalgamation of offices and the stoppage
of much of the present shocking waste. The officers
would have to be considered; many of them would be
Bntirely superfluous. But it would pay the policy-holders
handsomely to make a fair provision on dispensing with
their services.
Something might possibly be done also with
regard to the Australian policies held in foreign offices.
Some of these offices would, no doubt, be very glad to
transfer the whole of their Australian business to the
Commonwealth’s office, and the vast majority of policy-
holders would be glad to be relieved of certain haunting,
though possibly quite unnecessary, doubts and fears.
* * * *
Treasurer Peake, of South Aus., had a cheerful
tale to tell the Assembly last week. For the year to the
end of June he expected a revenue of £3,095,329, and he
beat his estimate by £559,337. A little surprise like that
is very grateful and comforting, but it suggests things.
When a Treasurer can’t get his estimate closer to the
reality than that, either he doesn’t know much about the
business (and nobody suggests that of A. 11. Peake), or
the State is liable to tremendous ups and downs. And
the State that is liable to downs can’t afford to
take the ups too seriously. In this case, certainly,
the Commonwealth refunds exceeded the estimate
by £175,000; and it is to the credit of S. Aus. that, in
view of those figures, it refused to join in the buzzing
around' the head of the Commonwealth Government.
To a great extent, however, the large Customs collections
were due to the almost phenomenal wave of prosperity
which swept over the State—the wave that is just about
certain to have a trough behind it; in fact, the trough is
already in view, and a good many of the passengers are
suffering even how from that sinking feeling. Also,
owing largely to the boom in metals, and the fact that
S. Aus. is the railway carrier for the Broken Hill mines,
the railway net revenue was £266,000 over the estimate.
So, one way and another, the total revenue was £459,000
ahead of that of the previous year.
The S. Australian expenditure was £3,171,144 ;
so the position for the year was—
Revenue .. .. .. £3,654,666
Expenditure 3,171,144
Surplus .. .. .. £483,522
And for the current year the estimate is—
Revenue .. .. .. £3,380,820
Expenditure .. .. ... ’ 3,308,897
Surplus £71,923
Thus the Treasurer expects to get in £273,846 less than he
got last year, and he expects to spend £137,753 more. The
revenue and the surplus are, however, to the extent of
£40,000 conditional on the passing of some amendments
of the land-tax—proposed last year, but then thrown out
by the House of Landlords, on the ground that the money
wasn’t wanted : a ground which, with the present con-
siderably altered outlook, certainly doesn’t apply now.
The Government’s proposal is to leave undisturbed the
present Jd. in the £ on estates up to £5OOO, but to add
from Jd. up to 3d. on estates varying from £5OOO to over
£lOO,OOO unimproved value. While it is proposed to thus
take £40,000 more from the Fat, it is proposed to take
£26 000 less from the Moderately Lean by raising the in-
come tax exemption from £l5O to £2OO, and making the
exemption apply all round. Hitherto it only applied if
your income was £4OO or under.
Though its record has been smudged by heavy
borrowings, S. Australia has done very good work in
recent years in cleaning up its books. In 1887—about the
blackest period in the State’s history—the accumulated
deficit amounted to £1,108,795 ; and, to push that skele-
ton further back into the cupboard, it was decided to
issue £1,100,000 worth of Treasury bills. At the same
time it was decided that the old drift positively must
stop ; wherefore a sinking fund of i per cent, of the pub-
lic debt was established. It was a case of the sick devil
resolving to be a saint —though a saint in strict modera-
tion. Fortunately, Broken Hill had been discovered just
before this, and first that field, and subsequently the
Westralian goldfields, put S. Australia on its feet ; for,
apart from other things, they opened up new and
highly profitable markets for S. Australian pro-
duce. Still, other deficits dropped in now and then,
and brought up the total to £1,609,545. The whole
of that except £95,451 has been got rid of; and, in
addition, £1,250,590 of a sinking fund has been accumu-
lated. It might, however, be a better sinking fund than
it is ; for, apart from its extreme gradualness, the S. Aus-
tralian Government has the bad habit of using it to
redeem bonds falling due. The essence of a sinking fund
is that it shall go on accumulating by accretions of
interest. Thus when £lOO,OOO of old maturing debt is
paid off out of sinking fund, the State ought to issue a
new lot of bonds for £lOO,OOO to the Public Trustee (S.
Australia has one) or the Sinking Fund Commissioners,
and go on paying interest. Sinking fund interest belongs
to the sinking fund ; and when the sinking fund is used
to wipe out instalments of debt absolutely, it is robbed of
interest to which it is entitled.
S. Australia has had wonderful luck. This
mere handful of people, living in a State which is subject
to violent fluctuations of fortune, had actually under-
taken to repay £6,034,700 within two years. Five years
ago the outlook was nearly pitch-black. The State had
an accumulated deficit of nearly a million ; and it had
only a few thousands in hand towards meeting its I O U
for £6,034,700. Yet even then it is doubtful whether it
realised on what a precipice it was standing. At that
stage a series of remarkable accidents began to happen.
The eastern States had a drought, and S. Australia, which
happened to have a fairly good season, coined money
out of shipments of fodder. On that followed
three excellent seasons and a fair one. Prices
were phenomenally high all round. Surplus followed
surplus. In the other States money was extraordinarily
plentiful. Helped, without a doubt, by the good advice
of the Bank of Adelaide—the bankers to the Government
—S. Australia grasped the opportunity and went on
borrowing steadily in every State of the Commonwealth
except Tasmania. So far, £2,940,100 of the old I O U’s
have fallen due, and £2,532,000 has been paid in cash-
debt transferred from London to Australia. On Janu-
ary 1 next the other £3,094,600 will fall due : £2,000,000
of it was offered the other day in London, and the under-
writers were left with no less than £1,360,000 —a pretty
good indication of the sort of soup S. Australia would
have been in by this time if it had not been for the run of
phenomenal seasons in Australia, and the consequent
ability of S. Australia to raise the money hereabouts.
The amount needed over and above the new loan—a trifle
over a million —will be paid out of the sinking fund and
some small local borrowings. And now, having got out
of a warm corner into which some amazingly foolish
Treasurers of past times had landed it, S. Australia can
take a long breath and then prepare for much the same
sort of thing in 1916-17.

In the 10 years preceding 1904 the total deficit was
£2,998,698 ; yet on the strength of a couple of fat years,
considerable reductions were made in rates and fares,
especially those in which “ the backbone of the country ”
was most interested. Approximately, these premature
concessions represent £114,000 a year. That they were
premature is further suggested by the large slump in the
surplus, notwithstanding the heavy increase in the ex-
penditure charged to capital account during the year.
This increase in the amount charged to capital account
is shown hereunder: —
1905 £62,341
1906 147,604
1907 159,428
1908 .. 400,015
Of the amount charged to this account during the past
year £77,511 represents the expenditure at Flinders-
street; a large part of the balance was spent on additions
and the strengthening of bridges, etc.—and in such items
it must be almost impossible to draw the line at what is
capital expenditure and what ought to be paid for
out of revenue. Included in capital account is
no less than £714,148 of money Spent on lines
now closed, or on surveys of lines which have
never been built. That is to say, the railways treat as an
asset £714,148 which exists only on paper or in the form
of almost valueless lines. This amount ought to be
steadily written off out of revenue ; and, if it is proposed
to run the railways on business lines, the special conces-
sions should have waited until this squaring-up had been
completed.

It is about time the daily papers—several of
them are sinners—dropped the standing wool-market
heading, “Prices Firm.” The only justification for it is
the state of the market with regard to good merinos.
They keep their price fairly well; but all other sorts have
suffered a further fall of from 5 to 10 per cent, during the
last month. And, unfortunately, when the bulk of this
season’s clip isn’t faulty it is very faulty. Of last season’s
wool held over the biggest percentage was defective ; it
was the good that commanded the limited sales. And
that disproportion of poor sorts in the held-over stocks is
playing the mischief with the market now, especially as
a bigger proportion than usual of this season’s clip is also
short-stapled or otherwise faulty. '
The Maokiland Public Trust Office

1908 .. 8,979 17,102 62,950
The amount written off is the sum spent on new office
premises. There is really no obvious reason why it should
be treated like that; still the whole cost is charged to
the year’s profit and loss account. Further, £l7lB,
“defalcations,” is also included with the year’s expenses.
Partly on that account, partly because the year’s ordinary
expenses have been larger than usual, the profit shows
considerable shrinkage. The revenue was, however, well
sustained. This revenue is derived mainly from two
sources—-commission and charges (as in the case of a pri-
vate trustee concern), and surplus from investments.
The latter marks the main difference between this office
and the private trustee co. If the Public Trustee is
appointed trustee, and the investment of the interest-
bearing funds is left to his discretion, the State guarantees
both capital and interest. TJie rate of interest is, how-
ever, fixed only from time to time. A man thus knows
when he make's his will, and appoints the Public Office
his trustee, that, unless the State goes broke, his bene-
ficiaries will be sure of their principal. As to the in-
terest, that varies.
The Office could guarantee a certain rate of
interest too ; but, as it would have to look many years
ahead, a substantial insurance fund would be necessary.
Moreover, it could only assume a low rate of interest,
just as a life-assurance office does. Any surplus, or pro-
bably 80 per cent, of the surplus (allowing the other 20
per cent, to go to an insurance or guarantee fund), could
then be divisible among beneficiaries as a bonus, which
would be exactly the same system as insurance offices
adopt. The time is certainly pretty near when trustee
companies will have to do a bit more for their
money. They make very big profits now in pro-
portion to their capital, and they risk practically
nothing. If they invest in mortgages the money may be
lost, but it isn’t their loss so long as they have a couple
of valuations to support them ; and, somehow, even the
best of property valuations, or what ought to be the best,
have a bad habit of sometimes panning out very badly,
Insurance offices practically guarantee their clients’ in-
vestments, both interest and principal; and the time will
come when the trustee office will have to do the same
thing. The Maoriland office goes more than half-way in
that direction now. It guarantees the principal, and it
'guarantees interest, but it only fixes the interest from
time to time, placing the guaranteed return as near the
full market value of money as it dares. The surplus goes
towards guaranteeing it against probable or possible
future losses. In the past year or two its sources of revenue
have been :
1905. ' ' 1906. 1907: 1908.
Commissions .. £13,784 j £l5 702 (- £15.815 £18,212
Valuation fees, etc. .. 804) ’ / 1,023 1,141
Surpluses .. .. 17,797 16,827 15,688 14,704
The last line further helps to explain the gradual shrink-
ao-e in profits. It must beTeinembered, when looking at
the size of those surpluses, that the money has to serve
as a guarantee fund, ensuring the return of principal ;
and it doesn’t take a very big slump to knock £30,000 or
£5O 000 off a million pounds’ worth of mortgages. Even
British Consols fell from 90J at the beginning of 1906 to
82J in September, 1907.
Besides looking after private estates the Maori-
land Trust Office manages the inadequate Maoriland
sinking fund and all other public trust funds—such as
the police provident fund, the coal miners’ relief fund,
the railways superannuation fund, and the native
reserves. All intestate estates it handles as a matter of
law, as well as those of persons declared insane ; and the
general results are so satisfactory that the N.S.W.
Auditor-General in his last annual report warmly recom-
mended the establishment of a similar office in the Ma
State. If the Ma State won’t, then there is all the larger
chance for a Commonwealth office, which, from many
points of view, would be likely to give far more satis-
factory results. For, as was shown only a few months
ago, a trustee office in S. Aus., no matter how good its
reputation, is barred from acting as trustee in the estate
of a person who dies at, say, Broken Hill, just over the
S. Australian border in N. S. Wales. A Commonwealth
office would get over that difficulty, among others.

The net revenue account for the past year shows that
£19,567 has'been actually paid as interest. But there is
also an appropriation of £2619 “ to meet accrued interest
on loans on which no accrued interest had been charged
to the end of the financial year in which such loans were
raised.” Apparently interest is payable on June 30 and
December 31 and previously the interest had only been
charged against revenue up to the latter date. Thus
£2619 of the Interest debited for this past year ought to
have been debited against previous years.
The depreciation charged against profits is at the
rate of 2f p.c. on permanent way, electrical equipment, and
such other capital expenditure ; and a very moderate 4
per cent, on preliminary expenses (altering telephone and
electric light lines, cost of raising loans, etc.), linking
fund contributions are at the rate of 1 per cent., with, of
course, the accumulating interest. The loan liabilities
amount to £463,423. Against that the sinking fund now
amounts to £11,896, and unappropriated profit amounts
to £5325 ; total £17,221. And against depreciation of per-
manent way, equipment, etc., there is a reserve of £30,253
—really a renewal reserve. General repai rs have, of course,
been charged so far, along with maintenance, as ordinary
working expenditure. This item will naturally grow as
the equipment, etc., get more venerable, and as it grows
profit will to the same extent diminish. In 1906 the total
was £1705 ;in 1907, £9383 ; in 1908. £14,130. Later on,
however, when large repairs become necessary the depre-
ciation reserve will be drawn upon. The situation at
present is that, after providing interest, and paying for
all current repairs, and providing £13,859 towards future
renewals, and putting by £4858 to wipe out the capital
debt, the undertaking in the past year made £4086. Of
that amount £1467 was added to reserves, and £2619 was
set aside to pay interest for the extra three months as
described above.

Except as to the amount added to reserves and the total
amount of reserves the figures for 1907 have had to be
partly estimated. Something more than £2OOO may have
been written off ; if more was written off, then the gross
profits were to that extent greater. The co.’s warehouse
premises were burned just after the year ended, and as the
stock of stationery went up in smoke no copy of the balance-
sheet was available. Partly as a result of that trouble,
but mainly on account of the very heavy liabilities,
further shares have been issued —a few ordinary shares
and 5414 pref. £5 shares. Two years ago the paid capital
was £lOO,OOO ;it is now £127,765. Meantime the liabili-
ties have been reduced from £90,757 to £69,332 ; so that
while £27,765 of capital has been got in, £21,425 of debt
has been paid off. The balance, along u'lth £BOB2
of book debts got in, has gone towards payments
on account of new warehouse property (£16,524). The
book debts are now £30,189, as against £38,271 two years
ago.. Part of the improved profit is due to the reduction
of debt, and, consequently, interest; as against that,
ordinary shareholders have, of course, to take the new
preference issue into account. The £2.7,070 which has
been raised in that way is still a debt so far as they are
concerned- Another part of the improvement in the look
of the profits is no doubt due to the lower price of wool.
Stock stands at £113,685 ; and that is necessarily a
matter of valuation or estimate. If, for instance, wool
went on falling, possibly something considerable might
have to be written off that item. There are large risks in
the business—larger risks than there need be or than
there would be if the Protection against foreign shoddy
goods were higher. Nevertheless, assuming that stock is
not over-valued in view of the weak wool market, and
considering the trouble which occurred early in the year
and the inevitable dislocation of business, the years
results are very satisfactory, and the general position is
distinctly improved. # # *
The Barrier Tramway Co. is to be wound up in
Adelaide. It never had a tramway ; all it managed to
get was a select committee of the N.S.W. Leg. Council,
which reported in favor of its bill, but on the second read-
ing it could only muster three supporters—which shows
how farcical the N.S.W. select committee system is.
There was absolute want of confidence in the, Barrier
Tramway Co. What it aimed at was a concession to build
a railway, connecting Broken Hill and the S. Australian
line, via Pinnacles ; but it hadn’t the money. In short,,
it wanted a concession to sell, wherefore Parliament
threw it out, on the ground that it hadn t
shown enough evidence of bona fides. And no
doubt quite a lot of people in the House were
all the more eager to do that because, if an accident hap-
pened and the money was raised to build the line, it
would compete with that mighty bit of 60 per cent.
Private Enterprise, the Silverton Tramway Co. In fact,
some people suspected that, if the Bill were passed, that
co. would find some way to stop the competing rails being
laid ; and, though the promoters probably never thought
of such a thing, some people who bought Barrier Tramway
Co.’s shares during their very short-lived boom in Ade-
laide reckoned upon at least making a few P°u nds
blackmailing way. But the collapse of the Bill spoiled
it all.

Very soon the Broken Hill tug-of-war on the
wages question will begin, and the opposing teams of
miueowners and men are now getting their feet set in trie
cleats. One difficulty in the way of a settlement is the
inequality of the contract system. It is owing to this
inequality that both sides are able to use such apparently'
convincing arguments tending towards diametrically
opposite conclusions. The men say' the present
system of setting contracts mostly favors the mine-
owners, and renders it possible for good workmen
to make a miserable ss. or less per shift. me
managers, on the other hand, point to the frequency
with which 15s. per shift is earned in stopes, Winzes, etc. ,
not in one mine, but right along the line of lode. Ihat
the average may be 12s. or 13s. per shift does not in the
least mollify the man who strikes a run of bad luck and
whose earnings week after week pan out at about Bs. per
shift. A reduction, be it ever so small, is regarded as
serious by such a man, and he is naturally inclined to
fight tooth and nail against it. To show how'
very difficult is the task of holding the balance
evenly between the contractor and the co., an
instance may be given. Two parties were working on
contract—both lots stoping ore—in the one mine.
The earnings of one party averaged 15s. a shift per man,
while the other party was making only 9s. per man. me
low-paid party complained to the shift-boss, and the fol-
lowing fortnight a fresh deal was made, the two parties
changing places. The result was that the party which
had been making 15s. a shift in its old place increased its
earnings to £1 per man, and the dissatisfied party wa.
not able to knock out the 9s. it had earned in the old
stope. These are the inequalities no conference will
be able to satisfactorily adjust. Some of them arise
from inequalities in the men ; others arise from ie
rapid changes which occur in the character of the ore.
Tn the concentrating mills of the Barrier,
fashions come and go. A little while back nearly every
millman along the line of lode became enamored of the
grinding-pan, and the implement passed through various
modifications, till everyone’s individual taste was suited.
Then somebody said “ tube mills,” and before long near y
every mill superintendent was dancing as gracefully as
might be to the new tune. The B. H. Prop, has just
picked four of the kind it likes best, and the South mine
has had them in use for some time past. The south
Blocks hasn’t room for one yet, and the Junction North
can’t afford one ; but the craze is evidently spreading.
An even-grade material is one of the great desiderata in
concentration, and the hunt for means to accomplish this
is at present occupying a deal of attention in Broken Hill.
Sanguine shareholders in the Broken Hill South
Blocks got a severe shock when, a couple of months ago,
the annual report came out alleging a net profit for the
term of only £332. It w'as mighty little to show' for an out-

high working costs. The type of bearing employed has a
marked effect on the power consumed in overcoming

superiority of the Hyatt Flexible Roller Bearings over all
other types of roller, sleeve, and other anti-friction

Wild Cat readers should get the Barrier
Miner, and read the series of articles on “ The Broken
Hill Mines—Their Costs and Profits,” beginning Sep-
tember 19.*
After conducting exhaustive tests in the con-
centration of the ores at the Broken Hill South mine, the
manager, Mr. Wainwright, has finally decided to instal
the Wilflev type of table, modified in the direction best
adapted to his particular local requirements. The great
advantage with this type of table is that it admits of so
many slight variations which adapt it to meet all classes
of pulverised ores. Provided the pulp for treatment is
graded it can be used to treat all varieties of ore from
slimes up to coarse grades. When treating slimes and
lightly mineralised ores, care must be taken to have the
slope of the table accurate, and a fine, smooth dressing
surface beyond the feather edged diagonal termination of
the rifHes. When treating coarsely ground ores or ores
heavily charged with mineral, as at the Broken Hill South,
the riffles or grooves can be carried further along the table
and in some instances right up to the concentrates dis-
charge end. Mr. Wainwright ordered six tables as an
experiment, with fewer riffles or grooves than customary,
and with some of tnem —the lower ones—carried to the
extreme end of the table. So successful has this type of
Wilfley table proved that he has ordered 50 more from
the Austral Otis Engineering Company. This same firm
has been placing several of the patent Callow Screens at
Broken Hill and elsewhere. This ingenious and simple
mechanism has solved the problem of grading prior to
concentration.* rg

All philosophic works, and Holy Writ itself,
teem with warning: of the uncertainty of life. Deaths by
natural causes have been pushed far down the years of
civilised life, but with the increase of that very civilisa-
tion the liability to accideht has enormously increased.
The railway, the ferry', the tram, and the murderous
motor car ine.vitably cause thousands of people to place
their lives and limbs in the hands of entire strangers, of
whose ability they' can have'absolutely no knowledge. It
would seem, therefore, to be only common-sense to guard
against the financial consequences of misadventure by
taking out an accident and sickness policy with the South
British Insurance Company Limited ; £5OO Death, £3 per
week Accident and Sickness Compensation for £3 per
annum. . Head. Office forN..S.W. ; 12 Bridge-street, Syd-

near Lithgow, will soon disappear, its place being taken

monopolist did not stand in the wav.

When the South Blocks chairman talked like a
father to the shareholders in the middle of July,
he told them a very different yarn from the one
they would be likely to pick up from the annual report,
even though they did take into account the things men-
tioned above. He said that if their mill could handle
8500 tons a month at 14s. per ton, and could get 78 per
cent, of the lead out of an ore going 16'5 per cent., then
with lead at £l2los. per ton and silver at 26d. per oz. the
co. could make £1750 a month profit. Figures for three
months are now available. The average monthly output
has been 8687 tons, or better than Chairman Govett bar-
gained for. The working costs have been a fraction under
13s. 6d. per ton—also better than the price Govett based
his figures on. The extraction has been 7S'2 per cent.,
and the ore has averaged 16 - 5 per cent. Whether
Govett’s estimate of profit was sound or not can’t be said
for certain ; but, anyway, the conditions are panning out
better than those on which he told shareholders they
would get a profit of £1750 a month. And lead is well
over his £l2 10s., and silver is about on the mark he
named, assuming that he meant fine silver when he
quoted 2s. 2d. per oz. Costs are extraordinarily low :
mining last month ran to only 6s. lOd. per ton. But
development ran into another Is. 3d. ; and that might
just as well be added first as last. Construction account
absorbed another 3s. 9d. per ton. That is the trouble with
so many of these pretty estimates relating to profits.
They are built in exactly the same way as the average
State Treasurer builds his surpluses—l)y omitting to
charge a whole host of things to working expenses. Yet
the money you call profit, but which you have to spend
on shafts or winzes or machinery, might just as well be
called something else for all the dividend you can get
out of it. Still, giving that all in, the South Blocks mine
has made wonderful progress, and is probably making as
much profit per ton out of its silver and "lead as any
mine at Broken Hill. Unfortunately it will never get
much help from its zinc; but there are 1 compensations
even with regard to that.
• * * *
Sydney shareholders, who own such a big bundle
of the shares in the Hillgrove Prop. (N.S.W.), met again
early this week, and expected to be told that the proposed
inspection of the mine by A. E. Savage and J. W. Ash-
croft—now, bj' the way, partners in a Sydney business—
could proceed at once. To the astonishment of some of
them they found that not even a cable on the subject had
been sent to the London office owing to the stone-broke-
ness of the co. at this end. Under those circumstances
all that the Sydney shareholders could do was to put up
the money to pay for the report; and on that report will
very largely depend whether the shareholders put up
more money or let themortgagees(debenture-holdersand
others) struggle with the disastrously-financed show.
From Baker’s Creek. 310 tons returned 6890 z.
gold, which included 160 oz. from the periodical clean-
up of the plates. This mine is economically managed,
and in that respect is a contrast to its neighbor next door.
Baker’s Creek management wasn’t the sort to spend
thousands to get over a difficulty with the firewood
carting in wet weather. Even in the matter of
development work they are trying to find out
what the prospects are below No. 18 in order
to shape out a scheme of development. Chasing
a thin streak of golden stone down to 2000 ft. requires
good management, if operations are to be made to pay.
Development work on a footwall branch of Smith’s reef
is giving fair results. The co. is well-off for cash, and if
there is an improved outlook for the deep levels, it will
help to lift the generaMepression at Hillgrove.
The Colon Peaks and other mines at Burra-
gorang and Yerranderie (N.S.W.) have been shutdown,
and the miners in that district are in a bad way. All the
shows are small, and mostly contain narrow, rich veins.
While the lead market boomed and silver was up in sym-
pathy, a few of them made good profits; but silver is
nearly 6d. per oz. cheaper now, and the smelters’ charges
have also gone up, so the mines are hit both ways. The
little mine, as a rule, comes out of a metal slump worse
than a big mine. The latter nearly always has some
patches of rich ore, which in ordinary times it uses for
“ sweetening,” and on which it can draw more freely
when things get desperate ; while, if it works at all, the
little mine has to work during a slump on just the same
class of ore as it worked before—it isn’t big enough to
allow of picking and choosing.

Mt. David (N.S.W.) crushed 380 tons stone for
1660 z. ; 21oz. from the Huntingdon mill and 550 z. from
340 tons of tailings. The tonnage (and it’s low-grade
stone) is small for such a big co., yet it seems as if an in-
creased output will have to wait until the new shaft is
down and the mine opened up more extensively.

"Charters Towers reports—“ Mexicans the only-
live stock on the market.” Brilliant Extended crushed
5000 tons for £11,114. Profit is estimated at £4lOO, in
which case costs must have comedown with a crash from
£2 a ton. Word also comes that the Bonnie Dundee has
a credit of £3117, with a big crushing going through ; but
as far as can be made out the profit at the close of the
half-year was £1631, arid to make up a credit of £3117
unexpended call money and £BO7 from forfeited shares
has been included, so the £3117 doesn’t represent working

3d. on 200,000 shares. As a pioneer of deep ground, the
Cross is asking for something out of the mining vote.
On the strength of results obtained by deep boring the
shaft is being sunk below 2000 ft.
East Mexican, Charters Towers, has had another
good crushing. The first contract for driving No. 6
level from the boundary is completed ; the heavy mineral
stone taken out has produced about £2500 worth of gold.
So far, No. 6 has proved very much better than the level
above, which has had hard ground and a narrow, erratic
reef to contend with. No. 6 has also proved fully twice as
rich per foot driven as the level below, (No. 7, N. B. Free-
hold), so the fears about the varying character of the reef
had some foundation. But the very fact that conditions
change so often is the food the sanguine punter lives on.
All four levels in the N. B. Freehold are on profitable
stone, and sinking the main shaft has been commenced ;
but it takes it all to hold up the exceedingly dull market,
which shows prices that are still a long way ahead of
developments. Mills’ United, which is acting the good
foster-mother to the Mexican and the N. B. Freehold, has
lately had an improvement in No. 10, west of the big
slide, and the rich block of ground in the upper levels is
helping to maintain quarterly dividends (£15,000).
It transpired at the inquest on the bodies after
the recent bad accident at Mount Morgan that numerous
big falls had occurred since the underground quarry
system was introduced in place of the square-set system ;
in fact, there had been two previous falls at the 750 ft.
workings. If these falls are frequent, then it is obvious
that the underground quarry system is not the proper
one. If the ground will stand, well and good ; there is
no need to waste money on timber in that case. But the
only Justification for doing without timber is that the
ground will stand without it. There certainly seems
room for inquiry into the alleged frequency of the falls.
The rather more expensive square-set system might not
in the long run prevent the heaviest of them, but the
timbers would gradually feel the weight, and would
“ talk,” thus giving warning of danger.

September was the best month Gympie (Q.)
has had for a long time, and the old field was quite lavish
of church money with half-a-dozen thrippenny dividends.
During the month 12,516 tons were crushed arid £12,895
paid in divs. No. 2 S. threat Eastern has been getting
good stone from No. 3 reef, and, after much doubt and
tribulation, has completed a century of dividends. The
output will be increased, as more men have been put on.
The. co. has £3OOO on fixed deposit, so if No. 3
and the Inglewood reefs continue to shape
well there should be another dividend before
Christmas. South Glanmire and Monldand is also
on the Inglewood, with rather poor returns ; the work-
ings have not yet entered favorable black slate country.
Columbia Extended keeps a battery of 10 head at work ;
the last crushing was 313 tons for 4020 z. This line is
much fancied on the strength of the specimen stone ob-
tained in No. 2 North Columbia. No. 1 crushed 288 tons for
2720 z., and No. 3 has struck gold-bearing' formation.
East Oriental is driving on good stone to the south ; 1505
tons (with some specimens from the show-case) returned
1085 oz. smelted gold. The mine is cheaply worked, and
four dividends amounting to Is. 4d. per share have been
paid. This development knocks out the theory that the
eastern ground is valueless. But, then, Gympie makes a
shuttlecock of theory.

* * » *
A water-jacket furnace is being substituted for
the reverb, at the Anaconda copper mine (W. A.). During
the past 12 months £44,000 has been taken out of this
property, and now that the supplies of oxidised ore are
getting short and the sulphides do not lend themselves
to the old method of smelting, the change alluded to is
being made. The present intention is to smelt 1000 tons
a month at the mine and to refine the low-grade product
at Fremantle.
* * * *
Garden Gully (W.A.) has just treated 300 tons
for an average of 2dwt. This is the mine from which four
tons of “ golden stone ” were alleged to have been stolen,
about a year ago, and which has since given a succession
of poor returns. Thera was a suspicion at the time that
the yarn about the robbery was spread in order that
shareholders and the public would blame the workmen
and not the mine for the wretchedly poor returns that
were inevitable. The management still, however, pro-
fesses to have faith in the property.

Someone has been induced to find more cash for
the development of the Boulder Main Reef, and this,
coupled with heavy local buying, has put the shares up a
bit. The present intention is to go down deeper and
then open out again, meantime allowing tributors to look
for something near the surface. The mine has some
speculative value on account of its past record and close
proximity to the Boulder arid Horseshoe; but not as
much as the present quotation indicates.
Shareholders were told at the Paringa meeting
that the Kalgoorlie property was best left alone for
the time being, and that a tight hold was being kept on
the remaining capital in the hope that something better
would turn up. An attractive proposal was said to be
before the Board, and if it proves to be as good as it
looks, shareholders are to be asked to come "along and
say what they think of it.
J. H. Curie’s opinion of the Burma mines, in
which the Lake View Consols Co. has sunk a good deal of
hard cash : “ In the flotation, too little cash was provided
for building the railway... Those in control, no doubt,
thought that for a 2ft. line, merely to run down 115,000
tons of slag, any sort of makeshift would do, and they'
provided accordingly. They forgot that the whole dis-
tance was through hilly country' and in dense jungle, and
that for several months each year the road-bed and cut-
tings lie under torrential rains. These initial mistakes
cost the co. ay'ear’sdelayin reaching its slags, likewise the
raising of £40,000 further capital for the mere completion
of the line. The transport question is the weak point, but
it does, not appear insuperable. Local tradition says the
mines have not been worked for 60 years, then perhaps dis-
continuously. I should imagine the days of their splendor
toihave been several hundred years ago. The fact to
grasp now is that there are some 55,000 tons of lead (in
the form of 48 per cent, lead slags) ready to ship, and the
co. expects to begin this work late in 1908. With good
prices for lead and the finding of pay ore under the old
workings the mines ought to be a successful venture.”
No doubt; assuming good prices and pay ore, quite a lot
of things would he successful ventures.
* * * *
Cathcart, Ararat (Vic.), knocked off 90 men
without an official word being known in Melbourne till
the blow fell. The Smelbourne Board says it knew no-
thing. Yet the local directors must have been mere
drifters of the first water if, perceiving that shares were
slipping downhill, they' did not try to get at the reason.
The Bulletin has, more than once, told all and sundry
what is happening at the Cathcart. First, the ground is
heavy and swells, so that work, besides being costly, can-
not be hurried. Next, the wash drains slowly. Further,
the lead is narrow', and, unless a big length of ground
is drained, the mine won’t stand a big staff.
The drives across the lead go at intervals of
200 ft. There never has been 500 ft. of wash properly
drained to operate on, and there never was any reason
for putting on 10 parties. Costs were all right as long as
even partly-drained ground was available, but, after that
boundary was passed, and although oz. dirt was being
stoped, it cost nearly the gold contents to take the wash
out. Chairman Dodds and the Ararat people formerly
fired abuse at Bewick, Moreing and Co. What about
their own shortcomings, and the promised divs. ? As it
is, the mine will have to pretty well stand idle for three
months.

Bad news comes from the Plateau, Ballarat :
South Star is throwing up the sponge so far as deep work
goes. This means that a large portion of the Bent grant
of £5OOO won’t be wanted, as it had been intended to use
the money to test the country out to the east. The
general taxpayer won’t complain, because he has no
belief in deep Plateau mining', as he judges by the poor
results got at below 1600 ft. Star of the Bast and Central
PlaiteaU, next door, will now feel the pinch, because it
takes the combined pumping efforts of the three to keep,
the water down. South Star will try its luck at the tippler
levels. ; What with the fear of flooding from the old lead
near the surface and the scratching out done below, more
than the ordinary mining risk has to be faced. The posi-
tion to-day is that 10 years of deplorable mining at the
Sebastopol Plateau have decided investors as to the poverty
of the ground, 1

“ Yea,st,” from Pt. Darwin : Itis no use miners
coming here at present. Take the West Arm, for
instance. Five claims are being worked, but only two
are employing labor—the Northern Queen (three white,
miners at 11s. Bd. per shift, which is the recognised white
miner’s wage) and the Mona, owned by a local syndicate,
employing five white miners and four white surface hands
(wages 11s. and 9s. 6d. respectively), along with 25 to 30
Chows. Several other claipis are being, worked by their
owners, who are struggling along. (Want of capital to
contend with the water is a great drawback. There are
some good claims which would pay well if they only had
the machinery and pumping gear; for the deeper
the lode the better the tin. There is a gold claim on
West Arm owned by a local syndicate, and known as
the Golden Boulder; to this the Government has given
£5OO to help develop the mine. That £5OO is going in
finding work for Chows, w'hile there are dozens of whites
out of work. Premier Price, when in England, said that
John Bull should not lend money to Germany to build
battleships. But how about his own Government lending
(or giving) money to a syndicate to employ Chinese? All
hands employed by the Government here are supposed to
get a “ tropical allowance,” but the only ones that get it
are those who sit in their offices for a few hours a day.
As regards the climate, those who are living on the
Chinese are about the only ones that run it down), and say
that white men can’t work here. They don’t want whites
to come along and upset things.
* * * *
Wunderlich, Limited, well and favorably known
throughout! Australia as makers of steel ceilings and steel
walls, and art workers in metals, opened new offices at the
works at Redfern (Sydney), on Monday last. The building,
as befits an artistic firm, is elegant, ornate, and made more
beautiful by being largely composed and altogether
decorated with materials made in the firm’s own work-
shops. Great sheets of steel were stamped into designs
by gigantic steel hammers, and the whole piece after-
wards painted and decorated by machinery for the
edification of the visitors, who, having had their
mouths opened by the simplicity and beauty of the
process, were then taken to where there was some induce-
ment for them to close their lips again on a good dinner.
The managing director told the story of the works, and
said that, although they had grown under Freetrade con-
ditions, they had flourished under Protection. What
struck his hearers most was the announcement that, owing
to Protection, the firm had been enabled to reduce prices
—the turn-over had increased so that, in proportion to
output, they were able to cut expenses considerably—and
that it paid to reduce the price of the products to make
them more popular.
* * « •
The editor of a small trade weekly published in
London was lately asked to supply an Australian syndi-
cate with an estimate of the cost of placing a new product
on J. Bull’s market in an impressive manner. The reply
was an interesting study in the rapacity of professional
floaters. For the printing of 100,000 prospectuses only
£2OO would be charged, but the issuing- of them would
cost another £3OB 6s. Bd.—which is a strictly legal-look-
ing estimate. Solicitors’ charges, including registration
fees, would tot up to £llOO. Advertising would amount
to £4OOO, and altogether a draft for £5618 6s. Bd. would
be required by the agents, who for that money undertook
to give the product a proper announcement to the British
public. Needless to say that the £4OOO of advertising
would be put into the hands of the combined firms who
have somehow got an octopus grip of the business in
England.

ABORIGIN-

“ Walilia ” (Papua), his life :
Daylight: And a raucous black voice saying-: “Master,
master, your coffee.” Climb out of bunk, feeling- crook—
sore head from fever over-night. Drink coffee, and wan-
der out to the verandah to cool and sample tropical
smells.
. Breakfast : Tinned beef and yams, hard biscuits,
tinned butter, tinned jam, tinned milk. A smoke-
tinned tobacco. Note arrives, which runs Plas them
boys as struk work and wood you come and talk to them
and put them in chokey yours respectally William
Bauer.” Dive into the hot, steaming rain to investigate.
Road through a sago swamp, mud like porridge, and a
mile of it to traverse. Get to Bauer’s camp and hear both
stories. Boys reckon Bauer “fights too b much.”
He says: “ You know, Boss, what them cows is fer back
tork? And that blank there this morning gives me lip,
and 1 ’its ’im with a switch, and then the rest just climbs
out of the claim and stops toil.”
Boy’s story: “ Bill, he hittem me alonga shovel, talk
no good, me lun ’way.”
Hunt boys back to work and caution William severely.
Return, change clothes, and start office work. Peace for
a while, then : “ Please, master, wife belonga me he come.
I keep him alonga my house?” I refuse. His seventh
“ wife” in two months I The last faded away one night
with all my socks.
Lunch : More tinned dog (this time feebly disguised
with currj'-powder and rice), more biscuits, more tobacco
—all tinned.
Afternoon : Dress native sores and wounds. Deal out
medicine and stores. Sneak out between showers and get
sweet potatoes planted ; also superintend the burial of a
native. Assault cook-boy for stewing toilet-soap with the
meat, despite his explanation that he wanted to make
“see-tew,” and took it for a “soup-tablet.”
Dusk : Tea and more embalmed cow, cake, etc. Mos-
quitoes arrive in clouds. Get under net and read and
smoke. Commotion ! Grab stick and go outside to find
my servitor scrapping with his wife. Drop stick on him
several times, and return to bed. Hot as Sheol, and the
drumming of the anopheles like unto the cries of souls
in Purgatory. I dream weird dreams. Wake up to find
a “boy” in my room. He says, “One fellow wild pig
been come inside fence and eatem tlousers belonga you
I wash to-day, no dry, leave him Tonga line, pig he find
’em.” Swear some, and urge the savage from my pre-
sence. Sleep again, and then : “ Master, master, coffee
he stop,” and another day begins.

“ F. Melton’s ” tribute :
Re Arabs. Am working at present on a Paroo station,
where most of the horses are by an Arab stallion out of
ordinary station mares. After several years’ experience
of station hacks, would rather have these than any others.
They are very docile, sound on their feet, hardy, have no
vice, and are good walkers. In addition, they are beauti-
fully easy and free to ride—a point that is appreciated
when riding 30 or 40 miles daily.

Matty would walk 20 miles on the off-chance of
gettin’ a punch on the nose, and his only interest
m a stranger lay in the possibility of the newcomer
wantin to have a few rounds with him. And
yet Matty was no great fighter ; as often

late lamented. X never saw a man so disgusted as

Harry Stockdale side-tracks the Arab :
Ex-Justice Boucaut alleges (B. 24/9/’08) that I supplied a
most misleading environment for the Arab horse when I
said that for 1400 or 1500 years he had lived in flat, sandy
country. Professor Ridgeway, of Cambridge University,
supported by numbers of other learned men, maintains’
that the Arabs possessed no horses of any kind until after
the birth of Christ, and probably not before the third or
fourth century after that. Lady Anne Blunt, whom Mr.
Boucaut himself quotes as a foremost authority, and from
whose stud the foundation of the Quambi stud was pur-
chased, speaking of the country near the stud of the
Emir Mohammed Ibn Rashid (the far-famed Nejd horses),
says : “To understand it rightly, one ought to consider
what Arabia is, and what the Arab character and mode of
life. The whole of the Peninsula, with the exception,
perhaps, of Yemen, and certain districts of Hadramant,
within the influence of the monsoon winds, is a rainless,
waterless region—in every sense of the word a desert.
The soil is a poor one, mainly of gravel or of sand, and,
except in a few favored spots, unsuited for cultivation ;
indeed, no cultivation is possible at all in Nejd, except
with the help of irrigation, and, as there is no water
above ground, of irrigation from wells. Even wells are
rare. The general character of the central plateaux, and
of the Peninsula, is that of vast uplands of gravel, as
nearly destitute of vegetation as any in the world. The
desert surrounds them like a sea.” 'Mrs. Burton, wife of
the celebrated explorer, says: “The famous mares are
kept in the desert.” Mr. Boucaut finally claims the Arab
to be “the grandest horse humanity has yet known.”
But is this absolutely correct ? As an enthusiast, he no
doubt fully believes the Arab to be matchless. But,
though I am an admirer of that animal, and a firm
believer in his courage and stoutness, very desirable
qualities in any good horse, I am not prepared to admit
his claim to such a proud position. Some capable
judges of horse flesh have set down the Arab as very in-
ferior. Personally, I cannot overlook the fact that nearly
all our most famous horses trace back to the imported
Arabs and Barbs.
* * * *
“Quan”: jRe the speed, endurance, and
weight-carrying powers, and the soundness of the
legs and feet of the Arab horse. Writing re-
cently about his well-known charger, Vonolel,
Lord Roberts said :
I bought the horse in Bombay in 1877. He was a pure-
bred Nejd Arab, and was then five years old, and had
quire recently been landed from Arabia. The following
year I took him to Afghanistan, where he was with me
for two years in extremes of heat and cold, and very
often with difficulties about proper food for him. But
while other horses fell off in condition from not
getting forage, the little Arab maintained his
throughout. I kept him all the time I was in
India, and in 1893 brought him to England. He
attracted great attention at the late Queen’s Jubilee in
1897. He died two years afterwards in Dublin, where I
resided while commanding in Ireland. During the 22
years he was in my possession he travelled with me
over 50,000 miles, and was never sick or sorry. He
measured exactly 14h. 2in.
Another Arab that was in Lord Roberts’ march
from Cabul to Kandahar was Maidan, which
belonged at that time to Colonel Brownlow, of
the 72nd Highlanders. From his second to his
fifth year Maidan was a racehorse. Then till he
was 17 Colonel Brownlow, who weighed 18st. 81b.,
used him as a charger. When his owner was
killed at Kandahar, Maidan was bought by Lord
Airlie, who won many races with him in India.
Subsequently he was sold to the Hon. Eustace
Vesey, who shipped him to England via Marseilles
(France). The voyage occupied 100 days, and
during that time Maidan did not lie down. When
23 and without a blemish, he broke one of his
legs, and was destroyed. He was half an inch
short of 15 hands high.
“ Werrimee ” : Some time ago Mr. F.
Turner, F.L.S., in an address at the Naturalists’
Club, stated that, in his opinion, the most remark-
able trees in Australia were the cream of tartar
(of N. Australia), bottle tree (Queensland), flame
tree (N.S.W.), messmate (Victoria), sugar gum
(S.A.), and the fire tree (Westralia). Which
suggests botanical names for the different Staters
more fitting than some of the terms by which they
are distinguished. Northern Terri-
tory, Cream-o’-Tartars; Queens-
land, Bottle-o’s ; N.S.W., Tree
Blazers; Victoria, Messmates;
S. A., Gumsuckers (usually applied
to natives of Victoria); and Wes-
tralia, Firesticks.

“ Machete ” : Re the Papuan sorcerer
(B. 24/9/’08). Far from being the alleged helpless
old gentleman who is made a victim to the super-
stitions of his fellowmen, and is then gaoled by a
complaisant Government official, the Papuan
“ pourri-pourri man,” to give him his pigeon-
English name, is invariably the most infernal old
villain in the vicinity. The Papuan only recog-
nises the direct and visible means of departure
from this earth, such as the stone club or the
rifles of the native constabulary, or similar
material agencies. Every form of death from
natural causes is consequently the result of be-
witchment—or “poisoning.” The magician, for
instance, will knot a strand of grass across a path
where his “mark ” will break it with his shin.
This done, the victim is supposed to peg out, and
often complies readily. Where pneumonia, or
something of the sort, takes off a member of the
village, the aged and perfidious practitioner takes
full credit for the job. In this way he estab-
lishes a connection that is both lucrative
and extensive without raising a finger in actual
violence. No one who has not experienced it can
credit the terror inspired by some of these old
charlatans. If one of them desires the best pig
or the plumpest maiden he asks for it, and sees
that he gets it. No young man is a “pourri-
pourri” artist, as a rule. It is a weapon in the
hands of the old men, and they guard it very
closely.
* * * *
“ Mallarapoonie ” (ramblingly anent cats) :
Where in this wide continent will ye not find
cats? They are everywhere. Once they were
tame and domesticated. But the march of the
years has rendered them wild—in many cases
dashed wild. I have met Thomas, or the tracks
of Thomas, in the sandy Centralian wastes, in the
tropical scrubs of the Northern Peninsula, and
among the Stokes, the Leichhardt, and the
Grampian Ranges. He prowls in the moonlight
on the shores of Lake Eyre, in the gullies round
Kosciusko, and in the spinifex regions of the
upper He Grey. A hardy, truculent varlet is the
wild Australian Tom—independent, savage, merci-
less, living by stealth and treachery, hating all,
by all well hated. He seems to thrive as well
amid Gippsland snows as in the arid Westralian
deserts. What manner of beast of prey will he
have evolved into by 20,000 A.H. or so? The
first of him seen in the Far Nor’-West was at the
time of the Kimberley rush. Some miners and
their families migrating with vehicles from
Queensland, brought him and his missus into
the Victoria basin. In that land of small, in-
digenous rodents Thomas throve. The natives soon
acquired him, and he became fashionable in the
blacks’ camps along the Nor’-West frontier. The
small niggers got to love him, and would play with
him by the hour on the blazing sand among the wur-
lies. The kids used to carry their cats about by
the tails swung over their fat and ebony shoulders
—till the cats protested by digging their nails into
their owners’ backs. Once, when the old station
cat had kittens in a stockman’s bunk, the boss
tried the experiment of cutting the cats’ tails
short off to euchre the small black nippers in their
portage habits. But when those cats grew up, the
little blacks carried them about by the hind legs
instead. The nigs, are dying off, but the descend-
ants of the cats they carried wax and flourish
mightily. Yes, I should be interested to see the
Australian wild cat of 20,000 A.D. —behind a stout
set of bars.
“H.G.K.”: “Swamper” and “S. W.
Gum” notwithstanding, “Ida F. I).” is nearly
right in her statements regarding nghamma (not
“namma ”) holes and night-wells. One of each I
can vouch for. The first is a night-well situated
about 100 miles from Albany on the inland road
to Phillip River, the peculiarities of which were
described in The Bulletin years ago. The other,
a nghamma hole, is in some flat rocks at a place
called Dhoorty, five miles south of Waigemoottha.
From this hole, which is about 2ft. deep and just
wide enough to allow dipping with a billy-can, I
have known 17 horses watered and thefluid remain
at the same level. The supply is, however, liable
to give out suddenly, but the giving-out seems
to have no definite relation to the amount
of water taken away. In the early ’nineties,
shortly after-a shower of rain which had filled the
shallow depressions in the rocks, I found at the
nghamma hole a German, who, with commend-
able thoughtfulness, essayed to fill it with the
water from the surrounding pools. They would
soon have dried up, and Hans reckoned it
economy to concentrate the supply in one deep
reservoir. Anticipating some fun I returned in
about three-quarters of an hour, and found Hans
with a disgusted look on his face. I inquired
whether he had filled the hole : “ No, no. I hafe
pud in von hondred and dirty-fife dishes, and the
vater haf nod gome bop von inches. Ach, the
dam ting leaks.” I explained the peculiarity of
the hole to him, and staggered him with the in-
formation that if he wanted the water back he
could get it by baling. I may mention, to dis-
prove the soak theory, that the soak waters
around this rock are salt. So what do you make
of that ?
* * * *
“P. Winkle”; There seems to be tan
affinity between snakes and fish in the matter of
plentitude, and vice versa! Have noticed on the
Big Trickle —especially about the notorious Snake
Island and the Moira Lakes—that when fish are on
the bite snakes are around at every turn. When
cod are taking the lines freely, tiger-snakes and
copper-bellies line the anglers’ way. When perch
schools are abroad—at dawn or sunset —then the
brown reptiles are to be met with. Maybe it is
because much of the snake-menu is that of the
fish, viz., frogs, insects, shrimps, etc. Once on
the Big Trickle I was following up a mate
who was pulling up the nightlines we had
set earlier in the evening. His duty was to
unhook and tether the fish caught ; mine to re-
bait the lines which he left on the bank. The
frogs (kept in captivity from a hunt of a fortnight
previous) on some of the hooks, especially on lines
off which fish had been recently taken, were
intact. I presently tackled a line near a clump of
reeds. I was surprised to find it stretched out
straight, and it seemed to be moving along the
bank of the billabong. Naturally, I started to
pull it in to get at the hooks. I didn’t notice the
resistance at first, but as the sinker came in sight
—I harried a lantern—l fancied I had made a
great catch in the shape of a big eel, the first I
had seen or heard of north of the Dividing
Range ! But I got a bit pale about the stomach
when, holding up his Nibs before the lantern, I
found I had roped in a six-foot yellow-belly snake,
that hissed like a saw-mill engine. I baited no
more lines that night.

“Oswald D.” : “ Boronia ” (B. 10/'9/’08)
agrees with “Groper” re the toughness of the
W.A brumby, but wants to know where to find
any nowadays ; and states that along the Black-
wood Biver the brumbies used to run in days long
ago, but do so no more. I have spent the last few
years on the Blackwood and its tributaries, and
know for a fact that up to some few weeks ago the.
brumbies were in hundreds. What they have
done, or where they have disappeared to since
then, I cannot imagine. My opinion is that the
brumbies will continue to breed in that district
for years, for there are miles of country on the
Upper Blackwood which is too hilly and barren
and poison-infested (except for an odd gully here
and there) for anything to live on but brumbies
and kangaroos.
REMARKS BY A DYING HIPPOPOTAMUS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
By the death of the large hippopotamus the Zoo has
lost its senior resident.— Daily Paper.
O Visitor, bespectacled and hot I
X do assure you that this life is not
What I enjoy. Once, in a jungle vast
I roamed at will, and did my hippopot.
No care I knew, but to the river brink
At dusk I’d come to meditate and drink,
And there I’d meet my fragile hippolove,
And she would hippopotamusly wink.
Here in this Zoo excursionists elate
Rush up and point, calling to Bill or Kate
To “’Ave a look at this ’ere What’s-its-name 1”
Or, “ ’lppopot! ” (Thej - steal my aspirate.)
Jesters have ofttimes stood in front of me,
And waved their cats in mock hilarity,
Cheering like this: “ Ilipp ! Hipp ! (Opotamus) !
The humor of such things I cannot see.
My hide is thick, but it affords no shield
To such remarks, and I have often reeled
Beneath the witticisms of the crowd,
And sought the further corner of the field;
O Visitor, bespectacled and hot!
I give my solemn word that I am not
A hit reluctant to resign this life,
And in the Styx to do my hippopot.
N.S.W.

“Dob Wilkins”: The other day, at
Wagga (N.S.W.), a local cabby made a wager
against a brother jehu that a certain bird of the
air—to wit, a domestic fowl, chookey-hen or egg-
layer—would not perform a deed which its owner
(charioteer No. 2) said with muchblanky-and-soda
it had accomplished on several occasions. That
was, it had perched at night upon the axle of his
cab, and been driven to the railway-station and
back home without showing the least concern at
the movement of the vehicle. The sceptical one
offered “ a pound to a shilling.” The subject of
the controversy was found reposing as usual on
the axle aforesaid, so the horse was harnessed and
a start was made, the other party to the bet fol-
lowing to see fair play. The journey, over two
miles, was successful; although dogs occasionally
ran behind in a cloud of dust and inspected the
egg-layer, it remained perfectly calm and un-
biassed. The parties received an uproarious
ovation on their return.

“Dandalup”: Be Port Hedland. One
“ Albatross ” (who is a mariner) reaches out (B.
l/10/’08) for the northern extremity of me—the
place where the hair ought to grow—because I put
in a good word for the Nor’-West, and said that
Port Hedland is “one of the finest natural ports
in the North.” For this seabird’s benefit, I repeat
that statement—it is, and I don’t give a hang
whether the other ports blush or not ! He admits
that Port Hedland really has got a depth of eight
fathoms in the west channel. That admission
substantiates my statement that “the port could
boast a depth of from six to eight fathoms,”
which it does also in other parts of the harbor
besides the channel. I’ll admit that the channel
is not the easiest in the world to navigate,
but, at the same time, the mariner (ancient or
otherwise) who is incapable of berthing his craft
at the Hedland jetty deserves to lose his “ elusive
ticket.” When this particular bird starts talking
about “bars,” he forgets that they are small
obstacles when there’s a good, land-locked harbor
behind and a rise and fall of tide amounting to
23ft. As to “Albatross’s” wild surmise that I
am a forwarding agent —I’m not. If I were, it
would give me extreme pleasure to forward
“ Albatross” to a warmer climate.

AUSTRALIAN WALKING MAN: “My word, Mrs.
Adam, things is lookin’ up! Them’s fine

IN THE COW COUNTRY.
SONNY (just received letter with the usual kisses from mother, who is on a trip to the city):
“Say, dad, wot does mother mean putting all them crosses at the bottom er the letter?"
DAD: “ Dunno. Reckon she means ’em fer barbed wire.”
“Edyson”: Poole took up the 300 acres
just beyond Justin’s selection. He had come
from the North-East, they said, and was the
son of an old settler. Anyhow, he was certainly
bush bred, a lank, angular young man with hip
bones like an old horse, and freckles like ironbark
samples. When he walked he shambled worse
than a tarantula, and his verbosity ran to about
10 words a week, two of which were expended in
wishing good morning to Justin across the rails,
and two more to asking for letters in at Crowbar.
The Justins were satisfied that Len Poole was
mad. His madness was not a dangerous malady;
there was just enough of it to make a peculiarly
eccentric and irresponsible ass of him. Poole’s
madness led him to hang about Justin’s house at
nights in the most ridiculous way. He would
Saunter up to the place after dark, and lean
loosely over the garden fence, or prop
himself between the chimneys, or
squat on the tank stand, and put
in an hour or so twiddling a
stick before straddling back
to his hut again. At first
the Justins would ask
him in, but Len
would grin and
squirm, and reckon
he thought he
ought to be going,
and when the
family went inside
he’d return to his
camp. He had
been discovered
leaning against the
house in the midst
of heavy rain, and
the family got so
used to his quaint
conduct that they
ceased to regard
him at all. When
Poole had been in
the district about
21 months, Annie
Justin was married
to Cooper at the
Mill, and after
that it was noticed
that Poole’s visits
ceased. A fort-
night later, Justin
met him on the
road, and bade him
good-day. Poole
rounded on his
neighbor, and his
face displayed a
co w 1 i k e resent-
ment. “Don’t you
be tellin’ me good-
day,” he said sul-
lenly. “I don’
wanter talk to you,
nor any belongin’ to
you. Nice girl your
daughter is, goin’ ’n’ marryin’ Cooper after the
way she strung me on/” Then ,Tustin understood.
Poole had been courting Annie in his own quaint
way, and no one had dreamt of it, least of all
Annie.
* * * *
“Arno Bay ” : One of the largest stump-
grubbing contracts on record has just been com-
pleted in S. Aus. A new stock route from Pt.
Augusta to Murat Bay, on the West Coast, has
been cleared a chain wide, mostly through dense
mallee scrub, for a distance of 150 miles, and tanks
have been sunk at 20-mile intervals. Time taken,
with a gang of eight men, 12 months, which is
held to be somewhat slick by Crow-eaters qualified
to judge.
“ Gil” : The saddle-horse and pack-horse
are little more than memories Outback on the
roads to the Big Sheds. They went considerably
out of date with the last of the good seasons, and
the speedy, economical bicycle and the hurrying
spirit of the age drove them further and further
into oblivion. Only their ghosts and dry bones
pervade the Big Silences now. The bike has
become a firmly-established circumstance, and in
fine weather it is a boon to the “big gun ” and
“ringer”—who always aspire to catch a distant
roll-call with about two and a half seconds to
spare. But in wet times it is a cross between a
family bereavement and a curse factory. Free-
wheels afford no advantage, and so the old-
fashioned but stronger and more reliable “set”
wheels predominate. When pedalling across the
level plains the cyclist is always under the im-
pression that he is pushing up-hill, and yet, on
looking back, he is prepared to swear that he
must have been coming down. No matter where
he be the rider can invariably delude himself into
the idea that he is in a geographical depression,
with the country gradually rising on all sides out
to the far horizon. But the best luck a cyclist
can encounter is to get on to a. fresh camel pad,
whereon the clods and ruts are ground into fine
dust and trampled down almost as hard as
asphalt. Consequently the wheelman is the only
man Outback (excepting the squatter) who can
mention a camel without holding his nose and
cursing in at least two dead languages.

“Kodak The experience of the Inverell
man who had one of his fingers bitten off by a
greyhound he was lifting out from the wheels of a
sulky reminds me of a similar experience. We
were enjoying the evening repast when a series of
dog-howls shattered the welkin. We rose in a
body from the hash, and rushed up the hall. The
leading lodger dashed back the glass-panelled hall
door, regardless of expense or the landlady’s feel-
ings, and we deployed into the garden. On the
ornamental iron-gate the fat brown dog from next
door was hanging by his hind legs. He had
evidently pursued the house feline home and, the
gate having banged behind him, had endeavored
to leap out again. A boarder—whose ambition it
was to win theV.C. —dashed forward with the
intention of untangling the brute and stopping
the hideous noise. We saw him get to grips with
it, but the subsequent proceedings were too
hurried to be distinguished. To us it seemed as
if 15 dogs and 10 boarders were having a fight to
the death, to the howling by a choir of 300 demons,
cockatoos and hand-organs. We all performed a
mad sort of war-dance round the whirling mass,
looking for a chance to rescue the boarder from
the dog, or the dog from the boarder, or the gate
from both of them. But no chance appeared.
The riot proceeded and grew in fury until the dog,
in making a particularly vicious snap at the
boarder’s ear, fell off the gate and rushed furiously
in pursuit of a cat that had been watching from
the other side of the road. We carried the bitten
boarder inside, and sent in next door to ask if
they had any whisky. Next door—which had
feasted its eyes on the fight—basely retorted that
it hadn’t a drop in the house, so someone borrowed
2s. 6d. and we adjourned to the pub.
“ Wang ” writes :
Horse intelligence yarns in The Bulletin some time
back remind me of old Prince.
He belonged to Cocky Thompson, for whom I was slav-
ing at the time.
Thompson kept his chaff lying loose in a shed. The
shed door was fastened with the mean type of cocky’s
favorite catch—a piece of leather strap and a peg.
Old Prince, nosing at the catch one day, was delighted
to find how easily it was manipulated, and some time
afterwards emerged with a very satisfied look on his
dial and a ditto feeling in his innards.
Thompson nearly took a fit when he found it out. But
his meanness was of the deep-rooted variety, and instead
of buying a bolt and padlock, he fixed up some contrap-
tion out of the cocky’s stand-by—fencing wire.
The regal equine came along next day as usual, and
after a few minutes’ fruitless nibbling, a look of dawning
comprehension spread o’er his brow. He called up his
r eserves, and deliberately turning his stern, backed up
against the door for all he was worth.
No respectable door could stand much of that kind of
treatment, and with a few groans it broke away from
Cocky’s patent-wire fastening. A full-blown Prince some
time afterwards staggered out of the shed just at the
moment that Cocky let the light of his countenance fall
upon him, also a couple of handy stones.
Then Thompson determined on something desperate.
He’d stop that dashed horse getting to that chaff or
know the reason why.
He lost half-a-day watching Prince from cover. That
old robber seemed to know something was in the wind,
and deliberately fooled about for six hours. Then, the
coast seeming clear, he applied his energies to opening
the door.
Thompson bolted around a circuitous route till he
came to the back of the chaff shed, and let himself in «
back door.
The other door began to hang out signals of distress,
and Thompson grabbed a pitchfork used for throwing
sheaves about, and fitted the prongs into the cracks in
the portal.
The door creaked harder, and Prince, evidently en-
couraged by these signs, put on more steam.
Thompson set his teeth and thrust.
In describing it afterwards, we couldn’t make out any-
thing barring that he thought an earthquake had struck
the place.
The door came back with a crash and caught fair on the
tail end of his countenance, and he thought his ear had
been attacked by elephants.
He arose in time to see old Prince doing an untimed
record away from the door, switching his fly-flicker
excitedly.
It cost Thompson something for a new door. And the
old horse never came back.
CANTANKEROUS FANTODS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Big Bill and Mick good mates had been,
I knew, since eighteen-ninety-three,
And many times the pair I’d seen
Linked in a fond, fraternal spree,
And miners who frequent the inns
At Sanger’s (known as Kelly’s Sins)
Had christened them “ the Siamese Twins.”
So when Big Bill fell out with Mick,
And pitched his luggage down the hill,
And Michael gave Big Bill the kick,
And went to board with Pickersgill,
We looked into each other’s eyes,
And scraped our chins in manner wise,
Like chickens clucking our surprise.
I spoke to Bill. He said ’twas true
He’d loved Mick Hogan like a brother,
“ But it’s like this, ’tween me and you,
We ain’t cut out for one another.
His jim-jams never would agree
With mine. ’Twas horrible to see
The beggars fight so eerily! ”

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MSS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.
A.L. : A host of good touches in your satire ;
but most of it suffers from a fatal defect, viz., obscurity
of purpose. A satire should flagellate its object first,
last, and all the time Comet: “As it Was in the
Beginning ” gives the world a hard and merited knock,
but its rhyming isn’t accurate enough. When you are
being sardonic in verse it is advisable to pay great
attention to your metre....The Wasp: The afflicted
Caroline makes a very dreary poem. A fragment:—
Down her back in glittering tresses
Hang her beautiful golden hair.
Yet a times a shadow of sorrow
Cloud the brow of the maid devine.
A hopeless yearning for the power of speech
Sadden’s sweet Caroline.
Pod : Most lamentable and incomprehensible verse...

unless you provide that city with three waterfalls and a

“While Lilia Pulls the Beer,” but it fades away
into nothingness. ..Winkle : “ Feelin’ Fit ” is below par...

question, and did it rather better....Sweet-briar : The
blooms aren’t poetry; they are only rather poor doggerel
...Aster Nirvanae: “Belle” is an old idea badly
handled ...Learner: Too trivial... A Vagrant: Beads
like a plain statement of fact —and excessive plainness
becomes dreary School-boy; Jokes re Bed Cross
Society don’t appeal to us. They are mostly puns, and
puns and limericks we loathe W.H.8.: Your assault
on infamy in high Q. places is too vague. Give specific
instances of the infamies, with names and dates, and the
information may be worth using.. .Munkowurlie : As to
pars, the B. has only three rules—(l) Those good enough
are printed, no matter who sends them ; (2) those claimed
for are paid for; (3) those not good enough are not
acknowledged Magpie : Now, how w'ould you go about
proving those things when the inevitable writ fluttered

run to Spring girl. And this paper must slam the door
