GAELIC
WHISKY
(The Old Smuggler Bottle)
BEST OF QUALITY.
PERFECT PURITY.
Do you
realize
the immense importance of the unique
superiority of Odol? While all other
preparations for cleansing the mouth
and teeth are effective only during the
few moments of application, the anti-
septic and refreshing power of Odol
continues gently but persistently .for
hours afterwards. Odol penetrates into
the interstices of the teeth and the
mucous membrane of the mouth, to a
certain extent impregnating them, and
thus securing a safeguard and preserva-
tive for the teeth such as no other denti-
frice can provide, not even approximately.
Of all Chemists and Stores.
PERSONAL
ITEMS
O. H. Brook, one time Ma State’s champion
cyclist, is now “ Constable Brook,” of the Sydney
police.
Poet Adam Lindsay Gordon’s armchair—
the furniture he dreamed his verses in—is one of the
few show-pieces of far-out Longreach (Q.). Mrs.
Sutton, the owner, formerly lived at Mt. Gambier,
and secured the treasure shortly after Gordon’s
death.
There are whispers in the chambers that
Chief Justice Griffith, of the High Court, is likely
to apply for extended leave of absence. His late
work on Dante has whetted a desire for travel.
He wishes to do more for Dante than he has done,
and thinks a personal tour of the poet’s country
would help him to present the character in more
complete form.
W. P. Crick died much as he lived. He
had made two wills, and had destroyed both.
Then he set about another, and when he was re-
minded of it he thought a moment, then —“Oh,
let it go !” So he died intestate, leaving £9900, in
Government stock, a small sheep holding at Gun-
dagai, and the Randwick house he lived in.
S. Australia’s Labor Premier Price does
not get better. He looks white, haggard and
tired, and his colleagues will be glad when the
present session is over and he can get a rest. The
voyage to England early this year was meant as a
holiday, but he really worked harder than he
would have done had he stopped at home.
John Marshall, who had groped together
the champion collection of facts (and thereabouts)
concerning the early years of the Westralian gold-
fields, died the other day, in the middle of another
book on his pet subject. He had used up many
incidents in his “ Battling for Gold,” published
some years ago, and Rougemont’s Wide World
Magazine and others had greedily gulped down
many of his goldfields hair-raisers.
Dean O’Hea (R.C.), who ruled at Pentridge
(Vic.) for many years, was an obstinate man, and a
dictator to be obeyed. Yet he found his match once
in a brick carter. Their vehicles met in one of
tl e narrow lanes about Brunswick, and, as usual,
“ the Dane ” wanted the right of the road. The
bricky must go back, nothing less would suit
“the Dane.” The priestly one took out his
breviary and commenced reading his “office.”
The brickmaking one produced his nose-bag, gave
his horse a fee l and sat down to his own midday
meal. After half-an-hour “the Dane” gave in;
he said it was the only time he had ever given in,
and he wouldn’t have done it only he had been
“Listing.” The Dean died in the purple (having
been made a Monsignor), with a big bank balance
to his credit.
CHARLIE McDONALD, M.H.R., OF
QUEENSLAND.
Aboriginal One-Eyed Tommy, who was
drowned lately in Moreton Bay, spoke English
correctly and with a perfect accent. Some years
ago he walked up to Major Lyons, of Brisbane,
and, giving a military salute, asked for sixpence.
The Major refused. Tommy then said, “I’m a
good bushman, Major, and if you give mesixpence
I’ll promise to help you to escape from Brisbane
when the Japs attack it. I’ll take you right
through the bush to Toowoomba without touching
a road.” Tommy got the sixpence.
The Westralian Commission
that is trying to find out if there is
a meat ring over on that side had a
look the other day at Isidor
Emanuel, head of the firm of
Emanuel Bros., and generally sup-
posed to be the centre-stone of the
ring, if there is one. A man of
many connections is Isidor. Over
30 years ago he got into the nor’-
west cattle business, and nowadays
he is interested in Emanuel Brothers
(the other brother is Sydney); and
in Forrest, Emanuel and Co. (the
Forrests are out of that now —
the firm consists of the Emanuels
and F. H. Brackenbury). With
the Pearses and McLarty they
form the Obagama Cattle Co.,
which owns Obagama, Jubilee and
Kolyeeda stations, about 1,400,000
acres in the lot; with the Pearses
alone they own Kamwell and
Meda; with W. J. Carson they
own Margaret Downs (1,950,000
acres) and a few other trifles; with
Syd Kidman and one or two more
they own Carlton (600,000 acres);
and Forrest, Emanuel & Co.’s own
exclusive little lot consists of Sturt
Meadows, Erlistoun and Gnarloo
runs, in Westralia, besides • great
slices of the Northern Territory.
And when the Westralian sees a
retail butcher’s shingle with the
name of Holmes Bros, on it—and
he can see it in most places—he
can reckon that Isidor Emanuel is
behind that, too.
Ambrose Pratfc has written
David Syme’s “ Life.” Alfred
Deakin has furnished a lengthy in-
troduction, and Ward, Lock & Co.
are to publish the volume simul-
taneously in Melbourne and Lon-
don this month. Two men more
unlike than David Syme and
Ambrose Pratt could hardly be
conceived; yet David in his old
age took very kindly to the weaver
of startling romances, and ex-
pressed a wish that Pratt should
be his biographer.
Ex-Judge Wilkinson, who died recently,
was the last of the District Court pensioners m
N. S. Wales. He had been on the list for 15
years. The State now pays pensions to four
Supreme Court judges—Foster, Walker, Stephen
and Owen. Messrs. Foster and Walker served
years short of the necessary period to entitle
them to pensions, but as they retired on the
score of ill-health, their short service made no
difference.
“ Bee ” writes :
Little Sumner Locke is a fine exposition of
Australian versatility. Though still in her ’twenties,
the small authoress has been as many things
as an ordinary actor on a long tour. She
started as a tea-room grace ; then helped her
political sister Lilian with a registry office ; afterwards
became a typist for Robert Harper ; then took up the
duties and responsibilities of sub-editress and chief clerk on
the Native Companion, writing dainty verse and feminine
little stories what time she wasn’t writing “ Answers to
Corres.” And, at latest, the indomitable little woman
has written a no-el, which is expected to boom, and the
very creditable Cumedy of a recent afternoon.
“ Quan ” : Be Henry Cobden (B. 10/’9/’08).
On first coming to N. S. Wales he got a job as
clerk and storekeeper at Lawson’s place at
Dave} o Plains, and after five or six years there
opened a store at Molong, which he kept till his
death in 1858. Before coming to Australia he was
connected with the manufacturing business of his
brother Richard, the Freetrade and Corn Law
crank, as commercial agent. In that capacity he
travelled a good deal in Europe and the United
States.
William Bowen, chemist, of Collins-street,
and an old Melbourne identity, died last week.
He arrived in Port Phillip on Dec. 29, 1852 ; off
Point Lonsdale, the vessel ran on a sandbank, and
all the passengers had to help pull her off. Mr.
Bowen started in business as a chemist in partner-
ship with George Lewis, and has been putting
sealing-w r ax *">n his neat little parcels ever since.
Malcolm M’Eacharn, one of Melbourne’s
past gaud mayors, has purchased Galloway
House, with park and grounds, etc., from the
Earl of Galloway, with entry at Whitsuntide,
1909. The estate consists of 10,400 acres of the
best arable land, and 18’Malcolm, it is said, will
make his future home there, so adding another to
the long list of absentees. Mrs. Malcolm is a
daughter of the wealthy Bendigonian, John Boyd
Watson. Billy Murdoch, the cricketer, married
another daughter.
Dr. Paton, Sydney’s Government medical
officer, is appointed Inspector-General of Chari-
ties, vice Brodie. The doctor is a well-known
and liked Government official. The Public
Service Board appointed him to this very position
in 1900, and he was all packed-up, ready to go.
But Critchett Walker was alive in those days, and
his battles with the P.S.B. were common know-
ledge. So C.W. said a medical man wasn’t
needed at the Asylums; and as Critchett Walker
was nearest the Ministerial ear, he won. Now
Paton, who was evidently eager for the billet, gets
it after all.
“ O.” : I am angry, wild, mad. I have
just read an article in the Strand Magazine that
makes my blood boil. It is about Smiths—
eminent Smiths, ancient ana modern; John,
Adam, William, and Heaven-knows-what Smiths.
Some are lords, some historians, dine bishops,
some worse even than that. There are also thirty-
six photographs of thirty-six Smiths, and remarks
about dozens of other Smiths. And yet—yes, it’s
true. Not a word about him! Plenty about
other Australian Smiths, including brewer Smith,
of Adelaide, and Villeneuve, of Tasmania ; but of
him, Bruce, nothing. And this in the Strand,
one of the magazines of the Empiah ! What
encouragement has a man got to fly flags and bore
youngsters on Empiah Day after this ?
A queer character of Melbourne’s whole-
sale fruit and vegetable markets is Joshua Moss,
of Fitzroy, widower and produce-dealer. He has
a storeroom down in King-street, but it hardly
counts. He strolls down to the Victoria market
of a morning and buys by the load, all for imme-
diate shipment to Sydney, Brisbane, Bendigo,
Ballarat, or some other place. The grower whose
load he buys takes it to the wharf or rail and dis-
charges it there, and Moss’s minions do the rest.
He pervades markets and wharves with his
pockets full of loose gold, and pays as he goes,
and has no dread of garroters or robbers. He
keeps no books, but his memory for the cauli-
flower and the spring onion is good enough to
prevent him being “ got at ” for a penny, and the
scale of his operations may be judged from the
fact that he sent to Sydney, per boat and rail,
2000 dozen cauliflowers in one recent week.

“ Sr.” : The old lady who ran an itinerant
match-selling establishment at the corner of
Spring and Collins streets, Melbourne, has ap-
parently gone the way of all old rnatch-sellers.
She knew every Minister in all the five Federal
Cabinets, passed the time o’ day to Alfred Deakin,
“worded” under-secretaries and stout State
officials, and sold sundry boxes of lucifers to
private members and aides-de-camp. She was a
nice, respectable, tidy old lady, but she has been
posted as missing at the Commonwealth offices
for the last few months, and fears are entertained
that she has foundered.
J. M. Stuart, K.C., who died in Adelaide
on Oct. 5, at the age of 75, was for years Crown
Solicitor and Bublic Prosecutor there. He was
long ago nicknamed “Old Necessity,” as the
result of the quip of a brother advocate, who so
christened him on the strength of the old
proverb—“ Necessity knows no law.” But it was
unfair ; for Stuart knew a good deal of law, and
he managed the Govt, legal work in excellent
fashion. He was once a legal partner of ex-Judge
Boucaut, and was noted for his long white
beard, his suavity, and his good fellowship.
Latest announced visitor to Australia is
Lord Rosebery. He has some property interests
in Australia —owns the Athenaeum Club building,
Sydney, and burnt his fingers over the land boom
co. which built the Middle Harbor (Sydney) sus-
pension bridge. Also, Lord Rosebery is credited
with remarking that the social leaders of Aus-
tralia were content to sit on 10-guinea chairs and
look at 10-shilling oleographs. Altogether, he
will be an interesting visitor.
The case of the seven Sydney Eight-Hour-
day veterans who make up 500 years among them
suggests how few persons in a line may connect
the present with the time when King Edward’s
family began to reign in England. Dr. C. R.
Gregory, an English writer, dug up the case of
Sir Stephen Fox. Fox married in 1654, and his
first child died in 1655, three years before the
death of Oliver Cromwell. He married again late
in life, and had a daughter in 1727. This
daughter lived 98 years and died in 1825, when
Queen Victoria was six years old. Thus 170
years elapsed between the deaths of these two
sisters.
Thomas Bingham Muntz, a well-known
civil engineer, died at Malvern (Melb.) on the 6th
inst. Deceased was born in Ireland, but came to
Australia as a youngster in 1854. When local
government was initiated, he opened out as a
civil engineer, and did good work, consulting and
otherwise, for many a shire and council. His
biggest works in that way were at Prahran. In a
domestic way he had the distinction of being the
father of two brainy daughters. One is Miss
Edith Muntz, Master of Science ; the other, the
well-known Mrs. Muntz-Adams, who is a very
clever portrait painter sometimes.
The aged William Valentine Robinson,
late Clerk of the Vic. Illegislative Assembly,
weighed anchor and left no address on the sth
inst. He was a native of Leeds (Ma country),
and was drawn to Australia in 1861 by a special
engagement as a Parliamentary reporter to a
Melbourne paper. Once in the House, he never
left it. Five years of newspaper work were
followed by some years as a Hansard reporter,
and by further terms as clerk assistant and finally
as clerk. The duties of his last job he carried out
stupendously for 11 years, retiring on a pension in
1902. When he deceased, he was 72 and a C.M.G.
Word comes from Italy about Francesco
Sceusa, the fiery Socialist who for years had a
billet in the N.S.W. Lands Department, and over
whose opinions more than one Minister was sadly
troubled. Sceusa, falling ill, returned early last
year to Italy; and there, having recovered, he is
fighting as strenuously as ever, mainly against
Nasi, ex-Minister of Education and member for
Trapani (Sceusa’s birthplace). Nasi is only
recently out of prison, having been sentenced to
11 months and the loss of political rights for four
and a half years for corruption. As he spent most
of the stolen Government money in Trapani,
Trapani thinks he’s all right. In June Sceusa
stood as a candidate against him ; for Nasi is
always elected, although ineligible. But, in con-
sequence of the wholesale arrest of country trades-
unionist leaders (his chief supporters), charged
with plotting, etc., Sceusa failed. On August 2,
however, at the municipal elections, Sceusa, with
three others of his party, polled heavily.
“ Bloggs ” goes to Monaco :
Sydney Daily Telegraph was right “ out ” in its leader
last week on the gambling competition between Sir Hiram
Maxim and Lord Rosslyn. The latter was congratulated
for his wisdom in playing with counters. “Otherwise,”
said the paper, “he would have added the experience of
going broke to the variegated experiences that he has
had already.” As a fact, even to go broke at
roulette would have been no new experience. He
journeyed to Monte Carlo two years ago on be-
half of a syndicate, of which he was a member,
played there a system of his own invention, and went
gloriously broke in a fortnight. Maxim is a crank on the
futility of “ systems,” but he is no anti-gambling tutor.
He is a regular visitor to Monaco and a frequenter of the
rooms. Years ago, when I paid my first visit to the
Casino, I was shown a short, white-haired man punting
beside a stout, red-haired Jew. The Jew threw two or
three louis on a number, which promptly turned up,
bringing him in a pile of gold ; the white-haired man lost.
The latter was Maxim ; the other was one of the Roths-
childs. Maxim is supposed to have originated the
epigram about “ red winning sometimes and black win-
ning sometimes, but white (i.e., Blanc, the proprietor of
the Casino) winning always.” In theory, like scores of
others, he is doubtless opposed to gambling ; but in
practice he goes in for it with considerable enthusiasm.
“ Laner ” : A kindly Bulletin par on the
late George Bruce, of Melbourne, might give the
impression that he was an enthusiastic Protec-
tionist, inasmuch as he happened to be chairman
of the Denton Hat Mills. George Bruce, to tell
the truth, was merely a commercial result of his
elder brother, the late J. M. Bruce. J.M. had
the brains (until a calamity befel him), and podgy
little George did as brother told him. As for the
Denton Hat Mills, it was started by J.M., in
league with other softgoods bosses, for the benefit
of their wholesale trade. The sale of Denton hats
to shopkeepers was conditional on the mainte-
nance of a fixed standard of prices to the public ;
and the public stands to be victimised all the
time by that sort of combine trading. The busi-
ness of legislation is, of course, to provide against
the danger of good things being put to bad uses.
Australia wants all the local industry it can get,
and the hat industry at present is insufficiently
protected, judging from the increased display of
imported felts in shop-windows. But it wasn’t as
a patriotic friend of Australian manufactures that
George Bruce became connected with the Denton
Mills.
“ One-Three-Six ” writes from the Bar :
In tipping the prosy Equity leader, Alexander Gordon,
and the dapper Adrian Knox for theN.S. W. Chief Justice-
ship, you make dark horses of Cullen —probably the
most learned lawyer in the Commonwealth after Griffith —
and the virile eommonsense Alex. Shand. Of course
Wade as Chief Justice would be a clumsy legal joke, and
he’s not quite so humorless as that. A puisne Judge can
“concur,” without much damage to sound law; but a
Chief Justice has to deliver the law. Wade might be able
to concur, but not to deliver judgments. Alex. Shand,
like Pilcher (whose appointment no one would question),
has as much knowledge of common sense as common law,
and with his vigor and grasp, would be a far more
suitable man for the Chief’s post than any
anaemic Equity man, always excepting Cullen. He is of
the Cussen type—we want more of them. Wise
was in the running once ; but the present Chief’s tenacity
staved off his rush. I plump for Shand as Chief —young,
vigorous, learned, full of eommonsense, and dignified.
And, by the way, “ 10-2-4 ” is out of it when he says that
G. H. Reid is a “ painful spectacle in argument before a
discerning Judge or Bench of Judges.” It is fashionable
to discount Reid’s ability in appeals. Of course, no
solicitor would brief him to argue on a point of pure
abstract law or Equity. But in certain cases he is the
strongest man in the profession. For example, his argu-
ment before the N.S. W. Supreme Court when the late
W. P. Crick was cited to show cause was masterly. There
everything was against Reid, and the Court came pre-
pared to strike Crick off without phrases. Reid’s effort
was admitted by members of the profession who were
present to have been a remarkably fine one. He not only
handled his case well, but his appeals to the higher
regions of abstract law, justice and civil right, his manner
with the Judges—a beautiful mixture of suavity and
sarcasm—all constituted a masterly bit of work, which
had its effect for the time being. Let there be no mis-
take, Reid is a strong man—within his limitations, per-
haps the strongest man at the N.S.W. Bar.

WHEN THE BLOOD’S BAD THE
DIGESTION IS BAD.
Facts that Dyspeptics Should Keep in Mind.
A Rational Way of Curing Indigestion by
Strengthening the Stomach—An Instance
in Sydney, N.S.W.
A weak stomach, and thin watery blood go
hand-in-hand. That’s why anaemic people usually
have indigestion. When there’s indigestion, the
food doesn’t nourish and the blood hasn’t a chance
to re-establish itself. Good blood is absolutely
necessary to healthy digestion.
That is why a debilitated, run-down condition
so often brings on a disturbance of the digestive
system that frequently runs into a very severe
stomach disorder.
The principle of the tonic treatment for indi-
gestion is that it makes the blood rich and red,
promotes a normal flow of gastric fluids, tones up
and strengthens the stomach, and recovery is rapid
and permanent.
This principle was illustrated in the case of Mrs.
S. H. Longhurst, 18 Kettle Street, Redfern.
She says:—
“I used to have very indifferent health. My
appetite was always poor. Some days I wouldn’t
touch a thing except my morning cup of tea.
Often after eating I’d bring up what I had swal-
lowed and sometimes quantities of green bile. I’d
often wake up in the morning with a violent sick
headache, and I’d have it all day. The pain was
on the top of the head, just as if it was opening
and closing. I’d be very restless at night and
might not close my eyes for hours. Every morn-
ing I’d wake up with a thickly-furred tongue and
a nasty sour taste, and my food was always repeat-
ing on me. When I took a meal the food would
lie at the breast bone for hours like a hard lump
that wouldn’t go up or down. . Some days I’d be
quite swelled out with wind. It would settle
round my heart and make it beat most irregularly.
When I took up a book the lines would run to-
gether as if I were in a mist. All day I was
drowsy. I was very sallow and I lost flesh con-
siderably. I’d have aches between my shoulde
blades, especially after a meal. Some days I’d feel
better and then I’d get a relapse. I was never
really well or hearty, but all my symptons began
to fade away as I progressed with Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills. My food began to digest more easily
and I could eat without fear of after effects. I
lost the pain in the chest and shoulder-blades, and
my headaches eased off every day. I got brighter
and more energetic and began to see well. The
attacks of wind eased off. I soon lost that sickly
yellow look and put on flesh. Every dose made me
eat better. I kept on till I was thoroughly cured.
I enjoy the best of health and am a firm believer in
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.”
By their direct action on the blood and nerves
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills have cured anaemia, sci-
atica, indigestion, paralysis, biliousness, nervous-
ness, headaches, backaches, neuralgia, debility,
rheumatism and all ailments of women.
Sold by all chemists and storekeepers, and by
The Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co. of Australasia,
Ltd., Sydney, 2s. 9d. a box, six boxes 15s. 3d.,
post free.

' It seems now pretty certain that the new
Defence system will provide some pay—say £1
for the fortnight’s camp—for the compulsorily-
trained citizen youth. The extra cost would not
be formidable, and could be met by special taxa-
tion of those who, for any reason, dodge service.
A member of the .N.S. W. Government ex-
plained, quite contentedly, in the Assembly the
other day, when discussing the Peel River re-
sumptions, that ‘‘the value of land for taxation is,
for some reason or other, invariably below the
true value of the land.” The “some reason or
other” is a contemptible evasion. There is no
uncertainty about the reason. It is that dishonest
landowners under-estimate the value of their hold-
ings so as to swindle the country, and dishonest
Governments condone the swindle.
The N.S.W. railways’ annual re-
port shows once more the size of the
subsidy which the “backbone of the
country ” gets from the State that i«
alleged to live on the backbone’s toil
arid sturdy independence. Last year
the average amount paid per ton for the
cartage of grain and flour from up
country was 3s. per 100 miles, and for
hay, straw and chaff 3s. 2d. per 100
miles; but the manufacturer, who has
to send his stuff in Class 11. or Class
111., had to pay from £1 12s. 7d. to
£2 os. 4d. It isn’t the “backbone of
the country’s ” fault that he gets his
goods carried for 3s. when the manu-
facturer has to pay from £1 12s. 7d.
to £2 os. 4d. for his goods ; but it does
suggest that wheat-growing and wool-
raising are not the great natural indus-
tries they are cracked up to be by the
importing crowd. Anyway, if an alleged
champion wanted a start of 70yds. in a
100yds, race, his claim to be called the
champion would come in for consider-
able scoffing.
It is an amazing thing, yet true
according to the records, that last year
in all the Northern Territory (523,620
square miles) not one person died be-
tween the ages of five years and 20.
That, of course, excludes aborigines, who
don’t register. Unfortunately it doesn’t
mean only that the Northern Territory
is a wonderfully healthy place. It means
also that the Territory is so shockingly
empty and neglected that of Euro-
peans there were altogether only 1302
on Dec. 31, and most of them adults,
and of Asiatics 2295 (and as many
more as could dodge the police count).
Of females the whole 523,620 acres owns
only 439 whites, and of Asiatics 148 —
one to every 15 Asiatic males : a shud-
dersome proportion which means fearful
things for the Black Sister who sits at
our front door.
Now that it has got a fairly respectable
all-Australian tariff for the first time, this country
is beginning to find out a few of the things it
really can make. Somebody, for instance, has
just remembered that way back Binghi, in Queens-
land, used to occasionally turn up in more or less
civilised parts wearing in his whiskers a meer-
schaum pipe of his own manufacture; and a
would-be Sydney manufacturer has begged the
Charters Towers Mining and Pastoral Associa-
tion to try to find out where that meerschaum
came from.
In view of the movement in favor of a
Federal progressive land tax, it is interesting to
recall the fact that in 1840, when S. Aus. —then a
Crown colony—got into financial difficulties and
tried to borrow £120,000 from the British Govern-
ment, the commission which considered the state
of the colony’s affairs made this suggestion among
others:—
Another measure which we should be very desirous to
see adopted in the colony is the imposition of a land-tax,
the produce of which would assist in rendering the income
completely equal to the expenditure, and dispense with the
costly and improvident plan of continued resort to loans.
It is a species of tax, we maj' observe, which has always
the further advantage of tending to prevent the accumu-
lation in private hands of large and unprofitable tracts of
waste lands.
Gladstone was one of the members of this com-
mission. Unfortunately, the good advice was not
adopted, or the public debt of S. Aus. would not
now be so colossal.
With the aid of wireless telegraphy, the
numerous islands with which Provi-
dence has sprinkled the Pacific may
become an additional strength to this
continent. In the event of war, a
fleet of fast vessels, fitted for wireless
work and acting in conjunction with
the telegraphic land stations, could
maintain a fairly effective screen,
through which it would be difficult
for a hostile fleet of any size to pass
unnoticed. Of course, in the present
condition of Australian defence, the
intelligence that the enemy was steam-
ing like Qheol for Sydney Heads would
be next to useless, for the hired iron-
mongery that litters the harbor what
time it isn’t pottering round the South
Seas and being laughed at by canni-
bals, could be finished by a very
moderate fighting ship in one bite.
Still, it might give us time to nail up a
few decorations and arrange a State
banquet for the conquerors.
The Kidston Government ought to get to
holts with the present distribution of the As-
sembly seats in Bananaland. The position is now
a scandal. Bulloo, which is foolish enough to re-
turn “ Janny ” Leahy, can only muster 975 elec-
tors, while Charters Towers has to be content
with two members for 10,977. Talk about one
man one vote !
The evil that befalls a tariff-smitten
country is still increasing Australia’s burden of
ruin. A company with a capital of £40,000
is being formed to turn wool into flannel and
blankets at Warrnambool (Vic.). It proposes to
put £19,000 into machinery, which will
furnish an annual output of 600,000yds.
of flannel and 12,500 pairs of blankets.
Even that part of Warrnambool which
won’t get a job in the factory will be
able to sell some of its spuds on the spot
instead of having to pay heavy freight to
feed them into foreign mouths.
Ex-Senator Downer, K.C., has been weep-
ing in public again. It was when he moved in
the S. Aus. Legislative Council against the awful
proposal of the Price Government that Australians
should be appointed to the superfluous job of
State Governor. The horror of it stirred up all
his emotions, and he blubbered out his peroration
in a succession of patriotic sobs. It was called to
mind that when he went to England in 1887 to
attend the late Queen’s jubilee, he wept copiously
while delivering a loyal oration at the big official
banquet.
PREMIER BENT, OF VICTORIA, IN HIS
NEW CLOTHES.
Photo. —The Ana Studio, Auburn, Victoria.
Murray, of Warrnambool, recently made
repeated requests in the Victorian Assembly that
the Income Tax Bill should be postponed till
after the Budget. It was a fair demand, but it
didn’t suit Adolphus at all ; for he couldn’t
simultaneously pose as a poor Treasurer who
badly needed the income-tax to help him along,
and a Heaven-born financier who had created a
huge surplus out of nothing. So it was necessary
to silence Murray. Did Bent bellow or roar or
swear, or promise impossible things ? Not quite !
He just blurted out—“ Now, wasn’t I good.to the
Warrnambool Hospital?” It was Bent’s idea of
political argument put into a sentence.
It is most unfortunate for Captain Jacky
Evans. Premier of Tasmania, that he is in the
shipping business. A while ago an inter-State
conference of Ministers of Agriculture was held in
Sydney, when it was agreed that inter-State ship-
ments of fruit, etc., should be inspected at the
port of dispatch; but the importing State was also
given the right of inspection—not necessarily for
publication but as a guarantee of good faith.
Presently trouble arose in Melbourne over some
Tasmanian shipments, and Victoria told Tassy to
send across and see for itself what sort of inspec-
tion was wanted. Though it wasn’t in his depart-
ment, Capt. Jacky went himself, and fixed up an
agreement for the inspection of Tasmanian fruit in
Melbourne instead of in Hobart. Of course, in the
event of the fruit being condemned (as thousands
of cases are) the shipper has to pay freight, but ap-
parently, though he is in the shipping business
himself, a little thing like that never occurred to
Capt. Evans. Yet some people, who don’t
know how impossible it is for a man to remember
every little thing, are making really nasty remarks
about it.

“H. Phelthatt”: Ever notice how the
English novelists instinctively tell you that Free-
trade England is no place to make money in ?
They all have three axioms: (1) Every novel
must have a hero ; (2) every hero must make a
fortune ; (3) every fortune must be made out of
England.

again without a bottle of Chamberlain s Colic,
G. Reid characteristically wishes to throw
the burden of Australia’s Defence, not on the
youth who is just out of the kid stage, but on the
very young kid. A compulsory cadet system is
his idea. Since many think that there is “some-'
thing in ” the cadet idea, it might be pointed out
that young boys cannot be trained so as to be good
adult soldiers ; and if they could, the interference
with their general education necessitated would
be so serious as to make the youngster a poorly
equipped citizen, even though a well-trained
soldier.
The recent progress of Freetrade England,
from figures published in the Pall Mall Gazette
(London): —
(1) The net emigration of people of British birth
meant a loss to Britain of 168,000 people in 1900. In 1907
the loss was up to 375,000. (N.B. —Germany used to lose
in this fashion also in its Freetrade days. It has lately
been gaining 22,000 people, of its own nationality, a year,
because Germans are returning to the Fatherland faster
than they leave it.)
(2) The number of paupers relieved in the British Isles
during July 1, 1900, was 761,000 ; on July 1, 1907, it was
868,000.
(3) In 1900, out of every 1000 British tvades unionists
29 were unemployed. In 1908 the unemployed got up to
90 per 1000. These are unionists only—men with recog-
nised trades and mostly the best men in these trades,
therefore the class which suffers least from scarcity of
work.
No wonder the tariff reform movement is spread-
ing fast and the old Cobden joss begins to totter
on its feet of clay.
Sydney Telegraph (which gets the sort
Of letters it wants) has had a note from one
Courtenay Smith, the most terribly frightened
man in Australia. Says Prophet Smith :
It. is said on good authority that if Germany can obtain
control of the North Sea for only two or three days,
London would be at her mercy, and the British navy a
magnificent spectacle of useless ships. Australia would
be the price of peace.
Mr. Smith wants to know of what use, in such an
event, an Australian navy would be. Where
fore :
The only real protection for this great Commonwealth
is to give Thomas Bent’s ten millions to the Admiralty,
and send another two millions as an annual subsidy.
Smith’s notion apparently is that Australia ought
to add a trifle to the “magnificent spectacle of
useless ships” in the North Sea. But to a man
who isn’t Smith it would seem a reasonable thing
that, if British ships are a mere spectacle, the
people who pay for the spectacle might at least
enjoy the spectacle. Also, if Australia is what
Germany really wants —it is to be “the
price of peace ” then it also seems a
fair thing to give Australia some protection.
It’s all very well for the police to watch the pro-
fessional burglars day and night, but, just in case
of accident —just in case one of the burglars gives
his watchers the slip—the Bank of N. S. Wales
never leaves more than half-a-million lying around
loose on the counters over-night. And Russell
French nearly always shuts the front door before
he quits for the Union Club.
A SITE FOR SORE SYDNEYITES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Throw up your hats and cheer for Canberra, the pro-
posed site for the Federal capital !— E. W. O’Sullivan,
in Sydney Daily Telegraph ( VJIO/’OS ).
The Yass-Canberra site consists of undulating downs, in
places fairly heavily-timbered, but for the most part devoid
of trees.... The equable climate favors also the growth
of all garden plants, even under the present disadvantage
of a want of irrigation.. .. Many permanent creeks inter-
sect the territory... . Not far from the weir site some fine
caves exist, which add greatly to the other attractions of
the district. ... A great point in favor of the Yass-Can-
berra area is its accessibility. It is easily reached from
Sydney. —Sydney Telegraph rapturously (same morning).
Give gladness vent;
Be joyful; cheer and yell, plug hat in hand ;
For Ma has got her sop. Strike up the band !
Spread the astounding tidings through the land—
A Fed’ral capital at her command
Is imminent.
How fair it is !
This site whose miles and miles of arid leas,
Dull brown or grey, extend the eye to please ;
While (crowning glory !) on all sides one sees
A splendid absence of encumbering trees —
A grand point this !
No summer rain
To speak of damps these parts, which is a boon :
Damp and pneumonia march in pairs. Full soon
With rain M.P.’s would wilt and die. The loon
Who sought wet here might seek for many a moon,
Yet seek in vain.
Dryness is so
Delightfully persistent in this spot
That he who dares to make a garden plot
Of cabbages, spuds, carrots or what not,
And does not own a dam, loses the lot,
And soon must go.
True, there are creeks:
But, small or dry, and far between and few ;
No mountain torrents here to spoil the view.
Did ever Nature with such charms endue
A promised land ! Full-handed, here she threw
All that one seeks.
Why, even caves!
And when he’s finished talking of the plains,
And of their treelessness, and of the rains
That come not, for the local still remains
One glory : swift escape by frequent trains—
And so he raves.
Which ravings fall
Right gratefully upon the State Fright ear ;
The harbor dweller’s simply charmed to hear
Praise of this site. The fact that Sydney’s near
Condones its faults —in fact, makes faults appear
Prime virtues all.
x.s.w.
J.P.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, October 13, 1908.
My Dear Arini,—
Bitter is the disappointment amongst people
who gossip over the untimely finish of the Big-
wood-Salvin entertainment. During its all-too-
buef run it afforded material for the most amazing
crop of yarns. Travelled folk appeared on all
sides and asserted that they had known Bigwood
in England, in Central Africa, in Hindustan, and
in Greenland, and that in these far spots he was
not Percy Plantagenet 8., but, variously, Sam
Biggs, Bill Woods, and occasionally Joe Bloggs.
1 ercy Plantagenet they alleged to be a nom de
theatre a name, to wit, adopted for the allure-
ment of the notoriously guileless theatre-maiden.
Other raconteurs claimed to possess the full
strength of Carrie’s attitude in the matter.
bhe , was represented to be madly in love
with her Percy. Thrilling tales were told of
how she acted to him nightly, and of how
Replied from a box with gestures of adoration.
Winch allegations were countered by others to the
effect that the star soubrette was considerably
riled with her enslaved, partly owing to the sul-
triness of his past, partly to his crass maladroit-
ness in allowing so much of its torrid warmth to
become diffused about the atmosphere. Also it
was asserted that Carrie had served a local news-
paper with a libel writ for £5OOO ; that she was
going to leave the stage and settle down with her
entire family at Plantagenet Manor, the English
abode of the Bigwoods; that the Bigwoods do
not own a manor and never did, but are poor, yet
meticulously honest umbrella-repairers in Holborn
Viaduct, London, E.C. ; that Carrie’s original
swain was on his way out with a loaded Gatling,
tor use upon Plantagenet, amongst his luggage;
that he approved of the match, had no grievance
against Percy, but regards him as a pal, and
k° ;Tu th - L iey are all demonstrable lies, doubtless,
but their recital gave many a worthy citizen inno-
cent pleasure ; so it is to be deeply regretted that
a couple of attorneys have seen fit to slit Dame
Rumor s weazand, as it were, by a sordid mone-
tary settlement just as the lady was warming up
and putting in some good work.
Wasn’t it Marcus Aurelius who said “For
gettm a.ll the joy that’s coinin’ to vou there’s
nothin like bein’ a mixer. The man who travels
one class all the time misses a lot.” If it wasn’t
the Roman potentate it was Tommy Burns who
said it, and Bridegroom Bigwood who acts it.
Even nis front names denote a parental desire to
mix things.
Just when the parties were busy oiling them-
selves for the fray, Mr. Bigwood decided that he
wanted to go back to England quickly, so lawyers
Carroll and Deery got together, arid Friday’s
evening paper bills screeched that the trouble
had been settled. Miss Salvin (lier name turns
out to be Salvin, not Silva) left the Hotel Mansion
as soon as the white light that beats upon the
plaintiff in a breach-of-promise action had located
her, ana departed for an unannounced address.
Her published portrait puts Carrie, in the matter
of looks, into a seat in the basement amongst the
cobwebs. If it be a good picture, she is a very
pretty young woman.
But there is no liar like the camera, and the
description your friends give you of the person
you haven t seen is often as unreliable as the
camera. Bigwood, as seen at Wednesday’s races
with Carrie (I had not seen him till then), was just
Bigwood not a Percy or a Plantaganet. There
was no more “distinction” about his exteriority
than there is about the average young lorry-driver
or horse-trainer. It may have been the native
worth, but it was not the personal beauty of
1.P.8. that lured the Merry Widow into matri-
mony.
Lord Dudley promises to be thejmost chased
and erawled-after G.-G. that has yet visited these
shores. At the races last week he was pilpably
keen on the running, yet wherever he went male
persons who were nuisances and bores, unless my
physiognomy lore is at fault, trotted doggedly
after him and barked observations at the back of
his neck, to which he hearkened with the strained
smile and the far-away eye of one who is inwardly
muttering the word that is spelt with a dash,
borne of the offenders were racing men them-
selves, and so should have known better. But
the majority were of the class that goes to Rand-
wick in support of its social position. A Society
for the Prevention of Grovelling in Public Places
is a requirement these days. On its formation I
'bhft.ll forward a list of names against whose owners
it is desirable that proceedings should be taken.
Ireland is well represented on the new Governor-
General’s staff. Captain Hore-Ruthven’s ances-
tral home is m co. Wexford. His wife, whom
he wedded only a few weeks before leaving for
Australia, is an Irish girl. She is on her way to
this country with Lady Dudley. Mr. Callan, the
secretary, is Irish, and possesses a brother who is
a priest. Dill is at present abroad, but if he does
not on his return have a word or two to say anent
this glut of Hibermanism in high places he will
disappoint many of his faithful followers.
At her third concert Crossley reminded of a big
glowing chrysanthemum—the kind that goes
through all the richer shades of brown and
merges into gold. A glittering brown haze' over
an underskirt of glimmering brown, gold and
bugle decorations, and silk embroidered storks in
shades of amber and brown ; a stork on the nape
of the Crossley neck—but this gown beats descrip-
tion and is worth going through a thunderstorm
Se f\ , even lf Ada singing inside
it! Adas consort, Dr. Muecke, isn’t a bit
the kind of man who walks in the shadow
of his wife s glory. He has a well-lighted
way of his own running parallel with hers.
Consultant physicians speak most respectfully of
his attainments and of hi§» originality when they
foregather with him at dinner and' drop poly-
syllables in the soup. ‘ 1 J
• \ he h° use . that Dr. Muecke came out of
is heayffy gilded. Muecke, sen., is acknow-
fVrmd, “ 16a f °» • tn ® . blg and prosperous
German colony in S. Australia. He is a mer-
chant and director of nearly all the fattest things
in those parts-the Bank of Adelaide, the Ade-
laide Steamship Co., and the Broken Hill Pro-
prietary mine. The original Australian Muecke
was a doctor, too—a philosophical old gentleman,
a triend of von Mueller’s, who left Germany in its
troubled times, 60 years ago.
The Girls’ Realm Guild put a running knot
around Ada Crossley last week, and, drawing it
tight, trailed her to Baumann’s ballroom, where
she was given tea and handed bunches of music
and admiration, and made a member of the Guild.
■Later she went on to the Wentworth Hotel, where
Mrs. Danvers held a tea-revel in her honor.
“ Louise Dale,” the other half of the Hamilton
Larle brace, would have come along with the
Crossley co. to lift her soprano in song if it had
not been for an eight-months-old infant. The co.
coulan t use baby’s baritone, and Ma would not
come without it. Mr. John Harrison, like Earle,
is a fairly recent bridegroom. He heard by cable
since the tour started of the arrival of a little tenor,
who is very exacting in his demands for lullabies.
Crossley co. left last night for Melbourne, after
a crammed matinee. Because the beknighted
-bent intends to make a speech at the railway
station, the men of the co. will have to arrive in
the southern city in frock-coats and top-hats.
Miss Katharine Goodson has given Melbourne
a sample of her hand on the piano, and next
k.aturday night, at the Town Hall, she will do the
like kindness by Sydney. A modern classical
programme and a special orchestra 70 strong will
assist. n
And talking of music: a cluster of Sydney
meiomaniacs are determined that a Musical Festi-
val is necessary for the education of this land,
twenty citizens of note who have music in their
souls (as well as money in their bank) have
donated £2O each towards its inauguration. It
will be one whole week of music—a debauch of
melody—in which nine concerts will be enshrined.
Promoters hope to have a crowded week of the
kind every three years or so.
Preparation for the Edith and Yera Dickerson
benefit concert is swelling visibly. The two gifted
youngsters, as well as the first-rate assistance
they will have, are deserving of a record audience.
Sydney is to have its “ Messiah,” after all. The
mental fur of the Philharmonic was stroked the
wrong way by Sykes, who slipped in with a
higher tender for the Town Hall; but, prowling
around to find a way out, the Philharmonics
decided on giving the oratorio in the afternoon
instead of at night. Numbers of devout persons in
this Statenever goto any musical festival but “The
Messiah. 1 hey would consider that they were out
of training for the choir invisible if debarred from
listening to “The Lord Omnipotent Reigneth ”
and saying for the manyth time “ Isn’t it lovely!”
Hazon had such a horror of this Handel master-
piece, through sheer weariness of conducting it
®h j he use d to say it had made him an unbeliever
and he took such liberties with the composition

Eighty men and women, who have paid £5OO in
passage money, fell upon Sydney last week, en
route from Brisbane to Mel bourne, to take part in
the Ballarat South-street musical competitions.
I hey swamped the whole of the saloon accommo-
tion (at least, no one but the meiomaniacs
was travelling on the Marloo that trip), so the
competition mania has struck them heavily. They
are the Ipswich Cambrian Society, and the
feminine members are to be guests of Ballarat
citizenesses whilst the musical rivalry rages.
. Sa ' v Tommy Burns last week. He came into
the Cafe Frangais, for lunch, with the Little Bov
from Manly. His solidity of figure, the Napo-
leonism of the upper part of his face, and the sug-
gestion of his jaw being of concrete, was borne
upon every diner present.
The Glaciarium has closed—a fact that will
possibly put Excellencette Rawson into lialf-
mourning of the mind. She enjoyed her skating
more than dancing or any other form of amuse-
ment-enjoyed it even more than her opening of
sales of work or canary shows, or even the inau-
guration of Pure Milk depdts. Miss Madge
Broomfield, who was so shockingly burned through
the ignition of her fancy dress—a cotton wool
vesture intended for the final carnival on the ice—
is recovering.
“Gentle spring in sunshine clad” beamed
upon some persons who had clad themselves
uncommonly at the races. One lady, who makes
it a great appropriation to her own good
parts (to quote Portia’s cumbrous phrase)
that she designs her own frocks, explained that
‘ this one I have on was suggested to me by a
Jive crayfish or lobster as he lay clawing the atmo-
sphere outside a fish shop.” The colors on a live
lobster are as beautiful as those of the darker sorts
of chrysanthemums, ranging from pale yellow to
the brown of an old saddle. So the gown was
artistic and up-to-date in its coloring. The cray-
fish certainly has the fashionable shading—when
he’s of the quick. When of the dead of course
he s scarlet.
One of the most richly-dressed women of the race
week was Mrs. Barney Allen, whose green silk
took a golden glint when she moved. The gold in
the silk was emphasised by gold net and fringe
decorations and wide borders of what looked like
sweet-pea embroidered in grey silk.
“Dejected Widow” Moore’s parasol deserves a
line. It was of light hue, with a deep border of
gondolas in beautiful bright colors. Rose pink
gondolas gliding round and round the rim of a
sunshade in peacock-blue and grass-green water
this is not an everyday vision.
Another novelty, worn by a very slight woman
in an embroidered linen, was a belt (it seemed to
be gold elastic something) with two little flat
pockets or purses, one on either side of the belt-
buckle. She carried her watch in one, and in the
other her money and the return tram ticket.
Mrs. Lady-o -war Napier was, according to a
recent frock-report, gowned in “gun-metal grey ”
It seems a very suitable phrase with which to
describe the frock of a lady wishing to do honor
to the complexion of a cruiser such as the Gib-
raltar. And it exactly describes the color of a
vesture worn by Miss Young in the Dejected
Widow.
The Sam Hordern (senior), with the pretty young
widowed daughter, Mrs. Gaden, will be back in
Sydney early in December.
Mrs Prendergast, a friend and contemporary of
Mrs. Burdekm, Mrs. “ Ted ” Simpson, and other
smart and handsome Sydneyites, is to be re-
married. Her selection is one of the great Vaughan
clan, and his front name is Reginald. Mrs.
Prendergast is the good “Roman” who is essential
in a Vaughan orange-blossoming, and she is, to boot
a woman of wealth Mr. Vaughan is likewise pos-
sessed of much cash, and he has, in addition, nine
Vaughanlettes by a previous marriage. The ladv
has a grown-up son, with whom she visited these
parts half-a-dozen years ago, since when she
has been living in England.
A Commonwealth Conference of Women has
un *? 4l brood u P° n w hat Light-of-Asia Arnold
called the empty eggs of thought,” at St.
J ames Hall. Delegates from various States, 130
strong, with Miss Grace Watson, of Queensland,
holding the ribbons—no, this won’t do at all.
I made made them hens, and now I suggest
that they are ponies. Anyhow, thev have begun
to discuss great questions in relation to God, the
Empire, and the proper feeding of infants. As
the spring lock shuts down on this letter just
when the proceedings open, further enlightenment
is delayed.

The cold, sensible Briton, who has been famed
tor more years than any well-brought-up female
likes to think about for the wearing of flat-heeled
shoes and flannel next the skin, and the consequent
possession of all the’level-headed virtues, seems to
be degenerating some. He (and she) grows
lamentably superstitious. A periodical, The
Mystic, is now actually published in sombre old
Eondon which tells the female Briton what to
wear to prevent the Signs of the Zodiac dropping
on her as she walks down the street. It adver-
tises charms that have been “specially designed
under the best influences of the metals of the
Eenehc Planets.” And here is one of its dress
advertisements:—
Florence Hall,
68 South St., Chichester,
In response to the wishes of a large clientele, has opened
a unique Salon of Millinery for the creation and
supply of
Symbolic Hats and Bonnets,
varying in price from one to five guineas.
Picturesquely made up from Psychic Observation and
Occult Knowledge.
Symbolic Motor Hats,
A Speciality. Half Guinea.
Some useful hints are supplied to females on the
edge of the matrimonial swamp. For instance :
-Nd weeping should take place before the ceremony.
(Mark the cynical italics !)
The underwear should have pale-blue ribbons.
The garters should be white, with pale-blue rosettes in
which a red rosebud lurks.
The lace of underwear or embroideries should always be
emblematic of roses.
The bride should be careful to put her right foot out of
bed first on the wedding morning.
To ensure the bride’s good fortune, she shall enter the
house after the ceremony under two drawn swords placed
in the manner of a St. Andrew’s cross.
It is unlucky for a bride not to shed a tear on parting
with her people.
“ Mario ” from Brisbane :
Brisbane’s bustling Mayor is having no end of trouble
in getting-the apple of his eye to blossom—said apple (to
tear my beautiful simile up by the root) being a town
hall. W e all want a town hall, and have been wanting it
for vears. Long* agfo, in the stone age, designs were
acquired and tenders called. But somehow the matter
slipped the memory of the “fathers” of the day, and
ultimately the tenderers died without getting a reply to
their letters. The same thing happened in' the steel age.
JNow the Mayor says that he will not have further pro-
crastination, but will go right ahead ; and in his bustling
way he is alleged to have overlooked certain regulations
and hurt the feelings of certain aldermen who ventured
to remind him of their existence. .Now, even aldermen
are human, wherefore they are kicking hard, not against
the building of the hall, but really against the Mayor’s
methods, and law and Costs are beginning to be talked
about. Should the law be against his Worship there is
likely to be chaos; and if in his favor, more chaos. Yet
all the trouble could probably be ended if the Mayor
admitted that he had rushed things, and asked for every-
body’s assistance to get the temple up.
His pale and interesting Ex. opened our Art Show in a
grey flannel suit and an air of resolution. He told the
students responsible for the weird anatomical studies to
consider the lilies, so to speak, and to go and sin no more.
Then he applauded Premier Kidston’s idea of sending
pictures round the country towns, but he thought an in-
structor ought to have gone with them to point out their
various points. Godfrey Rivers’ announcement that he
was retiring from the presidentship of the Society was
received with a silence that you could have leaned
against. The fact of the matter is that, rightly or
wrongly, he has been blamed for the row that gave birth
to the new Society of Artists—a break-away that left the
Q.A.S. poor indeed. Brisbane isn’t big enough yet for
two societies, so, not forgetting for a moment the good
work that Godfrey Rivers has done here, there is a feeling
that over his dead presidential body a more sympathetic
man might join the hands of the old andthe new.
Standing out against a background of commonplace
(and worse, as far as oils are concerned) is the study of
Coolangatta Bay, by the sunny-sroiled Percy Grainger
who can get more real sun into his waves than anyone in
Queensland. The champion water-color is also a N.S.W.
subject. Just a bit of the Illawarra coast; just a bit of
beach—but it lives. ■
Miss Ada Wiggs Dwyer, of the Cabbage Patch, has
hit the Brisbane public “right there,” and has been
accorded a tearful send-off after a record season. The
Hazy lady and the study of elongated stripes have also
been strongly urged to come again, and the Stubbins
drunk makes the waybacks green with envy. Ada Cabbage
Patch has been collecting black opals, making friends all
round, and (she says) eating far too much pineapple.
Isn’t the sublime confidence of youth a joy to behold ?
And when it is combined with curlv hair and an artistic
turn of mind, it’s just irresistible. The story goes here that
a critic paid a visit to the studio of a very young artist,
and remarked upon the work, “ You follow that soft
Dutch, school, don’t you ? I like it.” Dimples smiled
pityingly. “Oh no!” he said, “mine are very like
Turner’s.”
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.

The pig-tailed gentleman who leaves his
Joss-ridden land with the idea of disposing of
the “ spling callot ” at the kitchen door of Maori-
land is to be severely mauled by the M.L. Cus-
toms Department on and after October 23. From
that date onward all Chinese who wish to enter
Maoriland will have to read any piece of English
print selected at the discretion of the Customs
officers. Said officials have been specially in-
structed to see that the law is fulfilled to the
letter, and it is believed that as a result the
Celestial inflow will dwindle greatly. In fact it is
hoped that 50 years will see the Chow counted
right out in Maoriland.

and Janies Adam. 106 Magnificent Plates
(22in. x 17in.). Published in 8 Parts, £lO/10/- the
set: Sale price, £4/4/-. (Carriage Forward;)
(Apart from its historical and artistic interest as a
very representative record of the drawings and
designs of these celebrated architects, this work
possesses great practical suggestiveness, and should
find a place on the shelves of every modern architect
who aims at any approximation to completeness in
his library.)
An Artist’s Reminiscences. By Walter Crane.
Published, 18/- net; Sale Price, 7/6. (Postage 1/-)
(Mr. Walter Crane, who was apprenticed to Mr.
W. J. Linton, the engraver, in 1858, has been familiar
with the artistic life of London for half-a-century.
Reproduction of his own drawings add to the charm
of a pleasant gossiping book of memories.)
A Cerman Pompadour (Wilhelmine von Gravenitz).
By Marie Hay. Published, 12/6 net; Sale Price,
3/-. (Postage, Bd.)
(The “love story ” of Duke Eberhard Ludwig, of
Wurtemberg—“ the long-drawn episode which made
his name a target for the gossip and scandal of early
eighteenth century Germany.” The writer’s plan is to
work up the documents into a narrative treated in
the style of a novel; and this is done with literary
skill.).

George Paston. Published, 15/- net; Sale Price,
4/3. (Postage, lOd.)

who lived in interesting times and wrote most inter-
esting letters.)
Queen and Cardinal. By Mrs. Colquhoun Grant.
Published, 12/- net; Sale Price, 4/-. (Postage, Bd.)
(The romantic attachment between Anne of Austria
and Cardinal Mazarin is here detailed with a full

The History of “Punch.”* By M. H. Spielmann.
Published, 16/- ; Sale Price, cloth, 3/6 ; half-Mo-
rocco, 6/3. (Postage 4/-)
(In the construction of this veritable history Mr.
Spielmann took infinite pains. “During- the four
years,” he says in his preface, “in which I have been
engaged upon this book, my correspondents have
been numbered by hundreds. Hardly a man living
whom I suspected of having worked for Punch but I
have communicated with him ; scarce hut one has
afforded all the information within his knowledge in
response to my application.” The result, as might
be expected, is a book full of good things and enriched
with numerous portraits, facsimiles of autographs,
and other curiosities of literature.)
Oxford at the Cross Roads.* By Percy Gardner.
Published, 2/6 net; Sale Price, sd. (Postage, 4d.)
(A discussion of educational ideas and the Oxford
system. Professor Gardner deals with the advent of
the Rhodes scholars, and the persistent refusal of the
Board of Litteras Humananiores to give classical
archaeology a place in the filial examination.)
Annals of Covent Garden Theatre from 1732 to
1897. By H. Saxe Wyndham. 2 Vols. Pub-
lished 21/- net; Sale Price, 3/3. (Postage, 1/8.)
(This famous playhouse has had a chequered
history. John Rich, who built it in 1732, started
with pantomimes. Then came Handel’s oratorios.
Sheridan and Goldsmith followed, with the Kembles,
Macready and Garrick on the boards. Many further
changes occurred, including an interlude of perform-
ing elephants, before it became the recognised home
of grand opera. Mr. Wyndham recounts its history
in detail with many entertaining anecdotes.)

The MOST DESIRABLE TYRE
Reliability, durability and efficiency may be considered
as going hand in hand as the first considerations in the
manufacture cf the most desirable Motor Tyres—and
in no tyres will this trinity of features be found more
pronounced than in the ever-popular Continental variety.
The Continental Co.’s interpretation of the word
“reliability” is “ability to get there,” and not only
to get there, but to get there well; to be certain at the
commencement of a journey that the end is in sight.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, October 12, 1908.
My Dear Yarrie,—
Carrie Moore’s mamma is very angry with her
star offspring. Mamma was looking forward to
the arrival of future son-in-law Proctor with some
anxiety. (What devoted parent would not ? He
has £30,000 a year !) She had pictured Carrie
developing into a rich matron without delay, and
Hinging afar all frivolous footlighted ambition.
Mrs. Moore was quite enthusiastic in her first-
voiced objections to Mr. Proctor’s successful rival,
and her sympathy for the lady who missed him.
Miss Salvin s side of the case has its tragic
features. A quiet and dainty little woman—
younger even than the volatile Carrie—she has
quite a number of accomplishments. Amongst
other things, she knows enough about horses and
clogs to judge at an agricultural show. The lady
is somewhat frail in health, worse luck, though.
Herself she says nothing that is not forced from
Her at the point of a reporter’s pencil. The word
Higwood henceforth is dropped from her voca-
bulary, which proves the girl to be something of a
plucky one. For she won’t be easily able to forget
the years she spent in the soft radiance of Percy
iJantagenets smiles; the remembrances of that
time will live with her.
Caulfield revealed nothing startling on Satur-
day , for the weather clerk was bad-tempered in
the morning. New chiffons were, therefore,
popped back in the boxes, and out came the old
tween-seasons garb for another airing. A hat or
two of cartwheel dimensions caused some bad
language, but there were none of the nerve-shock-
ing startlers we read about in English papers.
Ihe most startling things were Mrs. Les-
lie Macdonald’s grass-green stockings. They
matched her hat of emerald hue, and this
capped a perfectly ripping dark-blue face-cloth
coat and skirt of a beautiful shade, and
cut in the latest mode, with black silk braid galore
to set it off. The G.-G. was there, but no other
viee-regals. With the G.-G. were the members
of his very correctly turned-out staff, all showing
a decided waist. There was quite a family bunch
of Maddens, of whom, on this occasion, Beatrice
was the swell. She aired a striking Empire frock
of a deep raspberry hue, with much oriental silk
embroidery on white all about the shoulders and
meandering a bit down the arms and back
Ihe youngest Cliveden hope, Miss Ivy, sported
like Mrs. Ernest Raleigh) one of those sat-upon
looking hats, hers being black, wreathed with
pink roses. She was a very particular swell in
her bridesmaid frock, a thing of cream with a
clash of pale pink underneath, and a sparkle of
silver or crystal—perhaps both—about the shoul-
ders which suggested chain-mail. From the pink
sash of several tones dangled crystal and silver
tassels. One of the Baillieu boys, who are
steadily climbing since they blossomed forth at
the Glacianum, was in devoted attendance.
Considering it was race week in Sydney
Sassiety was very well represented. For, during
Sydney race week, it is the thing here not to be
If you ,f re , not , ln Sydney, you ought
to be. In all the bunch that was there
tliere was nothing to cause a thrill, ex-
cept the wonderful ermine coat and muff—the
l ,' l r ttei ; T /' L : arl y as big as herself-worn by
Mrs. W. Finlay. The Grices, and Mrs. UrquharJ
the Ihomas laits, Mrs. Dunbar Hooper, Mrs
George Paxton, Mrs. Tom Smith and one of her
daughters Miss Muriel Gumming, the Fred
Paynes, the Frank Madden family, also the
Bowes Kellys, the George Fairbairns, Mrs. Bal-
main and Mrs. R. Casey were a few representa-
tives noted—although it is a dreadful thing to
mention all of them in one paragraph. Mrs.
Casey was one of the best-dressed women in a
mushroom-tinted marquisette and lace dress and
.nat all en suite , as fashion journals say.
Among other things of note was Mrs. Guv
Sherwood s limp. She was a fashion study in a
tailor-built nattier blue cloth, black braid and
silver buttons. Now, is a limp to become fashion-
aT,®’ 1 wonder as it was long ago in the days of
The Alexandra ’? Striped skirts with plain
colored coats were another notable thing. Two of
them were dazzlers, and caused one to wonder if
one by any chance saw double. When the
wearers were in repose they were all right—one a
black t other a blue frock. When the wearers
walked, though, the skirts sprang into pronounced
stripes.
Artist George Lambert and his missus, fired by
the triumph of Brother Streeton, are going to
make a descent upon this country. Thereafter
I s’pose, they’ll return to London laden with cash
cajoled from the few Australian pockets that
aren t guarded by unsesthetic minds. George
wears a Velasquez beard, these days; which is
carrying imitation into the realms of flattery with
a vengeance. But perhaps the chin hair is an
unconscious plagiarism of the gifted Spanish dead
un, whose spook so powerfully influences Lam-
bert s portrait groups.
Reg Carey, who t’other day was a small boy in
knickers, is producing Tittell Brune’s perform-
ances for J.C.W. this season. And the youth is
doing it without a hitch in the curtain or fits in
the wings The Carey boy is son of ponderous,
reliable old George Carey, who struts his hour in
bellow-drama mostly.
They’re a business-like community in Mel-
bourne. After their Fleet spree, they’re bein°- as
dilatory as a bankrupt in paying the piper The
orchestra, which played while Tom Bent slapped
bperry on the back and attempted to talk as
though he were a drummer fresh from Detroit
hadn t been paid when I last heard their loud im’
patmnt wails. Neither had a number of other
people.
There is one man,in Melbourne whose fortune
piles up daily under one’s very eyes—Showman
West. He has been specialising in Picture Shows
for years, and his continuous films grow lon°- e r
and longer at each new venture, and claw out like
octopus tentacles to draw in every subject under
the sun. Since he has annexed Olympia (that
vast barn built to the worship of Circus) the
I ictures have run the gamut, from mere mad
practical jokes to material for world-interest.
After the latest fight, the bruisers were bashing
each other on his screens almost before the
details were exploited in the papers. The
Olympian Games were re-played before the real
subject had dropped from the fickle public mind.
But the news-of-the-moment pictures that flash
freshly into the programme make only an item
Ihere is dustless, smooth travelling through
miles and miles of country and city streets, with
hardly a flicker,to disturb the impressive effect of
grey battlements or grim buildings, or' all the
life and gaiety of places it would take Years and a
fortune to ferret out. Or an industry "is followed
from first to last.
The crowd’s that do the rolling-up are mar-
vellous. Every night the hundreds of seats in the
big circus ring are crowded, and the “really circus”
seats, up in the amphitheatre, are packed with
gapers in the dim light. In the outer horse-ring,
where once the leopard prowled the full length of
his cage, and the lion roared, are tea-tables and
bars for the gentle ginger-pop. It is a wonderful
and unique spectacle, this taming of the crowd !
West has never let up on the real showman
touch—a man at a megaphone shouts the pro-
-BK?SQ?,pieoe - * s rnere Jy an enlarged edition
ot Roll U P. roll up,” which West himself has
shouted to a small show in an exhibition. The
real shovvman is passing, but he is very much
alive while West runs his little earth.
Changes are mooted in the Musgrove-Wren
combination. The present opera co. will proba-
bly be split into two, and the halves will get some
imported additions. One east will be entirely
grand opera, the other comic opera. The
change is likely to give Miss Stella Gastelle, the
gay little soubrette, more freedom. In “ Les
Cloches ’ she was like a butterfly trying to draw
along a fat, stagey Lohengrin. There are men in
that company with excellent voices, but they
move as though they had all the weight of grand-
opera tradition in their boots.
Miss Stella Gastelle is an astronomical point of
particular brightness. She is a most vivacious
sdubrette. To all appearances, she looks on the
Australian trip as a break in the habit of playing
L.t I oupee a habit which has lasted solidly
f or years- Since she opened in “Les Cloches”
in Melbourne she has been in hospital with
a “throat’’-resting for a future sparkle
which the management is cogitating. She is
a piquant little piece of goods, and made
herself most popular with the army of the chorus,
ror her part, she says she has never met such
chorus girls in any part of the world as the Aus-
tralian; their voices, and their life, and their
adaptability, she says, are the most welcome sur-
prise to one who knows that a chorus girl is so
often just a singing machine with movable joints.
Mr. Levilly, the lady’s husband and one-time
manager (she is the manager now), came to Aus-
tralia just to see her off the boat—stayed three
weeks, and then went back to his London and his
clubs. He was manager of the “La Poupee ”
provincial tour, in which Miss Gastelle played
lead. Her Alesia was responsible for the record-
breaking run of the quaint play. She has
played the part so much, from its very inception
that sometimes she forgets whether she is a
mechanical doll or a real live woman.
Miss Gastelle, though English born, studied for
ma ny youthful years in Leipsic and Paris. She
returned for a first London engagement at the
I mice of Wales Theatre, and was an immediate
and continuous success. She has had her successes
m pantomime, too, both in London and at Mr.
Wyndnam s Theatre in Glasgow ; but she prac-
tically created Alesia of “La Poupee,” and that
is her favorite r6le. Brilliant press notices follow
her trail m this like so many meteors. Even The
-Limes waxed almost enthusiastic, and told London
managers that, if “ they thought of reviving the
favorite operas of Offenbach, there was one°ladv
on the. English stage equal to playing and singing
the leading parts as they should be sung and
played—that is Stella Gastelle.”
The Sandringham Golf Tournament has ter-
minated, and there is a lull among excited girls
with stretched faces, and with golf caps on the
back of their wind-tossed hiar. Miss Elsternwick
-Kell carried off the win for the Doubles. The
other girls have flopped down in a heap, and
won t even polish their brassies. Some girls
within my ken were so keen in practising that
they had ■to “chuck” theatres and evening
frivols; they were that cat-tired they went to
bed at half-past eight every night, to be ready for
an early tramp. You’d meet them on a drizzly
grey day, when the atmosphere embraced one
with an icy grip. They’d merely chortle and say,
Glorious day for play—so nice and soft under-
foot !
That frocky Toorakety duet, the Macleod
Girls, usually in full blast at race time in Sydney
or Melbourne or Hobart, must be missed at Rand-
Wick this season. The girls deserted Melbourne
-tSJoek and fashionable cafes some weeks since for
Sydney and the Australia; but thev clipped the
races, and are rusticating at a station on some
wind-swept plain not a far coo-ee from Svdney.
One is clinging hard to the speedy wings of health
after a nervous break up—or,-break down.
The Willie a’Becketts have moved their furni-
ture—solid old mahogany—and their ox and their
aSS i nlu i, r man-servant and their maid-servant,
and I he Baby, from Toorak haunts to the country
hills of Berwick, far enough out for Baby to play
with woody bears, yet near enough to the city for
1 opper a Beckett to keep an eye and a telephone
ear on business interests.
Medico Smith’s clever daughter Louise, who
has been travelling over the byways and high-
ways of England and Scotland, has planted her
baggage at Edinburgh University, and is at the
moment splashing about in a course of Modern
Languages and Phonetics. One evening everv
week the girl-students hold a meeting in the
Royal Arch Hall to talk —not only with the elec-
tric ease of their mother tongue, but in any old
(or new) language they like or dislike. When the
meeting really gets going, the Tower of Babel
may as well scratch itself for all engagements.
Margaret Anglin patronises Callot Soeurs of
Paris, for her gowns. At that place they just fit
linings around one’s fairy form and then wind the
stuff on one. After that they wind up the bill.
Ihere is a legend that it takes two maids to put
Margaret into her frocks.
Margaret Anglin Camilled in splendid frocks
the loveliest, in my idea, being a lace one
divided into panels, with lines of gold thread in it
which veiled a floral chine silk. The richest was
of white satin embroidered with pearls, and inlet
with net panels thickly incrusted with pearl em-
broideries. This was worn in the ballroom scene
with lots of jewels, including a diamond- tiara!
Miss Anglin does away with the village-maid
attire in the cottage scene, and wears a beautiful
white ninon frock inlet with lace and made over
mauve silk. In nighties—but she does not get out
of bed to show theya off ! 6

Fieet week isn’t in it with the coming week of
the medicoes. A medical congress is to happen
adjacent . to the week of Caulfield Cup P and
visiting Sawbones from all the States and Maori
land will crowd into Melbourne with wives and
Alpine miounds of luggage. Then the men will
dine the men, and the wives will frivol into an
outburst of tea and garden party, and the whirl
will be like unto Catherine wheels on Guy Fawkes
Gay. Wait till I take a breath, and listen to this
tor a rough bare-bones advance programme :

From “Fosna,” in Adelaide
As usual, the Hunt Club races drew the champion
frocking to Morphettville. Any frill that has been pre-
Razed U P°” hy man, woman or cat is disqualified
tor this event; and for weeks beforehand the local woman
becomes a fearsomely secretive being who lurks. The
grand piano (which ought to have been given for the very
best frocky outburst, but wasn’t) is hereby divided as
follows keyboard to the graceful young Bymill dame,
who wore a heliotrope empire raiment and black plumed
millinery glory ; case to Mrs. Clem Hill, in pale-blue
chinon over lace, and more plumed millinery ; pedal to
a rothenngham damsel, in a pink glacb empire triumph,
with yoke of Honiton lace, and still more plumed mil-
linery; and the remainder of the instrument can be cut
into slices for Mrs. Jule Wendt (black and white check
costume, with a fetching touch of green about it) and
Mrs. Parsons (gorgeous lace over white glace, with some
thing more than usually intense in the matter of girdles).
The weather had its best clothes and complexion on, ana
the riding was as weird as usual.
The velvet-clad Cherniavski boys were by no means the
least picturesque items on the lawn, and came in for a
large slice of feminine attention. They own to belonging
to a family of nine, all champion noisers, including a baby
who sings. e 3
The youngsters gave five concerts during the week, and
the crowd brought out its encore newly oiled and wound
up, and half-killed the boys with hard work. If every
performer doesn’t respond to every encore every time
Adelaide feels annoyed. For once the populace kept its
senses firmly under its hat, and didn’t cast “ floral offer-
ings ’ at the defenceless boys ; but Madame Hooton came
m for some gorgeous vegetables every night. Their Exes,
took a party along to the first concert, and the Bevans,
Bass6s, and the Mus. Doc. Davies were among the
musical lights that illuminated each performance.
Mr. Dick Nitchske’s burst of melody on the 7th was a
more than usually fine burst, and disclosed the fact that
the Adelaide baritone has done great things with his
warble since he took it to London to be polished. He
was always popular here, and a great house turned up
and gave him a bumper reception. Elsie
piano-stroking, Nora Kyffin Thomas’ fiddling, and Martha
Bruggeman’s soprano warble also gladdened the already
very glad audience ; and each damsel found an encore and
some floral rewards waiting.
Mr. and Mrs. Nitchske have been handing out some
very excellent advice to young* musical career hunters*
the gist of it is, “ Stay in Australia unless you have much
money and more influence.” Without both of these thin ms
engagements are very hard to lasso, and starvation Is
career that most adventurers find in the Big Smoke.
The Nitchskes speak with some authority, too, for thev
have kept in touch with pretty well the whole fraternity of
Australian musical and artistic folk, to whom their house
in London was always hospitably open. Mrs. Nitchske has
gone out and collected more than one sick young fellow-
country-woman, and nursed her back to health. Fore-
most of all the comparatively new immigrants she places
the success of the just-returned Victorian damsel, Ilita
Mope, whose progress she says was quite remarkable.
The P. and O. Co. has come down from its high estate
and will allow its mailboats to tie themselves up to the
Outer Harbor, instead of standing aloof at the ancient and
sometimes boisterous Semaphore anchorage. The Mooltan
was the first to dare it, and the doughty deed was cele-
brated by a luncheon on board. Premier Price W'ent
down and shook the Mooltan by the mast, and patted it
on the binnacie, and said it was a good beast Then
everybody drank someone else’s health and said it was an
event of national importance. Not to be outdone the
N.D.L. boat pushed its nose in on Thursday, and was tied
to the Harbor too.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.

IN THE HOME.
A vast amount of human misery is
endured by thousands of men and
women who are never really well.
Headache, sickness, nervous depres-
sion, dislike of food, sinking sensa-
tions, with distressing dizziness and
weakness, make life a daily misery
to such sufferers. And the cause is
indigestion—a stomach that starves
the body, because it cannot digest
sufficient food to keep it well, and
leaves the mind weak, irritable,
depressed. To euch unhappy ones
Mother Seigel’s Syrup brings a mes-
sage of hope, brings the ready help
to health, brings the quiet, strong
sense of confidence which only
health can give—health re-made
and regained, through sound diges-
tion. Take it daily after meals.
Mr. J. Fitzgibbon, x, Hugo Street, Redfern,
N.S.W., writing on March 18th, 1908, says:
“ For years I suffered most severely from
acute indigestion. Nothing did me any good
until I got Mother Seigel’s Syrup. The
first bottle enabled me to eat and digest,
and soon I was quite cured.”
