Business, Robbery, etc.
Two of the three olive-trees which were the be-
ginning- of the olive-oil industry of S. Australia are still in
bearing ; in fact, they seem to have just begun to find
out what real bearing is. Just on 50 years ago the three
plants were brought out from Prance. At first they were
tried near Adelaide, but didn’t thrive. Then Samuel
Davenport—a fine old colonist, still alive—got permission
to transplant two of them at Burnside. That was 45
years ago. For some seasons past the harvest from them
has been steadily increasing ; and now they have knocked
out all records by giving between them over 12cwt. of
fruit, worth Bs. per cwt. —less 2s. 6d., the cost of picking.
They still belong to the Davenport-Clelana family.
* * * *
Early this year T. A. Rickard, in the Mining
and Scientific Press, made a list of the greatest gold-
mines of the world, with their profit, costs, reserves, etc.
Most of the figures were for 1906. The Esperanza (Mexico)
yielded most gold and profit that year. The output
(bringing Rickard’s figures to sterling) was £1,606,017;
the profit £967,000. In 1907, however, the Goldfield Con-
solidated (Nevada) came first in output and the Robinson
(Transvaal) in profit. There were in 1906 three Rand
mines (Robinson, Simmer and Jack, and Robinson Deep)
which gave each a yield of over $4,000,000 ; in Australasia
there were two (Mt. Morgan and Waihi). In the matter
of profit, however, only the Robinson beat either the
Waihi or the Oroya-Brown Hill (Kalgoorlie).
* * * *
The fact that garments made of red fabrics offer
better protection from the sun than clothing of any
other shade is now generally recognised, and a craze is
setting in for having tropical garments of a gory hue.
The new apparel is colored with scientific accuracy, and
is guaranteed to resist the harmful (i.e., blue or actinic)
rays of the sun, while freely admitting the beneficial
(or non-actinie) ones. The time was when man was con-
tent to accept the sunshine just as Providence poured it
down on him, but nowadays he filters the light of the
world with rags, and only uses as much of it as he thinks
good for him. Some of these days Providence will get
irritated at the amount of knowledge mankind is accumu-
lating, and will send along a second Deluge as a mild
reproof. Then humanity and its impertinent discoveries
will be wiped out, and Ignorance will rage through the
land with mud in its hair.
* * * *
“Itan”: The North Queensland canefields
are pretty “ white” now, but the banana plantations are
as yellow as ever. What is the Australian Parliament
going- to do about the fruit that looks like a sausage?
This banana trade is a big industry, and should be a white
man’s industry ; but Ah Fat and Ho Stink have got it by
the wool, and are holding' on with characteristic tenacity.
The white man, in the present circumstances, cannot suc-
ceed, because the Chow has the trade cornered. He
cornered it while the Tory prevaricator was declaring
some years ago that white men could not work in the
tropics. There are miles of country where not a single
white is employed. Then the product is handled by the
yellow ring of middlemen. It is largely due to this fact
that bananas have been sold at such a big price for the
past few years. When the yellow grower employs any
help he induces the aboriginal to fill the bill, and pays
him in rations—and opium 1 Immature fruit is flung on
the market, because the Chow ring is supreme, and can
command a sale even for that sort of stuff. Another
reason why the Chow picks his immature stuff is that
directly the fruit begins to fill out the fly attacks it. The
State law enjoins that each bunch must be enclosed in a
net, but as there is practically no supervision, the sinful
Chow only envelopes the bunches on the outside of the
rows, and picks the rest green in order to save their lives.
* » * *
A towheaded girl with scraps of faded ribbon
all over her came into the shop, sidled up to the counter,
and said :
“ Baker, ma says to call at our place on Monday ; No.
24 Horse-street.”
“ Yes,” replied the baker, in a non-committal tone, and
the girl went out.
“ A new customer for you ? ” I commented.
“ No. I ain’t going to call,” he said, defiantly. “ I’m
just about sick of pas and mas. For the last two years
I’ve kept a record ; and wherever the kids call the missus
‘ ma,’ there’s a bad time for the butcher and baker. I’ve
only got two ‘ ma ’ families on my list that pay their way.
If the nippers label the old man ‘dad’ and the mother
‘ mum ’ the prospect’s fairly good. Most likely they’re
poor ; but they’re battlers, and don’t waste money in
style. But the most reliable customers are those where
they call them ‘ father ’ and ‘ mother.’ When a youngster
comes to the shop and says, ‘ Mother wants you to call,
baker,’ I’m there bright and early.
“ They’re crafty, too, the pas and mas,” continued the
baker. “ Perhaps they’ll pay cash for a few days. Then
they'll pay weekly. After a while they’ll pay for half a
week and let the other half stand over. Then they get a
week behind, and soon they owe you for a month’s bread.
Then the band starts. You never see ‘ma ’ these times.
The little girl takes the bread and says,‘Pa will call
round at the shop with the money.’ But pa never calls.
Father always calls, and sometimes I see dad ; but pa has
never been in my shop except once, and that was to
threaten to punch me for daring to ask his wife for my
money.
“Ma is generally worse than pa. Pa usually blusters
and threatens ; but ma sometimes uses violence. At one
place they owed me £1 165., which is a lot for a suburban
family. I. tried all sort's of dodges, but never got a
bean. They lived in one of those ramshackle, two-storey,
single-fronted villas —ma mostly lives in a villa. One
morning, when I was standing patiently in the rain, wait-
ing at the door, I heard the window above me opened.
I looked up, and a whole bucketful of slops fell on top of
me. That sickened me of mas, and I fired a blue paper
at her next day.”
41 * * *
“ C.K. Y.” : Sydney mathematical experts have
just proved the truth of Tommy Brusso Burns’ dictum
that there is no money in poultry-ranching. An analysis
of the returns at the recent laying competitions shows
that the profits of the business are not worth talking
about. Even the breeders of champion strains are going
out of the biz. Like the pig, the chook only pays as a
consumer of waste—she is a by-product, not a staple
crop. Hundreds of dotty victims have spent their little
all finding out this simple truth.

“R. Snick ” : As a licensed pharmaceutist, I see
something of the patrician dame with a poodle, dachshund,
or other canine outrage of the sort. I may mention that
I can't see too much of her for my pocket’s sake. This is
the sort of missive I get periodically, to my great joy.
iiogville, , ’OS.
Dear Mr. R. Snick,
My poor Ponto is very sick. Will you come and see
him?
Yours, etc., D. Ixgo.
I call, and am shown an unsavory mongrel in an ad-
vanced state of mange. “Yes, madam,” I say thought-
fully, “ though your dog is in a very serious state, I think
I can cure him, if I have him continuously under my
care. You must let me take him to my canine hospital.”
After a while the lady acquiesces, and I call a cabman,
and order him to transport the hound to his vehicle.
There are a few muttered objurgations, but I jingle the
coins in my pocket, and at the sound Jehu plucks up the
pup and heaves him inside. Arrived home, I tie the bald
bulldog in the yard and feed the invalid on sulphur and
water. In ten days I return him a clean dog. Fee, two
guineas. But overfeeding and want of exercise do their
deadly work, and later on I receive a notification that
“ poor Ponto hasbeeome so, yes, so positively objectionable
that I have most reluctantly consented to his destruction.”
Ponto, hairless, wheezy and odoriferous as the Yarra
during the dog-days, is brought to my shop by the gar-
dener. I insert a drachm of prussic acid into him.
Finis. Fee, one guinea. One guinea, mind ! —for a dog.
Why, I’d poison half-a-dozen men for that.
Alexander Hay, of Coolangatta, will con-
test the New England (N.S. W.) seat next Federal
elections in the somewhat busted cause of Anti-
Sosh. Laborite F. J. Foster roundly beat Lons-
dale there in 1906, and Hay’s chances of doing the
same by Foster are dull. Foster was an unknown
man when he won ; he is now widely known and
liked. The wiped-out Lonsdale had an intimate
acquaintance with the constituency, and what he
didn’t know (for all his congenital foolishness in
other matters) about electioneering tactics wasn’t
worth bothering about. The local Anti-Sosh
organisations were in full bloom when Lonsdale
slumped; to-day they have mostly withered on
the stalk, and the Labor leagues are, contrariwise,
in a flourishing state. Hay contested the Illa-
warra seat (an appreciable section of which
belongs to the Hay family) eight years ago, but
was beaten out of sight by Gr. W. Fuller.
THE RUINED MARINERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When in latitude 20deg. south, a large quantity of
“ grease ” was seen floating on the surface of the ocean,
and, a calm prevailing, th e_men _ man aged to sco©p--up
several bucketfuls of the stuff. The “ grease ” was found
excellent, and it was used for slushing down the masts,
the balance being used by the men on their oilskins and
boots. The captain said that much more could have been
collected, but thought the amount picked up by the men
was enough.
It was not until a few days after the arrival of the
Antrope at San Francisco that the master of the vessel
learned that he had let a fortune slip through his fingers.
A small part of the “grease” had heen saved, and this
was identified by an Oakland druggist as ambergris. The
sailors had greased masts, sea boots and oilskins with
ambergris, valued at approximately £BO a pound, and
thus wasted about £4500 worth of the stuff.— Daily
paper.
Th’ pore but honest skipper wears a mournful sorter look,
An’ th’ first mate 'e ain’t cheerful any more ;
Th’ bosun’s sittin’ readin’from a scientific book,
An’ a-doin’ sums upon th’ galley door.
Justbecos we ’adn’t got a notion
We went an’ left a fortin on th’ sea 1
Left it slidin’ there upon th’ ocean.
An’ all of us as thirsty as could be !
There’s a Dago in th’ port watch sits lookin’ at ’is boots,
An’ a-talkin’ in ’is silly furrin style ;
Yer never can get any sense out of them d d galoots ,
But I know ’e’s goin’ mad about that ile !
Ho ! all f’r th’ want of eddication
We went an’ left a fortin lyin’ there 1
Oh ! don’ we deserve some admiration
An’ twenty pats upon our greasy ’air !
Whenever we goes up aloft we cuss th’ blanky mast,
An’ wonder if some ’eat would dror it out;
Th’ bosun seems t’ think that he may strike some plan at
last,
But ’e’s growin’pale an’ thin. (Once ’e was stout.)
Yus, all f’r th’ want o’ knowin’ better
We went an’ put th’ stuff upon a pole !
I’ll go an’ write me mother ’ome a letter,
An’ tell ’er th’ good news. Lor’ bless me soul!
I never ’ave no peace below—me honest rest I miss,
I don’t think that I ever will sleep sound ;
Me dreams is all of glassy seas and floatin’ ambergris—
Th’ drippin’ that’s worth eighty quid a pound !
Jus’ because we didn’t ’ave a notion,
We went an’ left th’ scum upon th’ sea 1
Left it lyin’ loose upon th’ ocean !
Oh 1 Lord ’a mercy on our poverty 1
Lord ’elp me !
We was lunatics floatin’ on th’ sea I

BEALE Australian Pianos are made in Australia under special systems and with valuable patents which greatly enhance
the musical qualities, as well as increase the durability of the instrument. The systems and practices of European and
American manufacturers are well known to us, and where they are perfectly satisfactory we use them, but in many
instances we have been able to improve on the methods of those makers, and have invented new processes in the manufacture,
and new apparatus in the pianos themselves.
Foreign makers have for many years sought a workable metal wrest-plank, but it remained for Beale & Co. to arrive at the
only really satisfactory solution of the difficulty. With the Beale-Vader patent steel tuning-system, pianos will remain per-
manently in tune after once the wires have fully stretched ; the wrest pins cannot possibly become loose, nor can the
wrest-plank split, and thus allow the instrument to get out of tune. Beale horizontal pianos do not require tuning before each
concert, as other grand pianos do, and the tone also of our instruments is superior, having no metallic ring. Our patented
system of building the cabinet-work completely obviates cracking and v/arping—even in the most extreme climates. Our
hexagonal bass-strings ensure pure tones in the bass, with no possibility of a “jingle.”
We have not enough space to enter into further details, but we shall be glad to send full particulars to anyone interested,
whether an intending buyer or not.

NOTIONS
A. J.C. Spring Show scraps : Third day saw only
56 starters out of 145 carded, and on the last day only 55.
Possibly the fact that the V. A.T.C. meeting- began on the
same day as Randwick fixture closed had something, but
not all, to do with the falling off. Visiting nags gathered
six races only—M.L. five (through Aeolus, Pilot and
Soultline), and Queensland one (through War Star). Nine-
teen winners were trained in N.S.W. Mooltan topped the
prize list ; his haul being £4276 (for three wins) of the
£21,244 offered. Among stallions represented, Positano
(four wins) beat Grafton by one. W. McLachlan had six
winning mounts, and M.L. cross-country rider T. Cahill
was up on Aeolus and Pilot in all tiieir successes. Biggest
local prize winner was “Mr. C. Jeffries,” who called for
£2028, through Parsee’s Derby triumph. Mundoie’s time
(lmin. 38|sec.) for Suburban Handicap Mile on Wednes-
day was but Jsec. slower than Charles Stuart’s Australian
best, and yet he didn’t finish among the first eight in The
Shorts on the previous Monday. Truly the racehorse
problem is extremely difficult. Stated that A.J.O. will
lose on the venture, as takings were much less than for
same meeting last year. Peru’s Randwick Plate (2J miles)
on Saturday occupied 3min. 56fsecs., and lowered Lord
Ullin’s Daughter’s Australian best—4min. 57Jsecs. Maori-
larid’s fastest is 3min. 56 l-ssecs., accomplished by Sea
Horse.
* * •*
M.L. nagu Aeolus and Pilot gathered all four
A.J.O. jumping events, and they were the only two
jumpers sent over to compete. Aeolus evidenced unques-
tioned superiority in both hurdles, showing greater speed
than anything opposed to him, and flew at least as
well as the best of the others. Grafnax threatened
trouble for Pilot in each steeple till the Syd-
neyite mulled the second-last obstacle on the first
day, and the last on the concluding day; but once
there was a difference of 2st. 101 b. in Grafnax’s favor, and
4st. next time. Pilot’s displays were a revelation to
Australian racegoers, who certainly never previously saw
anything better. iloth horses were sold before their
second efforts. Aeolus went into the possession of crack
bowler, billiard-player, and general all-round sport, Mr.
W. Lyons, for £2OO and £5O from the prize ; and Dr. Fred
Watson secured Pilot for 300 gs. Possibly Aeolus will be
given a chance on the flat, and he should succeed if
placed with good judgment.

Q.T.C. still struggles. Latest meeting showed
a loss of £197.
i* * * *
Seemingly the A. J.C. handicapper made no mis-
take in weighting Melodrama as a proved non-stayer.
Last year the son of Grafton and Drama received Bst. 71b.
in the Epsom Handicap, and Bst. 41b. in the Metropolitan.
He won the mile race and finished third for the Metropoli-
tan, the 101 b. penalty bringing his weight up to 9st.
This year he was handicapped at 9st. for the Epsom, and
only Bst. for the longer race. Again he won the Epsom,
and earned a 101 b. penalty for the Metropolitan, but this
time he carried his Sst. iolb. no nearer than seventh at
the finish. Roughly speaking, the difference between
eight furlongs and 12 in the distance makes a difference
of about 211 b. in Melodrama’s “form.”
On the other hand, what a good one is Mooltan
over a distance ! Last year Melodrama gave the Positano
horse 191 b. in the Epsom, and beat him by a head.
Allowing that the light boy failed to get every
possible ounce out of Mooltan, there was no
denying the fact that he had a good run,
and was decisively “pipped” at the finish. In
the Metropolitan, with 291 b. the best of the weights,
Mooltan gave Melodrama a 141 b. beating. This year he
improved immensely on that performance. Carrying
9st. 21b. (or 311 b. more than last year’s burden) he won
easily, whilst Melodrama (Bst. 101 b.) lagged far behind.
And in the meantime he had run second to Apologue for
the Melbourne Cup. The book shows that whereas a bit
of extra weight has made no difference to Mooltan’s
speed, every bit of extra distance over a mile has been in
his favor. With only two exceptions thus far, this real
good horse has finished first or second every time he has
started. If he keeps well until Melbourne Cup day, he
should run first or second for the two-mile prize. True,
9st. 121 b. is a steadier, but it is 71b. less than Carbine
carried four lengths ahead of a big field, and for all that
has been proved to the contrary, Mooltan may have Car-
bine’s capacity for carrying weight.
-<••***
The Y.A.T.C. made a good commencement
with the Cup meeting at Caulfield on Saturday, notwith-
standing that it clashed with the tail end of the Rand-
wick fixture. In so far that the two odds-on favorites
won handsomely, and the most fancied of the youngsters
scored in the Debutant Stakes, the public had nothing to
complain of. Moreover, it got a considerable education
in the relative “form” of the local and visiting nags,
which is likely to be confirmed next Saturday. Take, for
instance, the runaway victory of Soultline in the
Toorak Handicap. At Randwick, a week earlier,
the son of Soult had seemed to fail in the
Epsom for lack of stamina, the mile in lmin. 39Jsec.
being apparently more than he could manage. His
weight for the Toorak Handicap was 171 b. more than he
carried for the Epsom, yet, wdth a field of Victorian
horses against him, a somewhat heavy course, and con-
sequently slower pace conditions, Soultline (with 9st. 11b.
up) was far too good for- the opposition. He stayed out
the mile and finished as though he could have gone a lot
further. Enchanteur, from Westralia, ran a couple
of lengths behind the w'inner, whilst the name
of a dozen local competitors was simply Mud.
Had he only Victorian horses to meet in the;
Caulfield Cup, the 12 furlongs might not be too far
for Soultline, nor is it clear, from Saturday’s showing,
that he is a non-stayer in even the fastest company.
Maybe he was trained a little too fine for the Epsom, and
if so, the interruption of the journey to Melbourne has
benefited the Maorilander. He is a gelding, and geldings
run best w'hen they are not quite “ strung up.”
Of the 2-year-olds, that lined tip for the Debu-
tant Stakes, the best-looking was Sol Green’s Santos, w-ho
ran badly. The colt seemed very short of work, and may
be expected to do better at the V.R.C. meeting.

“0.K.”: In the language of stable boys the
term “ bloke ” is applied indiscriminately to man and
gee-gee. After the Steeplechase at Caulfield last Satur-
day, a mannikin who had been looking at the race
returned to his mate in the birdcage with the news that
“ one bloke broke his neck, and another bloke broke ’is
leg.” The victims were Melinite and Mayo, and the riders
had escaped without injury.
* * * < ¥■ *
A writer in Sydney Daily Telegraph (8/10/’08)
illustrates how badly backers fare at the hands of Ikey
Mo in comparison with their experience with the tote.
He shows how in a field of 12 starters, a horse with 59
bets registered against it would pay (through the machine)
a dividend of 23 to 1. What bookmaker, the tote enthu-
siast asks, would pay such odds? He took a fancy case,
when there was a real-life one at his disposal. In
Adelaide, when the totalisator was first installed there,
an aged farmer put £1 on a horse named D.O.D. The
horse won, and the agriculturist’s dividend was 800 to 1.
What howler of the odds since welshing was first invented
ever laid such a wager as that ? A tragic side of the story
is that the winner failed to hold on to his £BOO. He was
in financial difficulties at the time, and the whole of his
wealth was seized on behalf of his creditors.
* * * *
This season’s football competition in Bendigo
(Vic.) is closing with a shindy. Long Gully and Eagle-
hawk fought out the final match for the premiership.
Eaglehawk won : whereat sundry players from the Gully
were accused of being dead, although they didn’t fail
down, and the alleged dead ’uns, fearing trouble, did an
undignified bolt into a neighboring pub, where a troop of
puffing policemen guarded them. Next day the air was
full of whirling statements that the ’Hawks had squared
prominent Gull.vites. One man was said to have been
given £1 every time he kicked the ball out of bounds.
The secretary was accused of using persuasion with Gui’y
players, and a lot of other more or less vague yarns went
flying about. The Bendigo Football Association held a
meeting, and wiped out for life a Long Gully player from
Maldon ; the Eaglehawk secretary was given to under-
stand that he was guilty of something not too clearly
specified, a. number of other players were either dis-
qualified or cautioned, and more charges are to be in-
vestigated. Altogether the season has left a nasty taste
in the mouth of the goldfields football follower.
* » » *
Adelaide again claims the club premiership of
this continent in the Australian code of football, although
S. A. was so badly beaten by Victoria’s combined team in
Melbourne. The premier clubs of the two cities meet
after the season closes. Last year Carlton (V.) went
down before Norwood (S.A.). This time West Adelaide
just knocked out Norwood in the local final, while Carlton
was again on top in Melbourne. Carlton again made the
journey to Adelaide (with two good men absent), and
was quite comfortably beaten. “ West ” has never, until
this season, been able to beat anything above a team
from the Home for Incurables, and its success is con-
sidered a triumph for the training principles of Jack
Reedman, the veteran cricketer.
* * ■* m
Australian footballers in the Cold Country are
having a parlous time. The Wallabies, as the amateurs
call themselves, have lost Flanagan and Peter Burge
with broken understandings, and C. E. Murnin through
illness. The League team is minus Hennessey, who frac-
tured his jaw, or got it smashed, at Bradford, last week.
A. B. Burge and Ken Gavin have departed to reinforce
the Wallaby team, and should arrive in England about
Nov. 16, which will be in time to take part in the big
matches of the tour. British football is evidently still in
a bad way, as both amateur and professional teams have
so far won more than a fair proportion of the matches
played. The Leaguers were earning an undesirable repu-
tation for rough play, by the way.
* * * *
First-grade cricket in Sydney on Saturday was
a curious display of excellent form by comparatively
young batsmen, and shocking failures by big guns.
Folkard, of Balmain, made 149 ; Bubb, of Glebe, 134 ; and
Bull, of Middle Harbor, 131. On the other hand, Cotter
left for two, Noble was bowled for five, Trumper excelled
himself with 29, and the Rev. Waddy went for four.
The big guns failed in the bowling as well.
Cotter’s first 24 yielded 46, which was better than fish-
ing ; and while Noble took none for 28, his humble club-
mate, Sayle, smashed up three for eight runs, and Collins
got five for 20.
**■*•*
Rain during the week considerably affected
Melbourne wickets last Saturday, so the different debates
for the pennant were mostly distinguished by deadly
trundling and frequent blobs. The best score was Delves’
67 for Carlton, while the only ones which can be considered
respectable were Stuckey’s67 for North, and Healey’s 56for
Richmond. Melbourne, which had finished on the previous
Saturday with six for 256, had, considering the wicket,
an easy chance of a first innings victory. So they declared
their innings closed, sent in their opponents, South, and
walloped them all down for 97, Hazlitt taking seven for
31. South had another try, but the crushing only yielded
33 for five wickets —which result was due to Armstrong,
who, on the drying wicket, obtained four for 17. Mel-
bourne thus won easily' by 159 on the first innings. East v.
University' finished similarly. East (eight for 241) declared
theirinnings closed, rocked down the students for 108 and
four for 38 (Collins, 7 for 52). East thus had a balance of 133.
The argument between Carlton and Essendon, which
began evenly, resulted in an easy win for the first-named
b.y nine wickets. With a lead of 16 on the first innings,
the ’Donspatted up 84 very politely, leaving Carlton 101 to
win, arid Carlton did it with the loss of only one wicket.
P’ran, taking advantage of the damp turf, declared their
four for 215 finished, and murdered R’mond for 27 (Kyle
four for 60, and Blackie three for one). So P’ran won by
188, though R’mond managed to make 110 for six wickets
on their second appearance. Northcote were very' easily
beaten by North, with the margin of four wickets’and 85
runs, North having responded to N’cote’s 100 with six for
185. As for St. Kilda, they finished their small trouble with
Collingwood, with a win by 61 on the first innings,
scores being—Saints, 163; and C’wood, 102, and six
for 111.
* * * *
Those Sub-District matches which began in
Melbourne on Saturday were worse than unfavorable—
they were just devastations ! Port v. Williamstown was
practically notable for its array of ey'eglasses, 11 of them
being exhibited for the afternoon. Ports made 50, ’Town
41; then Ports lost three for one. Townsman Crowe (4 for
9), and Portsmen Purvis (4 for 5) and McDougall (6 for 16)
were the most valuable hurlers of the ball. Hawthorn v.
Malvern was equally tame from a batting point of view.
Malvern obtained 42 and three for four, to which the
“naice ” suburb has answered with 64. Haw-hawthorn
bowler Macrow secured best bowling honors with eight
for 10, while Malvernite Ballantvne gathered in six for 44.
The matches Brighton v. Caulfield and Coburg v. Elstern-
wick had to be postponed, as the Caulfield and Coburg
grounds were, for a good part, plain ocean.
* * * *
Sydney Flying Squadron also opened its season
with tvyo heats and a final, in which some very pretty
work was displayed. In the first heat Chris Webb, in the
Australian, downed both Vision and Donnelly, and in the
second T. Leach, in Zanita, won easily. In the final,
with the same handicaps, they both beat Australian, and
in the struggle for places Vision downed Donnelly by'2secs.
Vision would have won the double event but for
a sudden squall, which caught it under a full spinnaker
and laid the boat over, gunwale under, while the audience
held its breath and waited for the capsize. But if skipper
Duncan had been caught napping, he redeemed his repu-
tation, and brought the boat upright, but full to the
thwarts. Meanwhile, the balers worked feverishly', and
soon had the water out. Then Vision flew on, but it was
too late ; the wary Christian Webb had soared to the
front on that sudden gust, and the race was over.
* * * ( *
Webb now appears to have quite recovered
from the recent indisposition, and to be building himself
up in great style. He will need to, as Arnst has only 81b.
to get off to reach his cargo limit, and is also hard at
work. During the last few weeks of his preparation
Arnst will camp up the Wanganui River, and practically
live and sleep in the open air. When last heard from he
weighed 13st. 81b., and was generally growing in favor as
the coming champion, in spite of his tendency to roll,
and a shrug of the shoulders that is said to interfere in
some way with the clean delivery of his stroke. All the
same, he will have to look after himself, for Webb, in spite
of his illness, is a formidable rower, and a man who com-
bines sprinting ability with the power to last. And that
is always a dangerous combination.
George Whelch, although only 29 years of age,
has a fine record, having won 46 of 56 races competed in.
For a champion athlete he is small, his height being only
sft. 6in. and his rowing weight 9st. 101 b. But he has
mopped up the best Maoriland has brought against him,
including Webb, whom he downed in 1905 at Wellington.
In ’O6 and ’OB he won the single championship of the
State, and also (both times in company with J. Woodill)
secured the double sculls championship. If he downs
Thoroughgood, who is a giant compared to him, he will
come at the winner of the Webb-Arnst match.
* * * »
Almost a “ Henley ” crowd spread itself along
the upper banks of the Yarra on Saturday to watch a race
that lasted just a second short of five minutes. On the
previous Friday a Scotch College eight had won its heat
against Geelong Grammar School, Geelong College had
beaten Wesley, and the C. of E. Grammar School had
beaten Xavier College, but was “ outed ” for a foul: where-
fore the “final,” to decide which was to be “head of the
river” for the year, raised hopes of a good race. The
winning post has been shifted higher up, opposite the
Morgue, so as to avoid the bend, and the almost straight
mile clearly demonstrated the superiority of “Scotch,”
who, on an average weight of list. 131 b., rowed a long,
machine-like stroke that landed them home an easy two
lengths ahead of Geelong College, and eight lengths
ahead of Xavier. The Geelong lads scaled an average
list. o£lb., and as iit was that school’s first appearance in
this race they have nothing to be ashamed of.
While? this episode was perspiring on the upper
river, the single sculls Amateur Championship of the
State was being decided on the 2J miles stretch between
Williamstown and Footscray, where for the last seven
years P. C. Ivens has led all competitors home. P. C. has
given it best now, and Brasch, of the Mercantile,
was expected to row away from his only oppo-
nent, A. Richards, of South Melbourne. Starting
with 36 strokes to Richards’ 34, he did so at first,
and at the ’Scray Bottle Works was two lengths to the
good, but there Richards spurted and Brasch shut up
like a clam. At the Lyell Works the South Melbournite
had his boat’s nose in front, and thence to the finish he
increased his advantage, winning eventually by nearly
100 yards. Time (17min.) is 21sec. worse than Ivens’
best, but there was an ebb tide, and a strong freshet in
the river to contend against, which makes Richards’ per-
formance look promising.
* . * » *
How Maoriland has suddenly come to the front
in rowing! Here are Arnst and Webb, both Maori-
landers, about to contest the world’s championship on
the Wanganui; and Whelch, of Akaroa, is going to try
and wrest the Australian championship from Benjamin
Thoroughgood, at the Hunter River. If half that is said
about Whelch is true, he is a wonder. His endurance is
described as phenomenal and his speed as marvellous,
and he is said to be able to row a whole three-mile course
from start to finish at top speed. Evidently the grave is
being dug for Arnst’s championship before he has won it.
* * * *
Said that “ Old Bill ” Beach has a son who will
shortly take to the water, and prove to be a chip off the
old block.
* * * ■*
Newington College (N.S.W.) is the champion
school at rifle shooting, having last week won the Rawson
Cup for the sixth year in succession, and also maintained
its hold on the Great Public Schools Shield. The King’s
School was runner-up in both struggles, and in compari-
son to these two, the rest were nowhere. The dropping
of the bat and ball for the rifle is an excellent idea, and
deserves well.
* * * *
J. Salvana, the N.S.W. amateur cyclist who
started out to ride from Melbourne to Sydney in 53 hours
— a very serious problem—had to abandon his attempt at
Albury owing to fiendish weather. In the meantime,
Pearson’s record of 57hr. 24min. stands unfractured.
* * * *
The handicapping in the Warrnambool to Mel-
bourne cycle race indicates that the gentleman who fixed
the starts had a very high opinion of the powers of the
scratch men. To have won, any of them would have had
to cover the whole distance at an average rate of 23i
miles an hour, which is a tall order for 165 miles. Not
since Jack Arnst struck a weak field in 1903 has a scratch
man won, and, if the long markers are to be given such
handicaps in future, there is little chance of the scratch
men ever doing it again. For the long markers train
for the big race now as they did not in the days a-rear.
The system of long handicaps grew up in days when the
front-markers were novices and the scratch men were
riders with reputations. Now the whole bunch trains,
and trains hard, and the handicapper needs must make a
fresh start without any previous data to work on.

“ I’m glad, when at last, after three years’
trying, I have induced Mr. Johnson to meet me in the
ring, that it is to be right here in Australia ; for there
are no such sports in the world as Australians, and there’s
no place on earth where two men could get such a sure
fair and square deal. Why, when I whipped your cham-
pion the other day, you’d ha’ thought from the way they
cheered me that it was the other way around.” —Tommy
Burns at the Hanging Up of the Purse of £7500 on
Thursday.
* * ■* *
On Thursday last, Hugh D. Mclntosh deposited
with sporting scribe W. F. Corbett, cheques for £7lOO, the
stake in the Burns-Johnson disturbance, which will take
place in Sydney on the morning of Boxing Day. E. W.
O’Sullivan was in the chair, and many sporting scribes
looked on with watering mouths and wondered why their
misguided parents hadn’t brought them up to be world-
famous bruisers. Evidently the champion of the world
has no misgivings about the result of the struggle, as he
deposited with Mr. Corbett a cheque for £lOOO that John-
son would not stop him in 20 rounds, let alone 12, as the
browp man boasted he would, and another fqr a like
amount that he would win right out. So when Johnson
arrives he has something pretty to cover and battle for.
It was a cheerful function. The speakers were in good
form, the refreshments were excellent, and the informa-
tion vouchsafed was interesting.
Scientific Boxing and Self Defence, Ltd., it
seems, is four-fifths Mclntosh —the sterling sportsman who
made the League of Wheelmen run straight in spite of the
capers of the cronk riders, and who threw away the
Sydney Thousand because he could not guarantee a
straight run. So, considering that the Mclntosh is the
governing director of the syndicate, the public can rest
assured that the coming trouble will be a genuine battle.
Tommy Burns announced that the scrap is his last, and
when he has polished off Johnson, or yielded up to him
the championship, he retires in deference to the wish of
his wife. It is also more than possible that he will settle
in Australia, and, perhaps, spend his leisure in send-
ing forth over the earth a stream of Australian cham-
pions. This last was not announced, but writer hears it
on what may be considered reasonable authority. But,
all the same, Burns does not strike one as the sort of man
who will fool round turning out fighters. He is above it.
He is an anachronism in pugilism, a man who regards the
whole business with sovereign contempt. He found him-
self quite inadvertently a professional boxer, havingnlled
a gap on impulse one night. He also discovered at the
same time that he had splendid physical qualifications for
a professional boxer, and saw how', with bis physique, he
could make money quicker at it than any other w’alk of
life, and w'aded in to do so. And he has done it. And of
all his fights this one with Johnson is probably the only
one he will enter with any personal feeling. Johnson has
“pursued” him—mostly with talk—and in the riot some
things have been said that have irritated Burns, and the
result will be a fight that will be worth going far to see.
It will be interesting in more wavs than one.
In the first place, Burns’ reputation is at stake. The
other side has sneered at his reputation as a paper one,
made by sweeping through a crowd of mediocre fighters
and missing the giants. For it is the fashion just now to
regard Jeffries, Sharkey, Ruhlin, Fitzsimmons, and the
batch of fighters who were on in the last act, so to speak,
as something far superior to anything on deck just now.
But it isn’t Burns’ fault that he and they did not
come on in the same act. Had they been on deck there
is little doubt that the burly man with the Napoleonic
face and the slow drawl wouid have fought them every
one, and, considering the anatomical subtleness of his
fighting methods, would also have held his own. And
that is just where the real interest of the fight with John-
son crops up. It is the meeting of the old and the new.
Johnson, judging by his exhibitions in Australia, belongs
to the old school that swings mighty, bone-smashing
blows ; while Burns represents the new, short, quick jab
on the vital spot, repeated over and over again, till the
other man crumples up. The blow that Bang caught.
Burns would have laid out the old style of fighter who
stood up square and strong, whereas when it struck
Burns it lost its effect because it was like hitting
a swinging door, and he went with the blow.
Moreover, Burns’ methods of defence are unique.
Whereas the “ giant ” took the punishment on his glove,
or dodged it, Burns paralyses the attack by delivering
short, quick taps on the fighting shoulder or arm, which
divert the blow, destroy its force, and produce a feeling
of helplessness which is fairly close to demoralisation.

Arthur Douglas, a leathery S’ African, met
Hughie Mehegan at Melbourne Oyclorama, Mother
night. Douglas had a powerful reputation as a smiter,
and went to work in a vigorous way. Mehegan is among
the top flighters, and is a difficult proposition to ex-
tinguish early in the game owing to an effective smother.
In fact, his smother spoils his work from a spectacular
point of view. But smothering tactics went for nought,
because the man with the leather dial sailed in at close
quarters A la Burns, and in consequence Mehegan was
soon up the pole. Though th > contest lasted 11 rounds,
Mehegan never had his nose in front at any stage. In the
fourth round the S’African dropped Mehegan with a short
right that landed too high to settle the argument, and in
the ninth spasm Mehegan sank to the floor like sft. bin.
of tripe. At the conclusion of the 11th scene Mehegan’s
best friend skied the chiffon, and Douglas left with the
heavy end of the cash-box.
» * * * t
The opening of the season by the Royal Sydney
Yacht Squadron on Saturday last was a . social affair of
the purest fire-water and grape-juice. A couple of dinghy
races were meandered through, but it was mere make-
believe. The real business of the day was frill, and it
frilled exceedingly.
* * * *
Maoriland Rugby TTnion has started a revolu-
tion, and when Maoriland Rugby sees itself after being
remodelled, it will wonder what the apparition is. The
rules are to be thrown into the fire and re-cast, and our
old friend the wing-forward will practically cease from
wrangling and rushing, as he is to be penned back behind
an imaginary line drawn through the back.of the scrum
while the ball is therein. There will be much controversy
about the locality of that line in the future. The frequent
cause of much nose-wrecking will be done away with by
the referee putting the leather into the scrum with his
own fair hands. The line-out scramble goes overboard,
but crawls on deck again in a reduced state, as the ball
must be thrown in a full distance of not less than five
yards. Further, the game is to be divided into four spells
of 20mins. each, With an interval after the second spell.
Altogether the reformers have stolen quite a number of
points from the despised Australian game.
* * * *
Stories of the iniquities that killed bike racing
in Melbourne are as plentiful as pebbles now that the
game is up. The plans of the boss schemer were fallible,
of course, but as far as he could ensure the “ stiffness ” of
starters, other than the one that carried his bullion, he
stopped at nothing in his meth.ods of making them “safe.”
His selected candidate for a certain big Wheel Race (not
so many years ago) was found to be a very doubtful
“good thing” on his merits. As a matter of fact the
chosen one got beaten pretty badly in the race by men
who either couldn’t help it or who had been contemptu-
ously ignored in the deadening arrangement. But pre-
vious to the event the deadener had feared one above all
others of the lot left in for the final, and this one was so
confident about his chance that he at first refused to be
bought. His own particular boss was content to partici-
pate in the swindle, but he—the rider—expressed a de-
termination to win if possible. So the deadener came
along with an increased offer, which was refused. Finally,
he gave the.rebellious subject the option of being wholly
bribed or half murdered. His threat was undisguised.
He would stand no nonsense, he said. Intimidation
carried the point, and the rebellious subject agreed to be
one of the preordained losers.


ACUTE HEARING
Deafness and Head Noises.
The instances in which the unfortunate people affected
with Deafness and Noises regain their natural hearing
(says the Health Journal) are so rare as to arouse
unusual comment. Such, however, appears to have been
the case regarding the miraculous restoration of perfect
hearing to a woman named Mrs. Jennie Stuart, who
resides at 62 Bondi Road, Waverley, N.S.W.
Mrs. Stuart (who is a professional nurse) first noticed
something wrong with her hearing while attending a
patient in her professional capacity, whom she had to ask
to repeat her conversation on several occasions, and
presumed that it may have been the fault of her patient
not speaking sufficiently distinct. Mrs. Stuart, however,
suspected that there was something wrong with her
hearing when in church one Sunday morning, she could
hardly hear the sermon. Being a nurse, and understand-
ing the great complexity of the Aural construction and
the many ills this delicate organism is subject to, she
naturally realised the importance of immediate attention,
and at once consulted a doctor, who made a thorough
and careful examination, after which he prescribed the
usual treatment, but, surprisingly, the following Sunday,
Mrs. Stuart could hear no better, and, if anything, she
felt worse. She then consulted an Ear Specialist, but
with no improvement, so she bothered no more with
doctors for a while, in the vain hope that the trouble might
disappear and her hearing would improve in its natural
course. But, instead of improving, it was only getting
worse and worse. She later consulted another specialist,
and then another, but without any benefit whatsoever, and
her deafness was growing worse from time to time, until
one day (about 15 years ago) she noticed a peculiar roar-
ing sound in the head and a sort of ringing in the ears.
These noises started again a few days later, and repeated
the following day, so that it became quite annoying. The
disagreeable noises seemed to increase from time to time
until they appeared to be permanent, and caused the
deafness to advance to a much more severe stage.
Nurse Stuart then consulted other reputable specialists
and some of the leading medical practitioners known
in the profession. All these able men took advantage
of every recognised medical treatment in this case, for, in
addition to the financial consideration, their professional
reputation was at stake, and they took a personal interest
in the woman. Her case received special attention, but
all the efforts made by these well-known medical men
seemed to have been in vain, for the poor woman was con-
tinually getting worse. She then tried some of the
advertised devices for curing Deafness and Head Noises,
but without any benefit, until, fortunately, one day she
was told of the Earoscope, which applied Pure Aural
Kinetics to the Ear.
Nurse Stuart, having tried everything, with the result
that she was only growing worse from day to day, natu-
rally lost all hope of ever being cured, and only tried
the Earoscope as the very last resource. But what a
happy surprise it was for her to notice symptoms of
improvement after a few days’ application of the Aural
Kinetics. t
Nurse Stuart was gradually regaining her hearing in
such a manner that when she attended church ten days
later she was quite able to hear the clergyman speak
when sitting close by. The Ear Noises seemed to diminish
from day to day, and the good nights’ rest which Mrs.
Stuart was then able to enjoy seemed to constantly in-
crease her hearing, and in four weeks’ time she was so
improved that she could hear the clergyman quite plainly
from the very back seat in the church. The doctors
were quite surprised at the wonderful cure so success-
fully attained in this case, after they had given up all
hope of the woman ever regaining' her hearing, and even
the specialists had proclaimed her case entirely incurable
prior to this treatment.
Since then it has been proved that the Aural Kinetics
applied by the Earoscope has cured innumerable cases of
Deafness, Head Noises, Ringing in the Ears, and various
Aural complications. The Earoscope seems to be entirely
diffeheiit from all other treatments, inasmuch as it con-
tains no Drugs, no Chemicals, and no Electricity, but
simply Pure Aural Kinetics, which all sufferers can apply
themselves right at their own homes quite conveniently,
and perfectly safe, without the necessity of a doctor, and
without any interference to daily routine work, business,
or social duties.
Anyone suffering from Deafness, Head Noises, Ringing
in the Ears, or any other Aural complication, as well as
those interested in this subject, may write for further
particulars to the Earoscope Institute, which will be sent
by them free of any charge on mentioning this paper.
The address of this Institution is:—The Earoscope Insti-
tute of Australasia, 90 Pitt-street, Sydney, N.S.W.

SYDNEY.
An almost unknown unionist, E. J.
Cotter, a cooper, has reached the Victorian State
Assembly, as member for Richmond, at the first
time of trying. His starchy opponent, Councillor
Wishart, was a Kew land-agent; and as he had
I polled fairly well against the late George Bennett
and Solly at last election, other candidates with-
i drew under the impression that their chances
were hopeless—which they were.' Wishart did
little better than before, whereas Cotter polled all
Solly’s Labor votes and most of those that used
I to go to deceased George Bennett —2046 to 1195.
Cotter doesn’t promise anything startling, and
certainly won’t rival Deakin as an orator \ out he
is youngish, and will persevere.
To fill in time in the intervals of doing
nothing and pretending to do a lot, Adolphus
Bent is now trying to pass a Gaols Act Amend-
ment Bill, which purports to provide that female
prisoners in what the members call “a certain
condition,” or others who may have to undergo
specially difficult surgical operations, may be

G. Reid, during the Defence debate
Our ports may be thoroughly wellequipped with sub-
marines, and forts, and mines, and soldiers> , bat T !®
could not expect the fly to come into the web. The
more we fortified our coast the more the enemy would
keep clear of it.
In other words, if Australia equips itself with
submarines, mines, soldiers, etc., no foreign Bower
is likely to interfere with it; and conversely the
less it so equips itself, the more liable Australia
will be to foreign molestation. It was a weird
sort of argument to use against the institution ot
Australian defences—unless Reid wants to see
Australia molested by a foreign Bower.

m
HERE are num-
bers of people in
Melbourne and
in other parts
of Australia
who haven’t
seen Sydney,
and have only heard of it as a place
that is not too shy about its harbor.
That able-bodied fetich, certainly due at its
birth to the modest declarations of the proud
Sydneyite, has been growing and flourishing some-
what like the river hyacinth ever since. It is by now
a fairly vigorous idea, implying that there is very
little to see in Sydney but the harbor. That is a
mistake, as anybody who takes one of the steamers
of the Five Companies to Sydney, will see. After
the traveller has entered Sydney Heads, he cer-
tainly views probably the most picturesque sheet
of water in the world ; but, behind that is Sydney;
and behind Sydney are the Blue Mountains. All
of those things have come to be included in the visit
—for anybody who goes to Sydney without visit-
ing the Blue Mountains would be considered as un-
conventional and eccentric as the person who
would inspect the harbor without looking at Syd-
ney. To-day a trip to Sydney means rather a
trip to New South Wales.
When the steamer gets abreast of the Mac-
quarie light, which pokes up into the sky on the
South Head, the traveller begins to understand
why so much language has been poured upon the
scene that is spread before his gaze. As the
steadier gets fairly into the middle of it, he then
sees the full beauty of the scene. On his left are
those delightful indentations known as Watson’s,
Rose, Double and Rushcutters Bays, with their
green slopes and prettily situated villa resi-
dences. Ahead is Middle Head, slowly
lifting itself into full view, and, to the right
of it, Middle Harbor receding into the dis-
tance. All around are blue-watered bays,
and, with a generous sun splashing
the scene, the whole is a million
pictures in one. Then Sydney
emerges into sight; ferry-
boats buzz across the water
with more frequency and
numerousness ; big, fat mail-
boats with aldermanic waist-
bands, so to speak, crush
into the perspective at Circu-
lar Quay ; the steamer slowly
rounds Miller’s Point and brings up at itsappointed
place in Darling Harbor. Then the traveller
tumbles ashore with his baggage, and soon dis-
covers that bydney isn’t all harbor. An efficient
and speedy electric tramway system, which
wanders all over the city and suburbs,
is there, ready to take up and set down at
almost any address. The cars run all night
and all day—which allows the visitor to face the
prospect of getting home late, or rather early,
with equanimity. Moreover, they are as safe as
every public vehicle, excepting, perhaps, hearses !
£52?
When the stranger commences to look around
Sydney it won’t be long before he happens upon
the Botanical Gardens, which overlook Farm
Cove. These Gardens, which, along with the
Domain, comprise a pretty extensive area, are
noted for their pleasant situation and the care
given to their numerous flower beds, lawns, and
drives. Lying on the green slope above Farm
Gove one can gaze down upon as peaceably pretty
a scene as has ever been met with in an
album of British views. The Australian squad-
ron of British warships may sometimes be seen
lying at anchor—which rather aids the effect than
otherwise—while, farther out, the ever-busy ferry
boats—fussing about like the hostesses at a bishop’s
garden party—may be decried, like kids’ toys on
a toy sea. In the Gardens, or, rather, in the
Domain, is the Art Gallery, worth more than a
moment of time and demanding more than an
atom of intelligence. The pictures are instructive
and good, their general quality leaning to sanity
rather than to pyrotechnics; while the Minera-
logical and Agricultural Museum attached will
move the expert to sobs of admiration. Running
along one side of the Domain is Macquarie-street,
a most portentous and awesome thoroughfare.
There, clustering in a bunch of right roval edifices,
are the Houses of Parliament, the Lands Office,
Treasury, etc. —all of which the visitor cannot
omit to scan,, since they are the centre —the
official brain centre—of a State that has had its
name in the papers. But time doesn’t permit an
intimate inspection, unless the stranger has two
eternities’ leave of absence—which he hasn’t —so
he shoves his way on to a tramcar and leaves the
scene aft. When he has to seethe G.P.0., the
Town Hall, St. Mary’s R.C. Cathedral, the Queen
Victoria Markets, the Central Railway Station,
the theatres, the University—not to mention
numbers of others that would burst any ordinary
catalogue—he hasn’t time to gather matter for
architectural treatises.
What with Sydney trams and Sydney ferries,
the visitor will get quite a lot to engage his atten-
tion in record time. By the trams he can go to
Botany, La Perouse, Glebe, Leichhardt, etc., in
rather swift time ; while the ferries, which ply all
over the harbor from Watson’s Bay and Manly on
one side to the Parramatta and Lane Cove Rivers
on the other, open up, so to speak, the best bits of
harbor Scenery. Now that the summer is on the
threshold, the harbor will commence to look its
choicest, and the attractions of its many indenta-
tions be increased tremendously. Also, the season
for surf-bathing at Manly, Coogee and Bondi has
already broken out. and troops of people are com-
mencing to be joyful and shouting multitudes,
spending a loti 0 f their leisure in washing in the
sort of monastic garments determined by the local
councils.
Everybody who goes to Sydney goes to the
Blue Mountains—it is inevitable ! Everybody
takes train to Eatoomba, the most central spot
from which excursions are made. From Ka.toomba,
one goes downhill. The sights are spread out
lower down ifi splodges of grandeur' upon the
map. There ,are the Leura Falls, beautiful,
titanic, and of startling depth. The tableland
thereabouts has been cleft into chasms, and has all
the awesomeness of big, unfamiliar things. The
vegetation in the vicinity is as luxuriant, but less
garish, than a milliner’s showroom. It runs to wild
loveliness and extraordinary abundance, and the
memory of it is lifelong! Other places that demand
bare eyes or field'glasses are the Kanimbla Valley,
Minnihaha Falls, Govett’s Leap—a grim, shivery
thing, with a 520 ft. drop—and the notable Jeno-
lan Caves. Mugh iias been written about the
Caves —but not; too much ! To pour the diction-
ary on them is only to advertise them. They have
had thousands of visitors, and will have thousands ’
more. They have the beauty of all natural won- i
ders ; while the six-hours’ journey to them from
Mount Victoria is an interesting festival of the
unexpected. Weeks rather
than days should be spent ir
the Blue Mountains;
while some attention
could be given to the
Hawkesbury and the
attractions of the
Southern Tableland.
And all of those
things can be seen
and memorised by
first embarking on
one of thesteamers of
the Five Companies.


S. A. law-coui’fcs discover that existing legis-
lation makes a fool of them. The police-magis-
trate in Adelaide dismissed a gambling case. He
was overruled on appeal, but it seems that in such a
position there is no machinery whatever for collect-
ing the fine and Costs! The P.M. says that if he put
the bailiffs in, in the ordinary way, he would be
liable to an action for damages. As things now
stand, an appeal against a police-court conviction
has a sporting chance of success, whilst an appeal
against an acquittal is no good even if it does suc-
ceed. So the fool of an Act is to be altered.
Queensland Government has two railway
lines in progress—at least they are more or less in
progress—in the rich Atherton-Herberton table-
land (N.Q.): one the Tolga branch; the other
from Atherton to the Evelyn scrub. There has
been a good deal of growling about the conditions
under which the work is let, mostly in small
contracts, and feeling is being worked up against
Railway Minister Kerr. Both lines will open up
very rich land ; but at present thereabouts one
sees 30 Chinese at cultivation work for one
white Australian. Simultaneously with the
building of the lines, something drastic ought to
be done to prevent the leading of lands to Ah Sin.
It can’t be the climate in this case, for if the
white men can’t work on this tableland they can’t
work at Ballarat.
The Parliament that Adolphus Bent nomi-
nally bosses began its present session on July 2
15 weeks ago and so far the measures assented to
comprise two Supply Bills, a Bill to allow muni-
cipalities to spend their rates on Fleet celebra-
tions, three semi-private Bills (South Melbourne
Mechanics, Castlemaine land and Elsternwick
Land), the Ultima Railway Construction, and the
Mines Development (i.e. y mining cos.’ subsidis-
ing) Bills. Eight measures altogether—and not
one of even second-rate importance ! The hapless
State keeps two Houses of 100 members, and a
sub-Governor at £5OOO a year, together with all
the costly paraphernalia said to be necessary, in
order to pass eight trifling measures in 15 weeks—
work that a small, compact council could do in
half as many days at one-tenth the cost.
Labor member Page was one of the bright
spots (and James of Maranoa makes a large spot)
m the iron bonus debate. Various Labor mem-
bers whined that the Commonwealth couldn’t
afford a bonus. Fowler wanted to know how the
spending of it was going to benefit Westralia.
All this made Page as angry as he can be :
Why, when I listen to this sort of whining, it sounds to
me as if it ought to come from the Conservative Opposi-
tion, and not from my colleagues in the Labor corner.
Is this Commonwealth gone so low financially that it
cannot spend £20,000 to help an industry in which
thousands of men draw wages? lam not going- to lend
my vote to take the bread and butter out of those men’s
mouths.
"'Lm ” Page is an alleged Freetrader, and, as
a Freetrader, is presumably prepared to prevent
quite a lot of bread and butter going into Aus-
tralia’s mouth.
Now that the warm weather is coming on, the
problem for many people will be how to keep cool. Some
get into t.he sea at 6 o’clock in the morning, and* have a
good swim, believing, and rightly, that to thoroughly
cool the body first thing in the morning is an excellent
tonic against summer-heat. Other folks again reach the
desideratum by carefully regulating their diet, abstain-
ing from heating foods, and avoiding blood-heatino-
highly alcoholic liquors. But superior to all these is the
man who not only takes his morning dip, and carefully
watches his diet, hut who also adds true zest to life, gets
into a hammock or an easy chair on the shady sirie of the
house, fills his G.B.D. pipe with his favorite tobacco, and
enjoys through this famous pipe the sweetest, coolest
smoke possible to obtain.*
