The Lost Australian Oil Industry.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
An oil industry is like a sugar or dairy
industry, consisting of a big refinery surrounded
bv a number of crude oil works. Even the 19
Standard Oil Companies of the United States do
not depend on their own wells. They buy petro-
leum from thousands of wells, and make
their money out of the refining and selling of
it. The refineries of Scotland also buy oil from
crude oil companies, who make a good thing out
of the raw material.
It takes costly machinery, skilled chemists and
expensive craftsmen to make oil shale into the
adjuncts of civilisation. And hitherto Australian
shale has been worked with too small a capital.
Owing to the inaccessible nature of the country
the pioneers could not even maintain regular sup-
plies of shale for export for foreign gas makers.
And the erratic nature of the supply did much to
kill the trade. Then the foreign gas-maker cast
round for a substitute and found, among other
things, the incandescent burner, which made in-
ferior gas give a fairly bright light.
Unfortunately, the shale-bearing country of
N.S.W. is in very rough, inaccessible terri-
tory, and that is one of the things that
has practically prevented Australia having a
booming oil industry to-day. Even so rabid a
Freetrader as Henry Parkes encouraged the in-
dustry by a duty of 3d. per gallon. And it
was owing to that duty that sundry enter-
prising people tried to start a shale oil industry.
This difficulty of access has been the great lion
in the path. The late David Syme was a man of
giant resources and mighty energy and grit. He
knew all about the shale deposits of N.S.W., and
determined to blossom into an Oil king. He came,
he saw, and he skedaddled. He saw that till rail-
ways were built through the shale-bearing coun-
try it was useless to construct any great central
refinery. For, like sugar and butter, successful
oil-making depends upon concentration and the
creation of great refineries.
* * * *
Hitherto, the habit of the Freetrader and the
Standard Oil advocate has been, when an Australian
duty on oil has been proposed, to shriek, “Look
at the Scottish industry. If the Scottish industry
can thrive without Protection, the richer shale of
Australia can fight the battle of life without Pro-
tection.” But as the Freetrader is generally a fool,
it is quite safe to look at Scotland. The Scottish
shale oils are prodigiously rich in ammonia liquid
and paraffin wax, yielding as much as 20 and 25
per cent, of - the latter to the Australian
shale oil’s sto 6| per cent. So rich were
the Scottish shales in this respect that
they were able to treat the ammonia and wax as
primary products, and the oil itself as a secondary.
But more : the Scottish shale industry was the
first in the English-speaking countries. It started
away back in 1842, and for many years vast quan-
tities of Scottish shale oils were exported to the
United States, where illuminating oils were
made from them. It was .not till 1858 that the
Yankee oil industry started, and it was not till
1868 that John D. Rockefeller appeared on the
scene. In 1882 he floated his business into a
company, with a capital of $70,000,000; and
about the end of the ’eighties he at-
tacked the Scottish Shale Oil industry,-
and by sustained dumping endeavored to
slay it. So all those years, from ’42 to ’9O, the
Scottish shale industry had no rival in its home
market, and all those years the men who ran it
were perfecting machinery, getting better know-
ledge of their shale and its potentialities, and
securing their market. The Australian industry
has no such halcyon period in front of it. It has
to evolve its industry, to do much pioneering
chemical work, and scrap several plants in conse-
quence ; and then, having done all these things
and built a few railways to make its deposits acces-
sible and generally get the industry ready to
start, it has to come out and tear its market from
the very teeth of the Standard Oil Trust.
Can Australians reasonably expect that anyone
will put up the hundreds of thousands, if not
millions, necessary to do all this, unless Australia
shows some sign of being interested in the develop-
ment of its own resources,* and evinces some in-
clination to encourage such enterprise by securing
the industry at, least a share of its own market ?
It is as much the duty of the Australian people to
protect its capitalists from unfair competition as
it is to protect them from the attacks of train
wreckers and burglars. And if Australians expect
people to do so much for Australia without
encouragement, they expect too much. For
although the Scottish industry had 48 years’ start
when the Standard Oil Trust came along with its
Sherman methods, in the end the number of re-
fineries greatly diminished.
• * * •
Australia, with its practically unlimited
supplies of oil shale, has the makings of a
gigantic industry. But to organise such an
enterprise means money, money and more money;
and without security no one can get money.
When the Australian Oil Industry has been
builded up and makes its first effort in
the export line, it the launching of
tank . steamers, the construction of reservoirs
at various ports of shipment, and, as much oil
would be sent abroad tinned and cased, an army
of workmen would be employed in making the
tins and cases, and the supply of raw material
would increase and foster the tin and spelter
works, saw mills and other subsidiary indus-
tries. Australia would get its oil as cheap if
not cheaper than it does now, and instead of sup-
porting several towns out west in the U.S.A., the
oil trade of the world would support several
thriving towns scattered around the Blue Moun-
tains, and throughout Queensland and Tasmania.
This may seem a visionary view to take of
things. But it is ' not. The destruction of the
Australian oil industry is regarded as important
by the American oil kings not so much for “ the
present Australian trade, but for the possibilities
that lie ahead if that industry is developed.” Aus-
tralia has such vast potentialities in that respect
that at any cost the industry must be kept from
starting, and the most accomplished lobbyist in
the service of the Standard Oil Company of New
York was sent all the way to Australia to plead
the company’s case wdth our legislators. The
result was that the provision that only kerosene
which came into Australia in bulk should be free
was dropped, and a motion to place oil on the
free list was carried on the voices. The Standard
Oil Co. of New York is the parent company of the
19 Standard Oil Companies, and its directors are
the directors of the whole bunch, and control their
poHcy. , *
Now the question arises how is this splendid
oil industry to be fostered. As the tariff is dis-
posed of the question of imposing a duty cannot
easily be raised at present. The admission of
kerosene free of duty makes it possible for the
local industry to be fought by means of dump-
ing, and if not ruined outright, to be ren-
dered so non-lucrative as to disgust the
people who have put money into it. The first
really large-scale attempt to start the indus-
try is about ready to begin operations. It
has constructed a railway right into the shale-bear-
ing country; and is putting down a refining plant
that will compare favorably with any in the world.
Altogether, over half a million of capital has
been expended in getting the ground cleared
for the starting of a great oil industry.
Therefore the best way to encourage the industry
is to grant a bounty on the production’of crude
oil. And a bounty of 2d. per gallon will
place the Australian oilmen where the compe-
tition of the United States, British East
Indian and other oils will not be able to
crush them, without giving them the opportunity
of creating anything like a monopoly. To give a
bounty on the refined oil would make the bounty
a benefit for big capitalists only. Given on the
IN THE BLUE MOUNTAINS: WHERE THE OIL TREASURE LIES BURIED.
Photo.—Kerry & Co., George-street, Sydney.
crude oil, it will work for the ideal oil industry, in
which there may be a hundred or a thousand small
men working shale or crude oil plants and selling
their product to the big refinery to be further
manufactured. The crude plants will get the
benefit of the black oil and the ammonia liquid,
and the shale country, which is quite unsuitable
for pastoral or agricultural purposes, and would
otherwise be desert, will be made to support quite
a large population. Later on, when Parliament
has time to go into the tariff again, the bounty
can be made practically self-paying, by a duty of
a penny or two per gallon on imported kerosene.
This would not increase the price to the consumer
and would come absolutely out of the pocket of
the foreign exporter, for if the foreigner raised his
price he would be cutting the throat of his own
trade by encouraging the local industry still
further, and he is not likely to do that.
* * * *
Judging by some of the speeches that one occa-
sionally reads in Hansard, there are still those
among us who may inquire why the com-
munity should subsidise any industry. The
answer is obvious. Australia giving a bounty on
the production of crude oil is like a man investing
money in a paying concern. It pays out its money
and gets in return a thriving industry which will
give work to thousands of men who will pay taxes
through the Customs, 'who will become good
citizens, and form material out of which the Aus-
tralian army of the future will be raised. They
will also create much wealth which will be liable
to taxation and thereby give a direct return for
the bounty. What Australia requires is popula-
tion and more population. At the present time
almost every Australian State has men in Europe
lecturing and exhibiting limelight views of the
country in the hope of inducing people to come
out here and throw in their lot with us.
The establishment of a thriving 'oil, lubricant,
ammonia, coke and paraffin wax industry will
bring more people here in 12 months than a score
of lecturers will in half-a-dozen years. And they
will come to find a lucrative business awaiting
them at which they can start right away and make
money. Even now, in its initial stages, before
the industry has actually started, there are over
2000 men engaged directly in connection with
the shale industry in N.S.W. Under exist-
ing circumstances, they may decrease, certainly
they will not increase; but if the Australian
nation shows by its actions that it is interested in
its shale oil industry, and is determined to
foster it, the number will increase and multiply
rapidly. The operations will become more elabo-
rate.
Up to the present the most of the money
Australia has got out of its shale has been
derived from the export of shale in the crude and
quite raw state, just as the picks of the miners tore
it from the ribs of the Blue Mountains. What
the people who bought it wanted was the gas that
it yielded. And there is no reason why we should
not export the oil instead of the raw shale. If the
oil is made here, the man who hitherto made it else-
where will come to Australia and make it here, and
Australian shopkeepers, manufacturers, newspaper
proprietors, doctors, and so forth will get the
benefit of that money instead of the people in the
same lines of business in Britain and Germany.
It must also be remembered that the State
has a direct interest in the shale industry.
It gets a cash return from it for this reason —that
every ton of shale torn from the ribs of the Blue
Mountains yields the State fid. royalty. And
when the industry is in full blast and a hundred
shale mines are each employing a few score of
miners, ripping out a few million tons per annum,
the shale royalty will represent quite a respect-
able income. Above all, the chief reason why
Australia should grant a bounty to the oil industry
is because it is a good investment. If a
man came to John Smith, and showed him
that by investing £lO,OOO per annum he
could get a steady income of £20,000 per
annum, the wildest Freetrader that ever lived
would pronounce John Smith a fool if he threw
the opportunity away. And yet Australia is in
the position of John Smith. Splendid pioneering
work has been done. The nucleus of a great in-
dustry has been established. The waste places of
the Blue Mountains have been made sources of
great wealth, and all that remains is for Australia
to rise to the occasion and make up its mind that
it will have an oil industry and get into its pocket
the wealth that at present lies amidst the ravines
and gorges of the Great Dividing Range.
* * * *
India, Japan, Russia, Austria, Canada and
France, Germany, Italy, Roumania, the Nether-
lands Indies and other countries have all found it
necessary to protect their oil industries from the
attacks of the great Yankee octopus. Which ex-
plains why a recent writer in the World’s Work
remarked pathetically that, if the Standard Oil
Companies received many more rebuffs like that
experienced in India, several American towns
would go back to the wilderness.
Well, it is better that any number of American
towns should go back to the wilderness than that
the Australian wilderness should never grow a
city or two. We are here to consider Australian and
not American cities. The Standard Trust, which,
with all its faults, and notwithstanding its infamy,
is a monument of splendid organisation and
concentration, has shown the world that a
really great oil industry can be created only by
working on a large scale. The experience of
Scotland, India, British Borneo and Japan has
proved the same truism. And if Australia
desires men to invest large sums of capital
in its oil industry, it must give some encourage-
ment to capitalists to do so. It would be pre-
ferable to see an industry established by small
men, if it were possible. But it isn’t. In the
matter of shale oil we cannot do without the big
capitalist, and when he is necessary he must be
welcomed and encouraged. And in this case the
large capitalist is compelled to bring with him an
army of small men. Which quite removes any
objection which may be held to the importation
of foreign capital. The oil industry is one in
which labor represents everything, and of the
wealth made by a great shale oil industry 90 per
cent, will stay in Australia under even the most
adverse circumstances. We have to chose between
sending our kerosene shale abroad in a crude
state for the workmen of other countries to
get the benefit of manufacturing, oi: of invest-
ing a certain amount of national capital in it
to encourage capitalists to bring their work-
men here and make it, and thereby give
our country the benefit of wages spent in the
manufacture. Considering how urgently neces-
sary it is that Australia should get more Workers*
to swell the population, there can be only one
choice.
Therefore we must do as India, Japan, Russia,
Austria, and the Netherlands Indies have done :
Protect our industry. A stiff duty on imported
oil would have been the best method ; but, owing
to the Tariff having been settled, such a dutv is,
for the time being, impossible. Still, we want
the industry badly, and as, owing to circum-
stances, we cannot protect it with a duty,
it only remains to foster it with a bounty. Our
Australian legislators made a fearful blunder
when they made oil free—on the voices—for the
industry was just about to get its first chance of
real success. Although it does not follow that the
industry will close up if the bounty is not granted, it
is dead certainty that if exposed to the cUt-throat
competition to which the (Standard Oil Trust has
subjected all other competitors in the past, it Vvill
have a hard struggle to maintain itself, and cer-
tainly no chance of expanding and becoming a
world industry, bringing annually great sums of
money to Australia from the oil-burning, countries
of the world. And. there is no use having
the best and largest deposits of oil shale in
the world if we cannot turn them into wealth.
Further, to allow the industry to struggle on
under present conditions is practically to divide
the oil trade of the Commonwealth between a few
great corporations. For unless there is a bounty
to encourage people to develope the industry,
Australia will be at the mercy of the two big cor-
porations which at present face each other, viz.-
the Standard Oil Trust, which employs only dis-
tributing agents and Parliamentary lobbyists in
Australia, and the Shell Transport Co. On theother
hand the newly-created Commonwealth Oil Cor-
poration is at present employing over 2000 Aus
tralians, and has erected a plant and railway and de-
veloped shale mines at a cost of fully half a million.
A bounty, if granted on the production of orudeoil,
will bring others into competition with these
and completely break up any monopoly they
may possibly aim at. It is not probable but quite
possible that under existing circumstances these
two huge concerns might, instead of cutting one
another’s throats, combine to bleed the Australian
consumer, and make him pay through the nose for
not only his kerosene, but his sulphate of ammonia,
his paraffin wax, all his lubricating oils, his
naphtha, benzine, gasolene, vaseline, and all the
other products of the oil industry. With a bounty
on crude oil that would be impossible. A bounty
on the refined oil would not do so. Instead of
developing our own splendid deposits of oil shale
it might mean only the establishment of a few
refineries to manufacture crude oil brought in bulk
from the East Indies, and even far away
America. Paid on the production of crude oil
only, it will encourage only the manufacture of oil
made from Australian shales.
* * * *
At present, and for some time to come, New
South Wales would reap the entire benefit of such a
bounty. But in course of time the deposits of
Queensland and Tasmania would be developed
under the stimulus, and a great export trade such
as that which has brought so mtich wealth
to the United States, be built up. Of course,
there is no need for Australia to pay a bonus
on the oil exported; when the industry has
reached such proportions a bonus will be no longer
necessary, and if the oil bonus is still pay-
able, it can be recovered at the port of ship-
ment. All that is necessary for Australia is
to give such encouragement by means of a bonus
that our oil industry will have a chance to develop
its potentialities, and, when that is done, our
natural advantages are such that our oilmen can
fight the great Yankee octopus single-handed and
without State aid.
Be it therefore remembered :
That without a bonus on crude shale oil, or
duties on all shale products, Australia must
remain without an Oil Industry.
That without an Oil Industry at least 10,000
artisans must go without employment and about
two millions of Australian gold will continue to
leave Australia yearly, mostly for America, the
great bulk of it’ into" the maw of the voracious
Standard Oil Trust.
Without an Oil Industry, and with the largest
and richest shale area known on the globe, Aus-
tralia makes itself the laughing stock of the world,
except, of course, that part of it engaged in dump-
ing oils into the country. While it receives the
applause of the Standard Oil Trust, a picture of
Rockefeller, having a quiet laugh up his sleeve,
would not be out of place.
With a bonus or tariff a Shale Industry will be
established in different parts of N.S.W. by leaps
and bounds, also by gradations in Queensland
where there are known to be immense shale
deposits, Tasmania and other places. The N.S.W.
Ooke Industry has been established and is
to-day protected by 4s. a ton duty, which
is equal to 30 per cent, of the value. (The
recent contract to dump 10,000 tons of German
surplus coke into South Australia alarmed even
the Sydney Freetrade papers.) ■ And if the simple
act of turning Australian coal into coke has
already justified a duty of 30 per cent., surely the
turning of Australian shale into oil, ammonia, and
then into ammonia sulphate, into paraffin wax,
then into candles, and many other things : refining
the oil into kerosene, benzine, naphtha, motor
spirit and various other oils and greases —surely
the equal certainty of establishing this infinitely
greater industry warrants twopence a gallon bonus
on crude oil production.
In fostering the Australian Oil Industry by Pro-
tection, the Commonwealth is setting no prece-
dent, only following the example of Canada,
India, Japan, France, Germany, Russia, Spain
and other countries—all except bonnie Scotland
where, ye ken, the Oil Industry was founded
many years before oil was “struck” in the
TJnitedStates and over a quarter of a century be-
fore the Standard Oil tentacles were fully de-
veloped—hence only a few Scotch refineries
have survived England’s Freetrade and America’s
octopus —proof that Protection and eternity are
the only fair handicaps when an industry is up
against American Trust Oils.
, With our own Oil Industry, numerous new
shale mines will be at work, employing many
hundreds of miners to raise the million odd tons
of shale required for Australia’s kerosene con-
sumption alone, and the million odd other tons
wanted for other shale and oil products; some
thousands of men, women, boys and girls will find
work in the innumerable branches of the industry,
and the Factories Act and other industrial laws
will see that honest wages are paid them.
An impetus, if not a boom, will be given to
shale areas of N.S.W. if the proposed bonus on
the production of shale oil is passed, whereas, if
rejected, these Blue Mountain shale areas, which
cannot carry a rabbit to the acre, will remain the
desolate wilderness predicted for the oil towns of
the Middle West of U.S.A. if the Standard
Trust does not win its fight everywhere. The
Federal Parliament will soon be called upon
to determine between allowing these mountains,
canyons, and gorges to remain undisturbed in
their natural barrenness, the home of a restricted
number of wallabies, or turning the desolate
wastes into prosperous communities, the home of
thousands of Australian workers; building new
railways, retorting and refining plants, and other-
wise finally banishing the Standard Oil trust
from our then deservedly-named Commonwealth.
Meanwhile, let every reader of The Bulletin
keep at least one watchful eye on the votes and
voices, and be ready to put a big black inefface-
able mark against the name of every political
traitor to Australian interests and every Parlia-
mentary partisan of the Standard Oil trust, so
that mark and man will be remembered till the
Day of Judgment.
C.A. JREFRIES .

The Day Our Flag Comes Down.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The day Australia’s ensign falls—when Yellow
rags are floating
Above our Yellow Guv’nor’s halls, where Yellow
aides are gloating
Upon the goods we’ve left behind ; when we’re a
Yellow nation
With Yellow bosses set to mind the fat, well-found
sheep-station
And run Australian farms; when cheap white-
trash in bonds is striving
To earn its dole of rice, and keep its Yellow
• master thriving,
What warning will the Mongol’s crimes bring
home to us in Sorrow ?
What but the war-sign of our times? “Shem
will be here to-morrow ! ’’
That day coarse Yellow lips will tell a lovely tale
—of beauty
ihat we, dead rebels, loved so well before we died
for d u ty ;
Of snowy-limbed Australian girls that Yellow
eyes lust after—
Who shrink in horrible alarm from sounds of
beastly laughter
And Yellow hands that reach. . . . and hold. . . .
and drag their chasteness, screaming,
Down to black deeps of shame untold where foul
things lie, drug-dreaming.
In that wild, dreadful grab they’ll kind of hustle
one another—
In beauty-trade he’s far from blind, our gentle
Yellow brother !
.The day they hoist the Yellow flag where once
Australia’s floated,
The pages of each groaning Torv rag will be
devoted
To Yellow politics and news of Yellow war suc-
cesses,
And crawling, Yellow Tory views of how kind
Yellow blesses
Thfe people it appears to crush ! Our trams like
monkey-cages
Will seem, when comes that sudden rush of
Yellow in all stages ;
The pleasant tea-rooms on our Block will lose
white Lil and Lizzie,
And keep, each day at four o’clock, new Yellow
tea-girls busy.
When our good colors waken mirth on countless
Yellow faces,
Oh, we will turn beneath the earth that hides our
poor soul-cases
While, up and down, the waster few, their useless
arms discarded,
Go cursing them in language blue—in language
warm but guarded !
And take, their Yellow fear to drown, some
Yellow boozing-lotion
As slow the Yellow sun goes down behind a
Yellow ocean.
The warning’s written large and clear, a doom of
wrath and sorrow
And endless woe : “ Shem will he here, with ships
and guns , to-morrow /”
VIO,
FRANK HENTY.
This, from a correspondent at Kobe, throws some
light on a cable received in Australia on the last
day of August reporting that Japan had decided
to cut down its expenditure by £2O 000 000
looping 30 per cent, off the army and 10 per cent!
off the navy estimates:—
Events have culnuuacec) in the dissolution of the Saionji
Ministry. It had proved itself unequal to the financial
crisis which confronted it. For some months its awkward
manipulation of national finance has been the talk of the
country’s leading financiers, and, indeed, of the leading
economists of the world. The Finance Minister was
changed, but no improvement ensued. Notwithstanding
the large sums accruing* to the national exchequer from
the war fund, notwithstanding* an increase in war taxes
and Customs duties, notwithstanding* the sale of mono-
polies, the debt continued to grow, the industrial and
commercial conditions of the country were seriously
hampered, and credit abroad was greatly impaired. The
Government made an attempt to offset the despondency of
the people in not receiving the anticipated indemnity
from Kussia by launching out into enormous post helium
operations, instead of curtailing expenses and adjustin'*
financial responsibilities, already incurred ; and these
extraordinary undertakings of the Government set in
circulation an abnormal volume of borrowed money and
produced a fever of enterprise which resulted in reckless
speculation and finally in disaster. To make matters
worse, instead of paying off in ready money the big war-
loan of 100,000,000 yen recently due, the Government
instituted an unpopular system of competitive discounts
that materially disturbed banking business and threw
some institutions into liquidation. So the retirement of
the Cabinet is everywhere regarded as a relief
When the Marquis- Saionji handed in his resignation to
the Emperor, he recommended Marquis Katsura as his
successor; and the whole nation agreed that there was
but one man available, for the people had not forgotten
his consummate leadersn.p m the struggle with Russia
nor his resignation only out or sympathy with the nation
that resented the terms of peace. He is hailed now as
just the man to meet the present financial crisis, and at
the same time to be in rational sympathy with the more
moderate of the militarists. He is expected to be heartily
in favor of the new policy of curtailing naval and military
expenditure until there is an improvement of the financial
situation ; and his tolerance and tact will make for a har-
monious co-operation among the members of the Cabinet
Yet in surveying tine personnel of the Cabinet it must be
borne constantly in mind that the destinies of the
nation are ultimately in the hands of the Genro or
‘Elder Statesmen,” the guardians of the “patriotic

Some music never grows old, particularly
if it recalls pleasant memories. The Edison
Phonograph can reproduce for you the
marches, ballads and airs that stirred you
in the old days just as well as it can sing
the song that is the current hit of the
music halls.
Tt. gives them all with a clearness, a
fidelit} 7 and a beauty and volume of sound
that is not to be found in any similar
instrument.
The Edison Phonograph is all things to
all men at all times. Simply by changing
a record it may be a brass band at one
minute and a violin virtuoso the next ; a
singer of rag-time or of grand opera ; a
funny vaudeville team, or a quartette
singing a pretty madrigal.
Write for lists of records and machines—
then buy an Edison for the Summer time.

“ Oby ” writes :
Some little country shows that seek Government sub-
sidies want careful looking' after. Hearing of the doings
of Sydney’s Agricultural Association, numbers of one-
horse townships are stirred to hold a meeting and start
an agricultural show of their own. The cocky show
seldom does any good to agriculture. The affair is simply
a mutual cocky admiration society. An investigation
some years ago squashed several of these enterprises, but
another overhaul is wanted to-day. N.S. W. estimates of
1892 provided for a grant of £2OO for an inlahd pastoral
and agricultural society, which consisted of five persons.
They paid nothing towards the establishment or upkeep
of their society, but simply whacked up the £2oo—£4o
each.
“ Tema, ” from a back street close to Spats’
factory :
I happened across what was nearly a tragedy. An un-
offending soul in the shape of a cheap jack took his
stand in a byway near a warehouse and proceeded to talk
some. Gradually people drew round and were treated
to a free lecture on pen-nibs and tin-openers. At this
juncture out trotted a number of factory toilers for their
dinner vacation, accompanied by the inevitable football.
The space was limited, and after a while it was plain that
trouble was brewing if somebody didn’t shift; but the
tin-opener purveyor was on good money, so he risked it.
The ball started rolling with about 20 budding
Messengers behind, and the first rush ended in a field
goal kicked close over the heads of the audience. Pen-
nibs cursed, but stood his ground. The team formed a
scrum, then broke, and the next rush was disastrous.
Ginger collared the ball and went for a try. With the
balance of the team at his heels he made a vicious rush
for the nib stand, ducked between the pedlar’s legs, and
precipitated him in a heap on the footpath. The crowd
opened out and Ginger grounded the ball amid trium-
phant yells, with the rest of the team falling all over him.
On recovering from the shock, the itinerant cove arose
hurriedly, viewed his scattered wares, grabbed a tin-
opener and pounced on the malefactors. He managed
to get hold of the ball and drove the weapon clean
through the cause of the trouble. The-team got angry
and formed up to charge, but a limb of the law arrived in
time to save the situation. Ginger collected the
punctured leather, and a sad individual gathered up
sundry household articles and departed for a less strenu-
ous atmosphere.

BRONCHITIS
CURE.
The Famous Remedy for
Coughs, Bronchitis, Pneumonia,
Pleurisy, Asthma <fe Consumption
BRONCHITIS and PNEUMONIA
Cured by
HEARNE’S BRONCHITIS CURE
After other Treatment had Failed.
Mr. W. G, Hearne. Dear Sir, —From a strict sense
of duty I feel that I should publish the following state-
ment, so that others may know what to do when the
awful fact is evident that a life is in danger of being
lost: —In September, 1906, my little girl, aged at that
time 3 years, contracted Measles, and in the following
October was attacked by Bronchitis, Pneumonia and
Congestion of the Lungs. She was attended by a
legally qualified doctor of high standing, but his treat-
ment was not successful in arresting the progress of the
illness. On Saturday, the 21st October, 1906, he said
that her life was in danger—that there was very little
hope for her. For eight days and nights she had been
prostrated by Cough, Pain and Fever, and was lying
like a statue, unconscious. At this stage I was per-
suaded by a friend to obtain Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure,
with its auxiliary medicine for the Fever and Conges-
tion of the Lungs, as directed in the Catalogue of
Medicines which accompanies each bottle of the Bron-
chitis Cure. I gave the medicine as directed, and there
was an improvement from the first dose of Hearne’s
Bronchitis Cure. The improvement continued after
each dose of the medicine. In a week she was perfectly
free from the Pneumonia, Congestion, Cough, Pain and
Fever, and was well, except that she was still weak. In
a fortnight she was quite recovered, and is now in
splendid health, and stronger than ever. Any person
asking for information about this grand medicine can
be supplied by me, or by any of my neighbours who have
witnessed its wonderful effects. It absolutely snatched
my child from an early grave.—Yours gratefully,
D. GARDINER,
Police Station, Geelong East, Feb. 5, 1908.
BRONCHITIS.
A Sufferer 73 Years of Age.
Thoroughly Cured by Two Bottles of
Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure.
Immediate Relief-Effect Wonderful.
Mr. Hearne. Sir, —I was very ill with Influenza
and Bronchitis. A friend of mine persuaded me to try
your Bronchitis Cure. The first dose gave me imme-
diate relief, and after taking the second bottle I am
thoroughly cured. Its effect on me has been most
wonderful. I am 73 years of age. I trust you will
make use of this statement by publishing it for the bene-
fit of humanity Yours most respectfully,
THOMAS R. TRF.ZISE,
Reedy Creek, Victoria.
PNEUMONIA and PLEURISY
cured by
HEARNE’S BRONCHITIS CURE
After other Treatment had Failed.
I, Prudence M‘Kee, of Carr-street, South Geelong, in
view of the importance of a person making it quite clear
what treatment was successful in curing a serious and
complicated case when the medicine, directions and treat-
ment of a legally qualified doctor had failed, state as
follows:
My son, Henry M'Kee, then aged 8 years, had been
attended by a legally qualified doctor, who pronounced
him to be suffering from Pneumonia, Pleurisy and a
stoppage of the passing of Urine. Under the doctor’s
treatment the child gradually got worse, and the doctor
pronounced the case hopeless. He told me that the
child could not live. At this stage I obtained from
Mr. W. G. Hearne, Chemist, of Geelong, a bottle of
Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure, and gave it to the child, ac-
cording to the directions which accompany each bottle
of it. The child improved after the second dose of
Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure. He continued to improve
each day from each dose of Hearne’s Medicine alone, and
within three days he was free from the Cough, Pneu-
monia and the Pleurisy, and the Urine was passing satis-
factorily. He was out of bed at the end of a week,
completely recovered, and he is now in perfect health.
PRUDENCE M‘KEE,
Carr-street, South Geelong, Feb. 6, 1908.
CONSUMPTION.
Too 111 to Leave His Bed.
A Complete Cure.
Mr. W. G. Hearne.
Dear Sir,- —I am writing to tell you about the wonder-
ful cure your medicine has effected in my case. About
three years ago I began to cough. At first the cough
was not severe, but it gradually got worse, and I became
very weak and troubled with night sweats, pain in my
chest, and great quantities of phlegm. On several oc-
casions there was blood in the expectorated matter. I
had been treated by a doctor who pronounced my case to
be consumption, and various other treatments had been
tried, but without benefit. It was at this stage that I
heard of your Bronchitis Cure, and sent to you for a
course of the medicine. When it arrived, I was too ill
to leave my bed, but I commenced taking it at once, and
gradually improved. lam glad to say that the two
lots of medicine you sent have effected a complete cure,
for which accept my very best thanks. —Yours gratefully,
I. BLAIR.
Westminster Bridge-road, S.E., London.
BRONCHITIS and PLEURISY.
A Severe Case Cured by Two Bottles of
Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure.
After Other Treatment had Failed.
Mr. Hearne, Chemist, Geelong.
Dear Sir, —Some months ago, in Sydney, I suffered
from a severe attack of influenza, and was confined to
my room for about a week, at the end of which time,
feeling somewhat better, I got up and tried to transact
my business as usual. But I got up too soon, for the
very next day I had a relapse, and suffered tortures from
what the doctor told me was pleurisy and bronchitis.
The pain from the former in my chest and shoulders
was frightful, and for four long weeks I was confined to
my bed under the care of a well-known Sydney doctor,
and all the time his medicine gave me but temporary
relief. The landlady of the hotel (the Cleveland), where
I resided, told me of a medicine —Hearne’s Bronchitis
Cure —from Victoria, which had cured her of a bad at-
tack of bronchitis and pains in the chest, and begged
of me to try it. I did so, and, in thanks and gratitude
to you, tell you that, after the second bottle, my cough
had ceased; but what is more astonishing, the pains from
pleurisy entirely left me, and in about a week I was able
to attend to my duties as usual.—Yours faithfully.
J. BRAHAM.
Melbourne “ Punch ” Office, Melbourne.
ASTHMA-A 17 YEARS CASE.
Previous Treatment Failed.
Cured by Three Bottles,
Mr. Alex. J. Anderson, of Oak Park, Charleville,
Queensland, wrote: —“After suffering from asthma for
seventeen years, and having been under a great many
different treatments without benefit, I was induced to try
Hearne’s Medicine for Asthma. After taking three
bottles of this medicine I quite got rid of the asthma,
and since then, which was the beginning of 1883—fifteen
years ago—l have not had the slightest return of it.
The medicine quite cured me, and I have much pleasure
in recommending it.”
Speaking in February, 1908, he states: —“I am keeping
very well. Never have the slightest return of the asthma.”
“ Hearne’s Medicine cured me of Asthma, from which
I had been suffering for twenty-five years, during which
time I had used almost every patent medicine on the
market —including asthma inhalations—without getting a
cure. It was 8 years ago that the cure was effected
by Hearne’s Medicine, and I now feel stronger than I
have felt for years—in fact. I feel splendid.”
C. WISEMAN,
Meredith, Victoria.

Doom.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
OUT of my house at evenfall I came,
Burned onward with a dim perpetual
flame
Of old disquiet, new awakened
By some far voice of the embalmed dead
That look upon me round about my room.
Out of my house of stone at dusk I came,
And standing, gazed upon the street of stone,
A flare of traffic in the night’s wide gloom.
With still feet on the footpath, I alone
Watched Sydneyside go by me like a dream.
From flare to flare sped jarring labor on,
With grinding wheels, sharp footfalls, clattering
hooves.
So thought I then. I, standing silent, seem
The only one whom no commotion moves ;
And the whole vast of murmurings was gone
And a new multitude along the grooves
Of hurrying custom ground, and light and sound,
Heaved like a curving wave upon my brain.
My eager feet devoured uneven ground,
Out of its passing swoon the old grey pain
Raised up its serpent head and hissed at me,
And I was ringed with midnight and the sea.
The black unfathomed wave on Bondi rolled,
Eternally murmuring the answer old
To the old riddle of humanity.
“Peace and unrest, peace and unrest,” it saith,
“Mortality shall solve that riddle yet.”
But now and then a gust of petulant breath
Blew in on me, and all that vast profound
Roared in a storm of half articulate sound
Furying that humanity, beset
With tiny snares, drones on. And blinded eyes
Feeding on'knowledge care not to be wise,
And the profoundest mysteries forget.
“Doom!” the incessant surges murmured,
“ Doom !
I am thy judge, the hoary, inveterate sea,
And if mankind find not my harmony,
Dissolving in the vast it must make room
For one more huge and warring energy
That yet shall bud from out this withered bloom.”
The strange star-fires that wander in the deep,
The crying wind that swings about the steep
Storm-beaten headlands, and the moon-white
sand
That like a goddess lies along the land
In dreamless sleep, all murmured, “Hear thy
doom.”
N.S.W.
H. M. GREEN.
Captain Grain’s Revenge.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ HERE’S more goes on at sea nor
I what Lloyd’s know, nor what gets into
the papers,” said the big, pock-marked
Irish sailor. “ Oi’ve followed the sea for twinty
y ears _Oi’ve sailed over ut, and swum in ut, and
fought on ut, and broken eight of the Tin Com-
mandments on ut. The other two Oi’ve broke on
land. But that’s by the way—an’ Oi wance had
a touch o’ the sun at Port Said.
“What did Oi say about Lloyd’s? Oi’m cornin’
to ut. Did yous iver hear iv the thramp steamer
Sarah Springhall? No? An’ yet the affair was
cabled all over the world, an’ come out in all the
papers from Archangel to Melbourne town—that
is, parts of ut—an’ for the rest ivery sailor-man
on ivery sea an’ in ivery pub knows what become
of Captain Larry Grain, an’ why we done ut.
“‘The steamer Sarah Springhall has bin
wrecked,’ said the papers, ‘ upon the Albalones, a
desolate group of islands near the northern coast
of Formosa. Out of a crew of thirty-one men
only six were saved. It is conjectured that the
captain, being prevented from taking solar obser-
vations by the thick weather, had ignored, in his
dead reckoning, the set of the current toward the
group.’ There now ! An’ that’s all you know,
an’ that’s all Lloyd’s know. An’ Captain Grain,
him who made the error in his dead reckoning,
can’t say aught else now, because he’s dead and
damned. But the common sailor folk know,
because Oi told some, an’ George Paston told a
few more when the dhrink was in him.
“ We had a nigger on board when we started
from Melbourne that thrip—the skipper picked
him up dhrunk one night outside Antonio’s,
where the Russians an’ the Dagoes an’ the Dutch-
men an’ the Bluenoses all booze together quiet an’
friendly. Moind, Oi don’t think ’twas all his
fault in the long run, but ut all come about by
what me and Captain Grain done to him. An’
yet we had to do something when we found out,
which we did on the fourteenth day. We’d
dhropped Townsville then, an’ the Cross was
hangin’ very low down astern. Oi remember
that because when the splash But that comes
afther.
“ This nigger had a disease that no man names
when it gets as bad as he had it. The men
thought ut was smallpox, but Oi knew what ut
was, havin’ had a mate that died of ut in China.
You know what Oi mane—the Chinayse disease—
an’ ivery sailor man knows, and the sickly little
clerruks out iv the shops an’ warehouses say they
know, an’ laugh about ut. But they don t if
they saw ut they’d die iv the fear. Oi wandher a
bit, p’raps, but Oi always get back. _ Don’t moind
me’. Oi had a touch o’ the sun in Port Said,
wance. They say Oi’m a bit quare. Well, ’tis
quare things Oi’ve seen.
“ The captain says to me, ‘Mick, what ails that
son-of-a-gun of a nigger that he’s lyin’ in, an’ the
men are growlin’?’ Oi told him. ‘He’ll niver
work more, captain,’ says Oi. What, says he,
‘ is he that bad ? Shipped aboard my ship to loaf
the whole voyage, an’ maybe die on my hands
blast him ! ’ He looked out over the sea, tuggin’
at his beard. ‘ What are the men sayin’, Mick ? ’
“‘The men are sayin’ that if he don’t die or
get shifted from the fo’c’s’le they’ll stop work, sir,’
SrIVS Ol*
“ ‘ Where the Hell am Oi to shift him ? ’ says he.
“‘Don’t know, sir,’ says Oi. An’ he looked
me square in the eye an’ Oi looked at him, an’ he
looked out to sea again.
“ ‘ He’ll die soon, eh, Mick?’ says he—by the Lord
he was a hard-moinded divil was Captain Grain.
An’ afther he said Oi had misundherstood. What
would you have undherstood ? ‘ He’ll die soon,
eh Mick?’ What ilse could ut mane, f’r the love
° f my touch o’ the sun has made me a bit
wake in the head—Oi may not have got Captain
Grain right. But over the nigger went that night,
an’ Oi reminiber how the reflections of the Gross,
low down, shivered in the splash he made. .
Dead ! Well, perhaps he was. The shadows and
the lift o’ the wave might have desaved me, an
his arms mightn't have moved. An’—ut may have
bin only the scream of an albatross Oi heard. Oi
wasn’t froightened to handle him, though few
others would have done ut. An’ he was only a
nigger, afther all.
“ An’ then Oi went below. ‘ The nigger’s dead,
sir,’ Oi said to Captain Grain.
“ ‘ Is he ? ’ says he.
“ ‘ He is,’ says Oi.
“ ‘ Overboard ? ’ says he.,
“ ‘ Overboard,’ says Oi.
“ ‘ Take a dhrop,’ says he, pushin’ over a bottle
o’ squareface.
“ Oi took wan.
“ An’ then, at Singapore, they took me an’
Captain Grain ashore, because the other men got
talkin’ in the pubs, an’ they put us on our thrial.
An’ we were acquitted, an’ the captain went back
to the ship wid the black hate in his heart against
the men whose jaws had got loose and risked his
neck. What was the charge? ’Twas said we
murdhered the nigger. Yes, by law we were
acquitted, and the crew got Hell when we stood
to sea again. One man said he smelt death in the
ship—y’ know there’s a sort of quare, musty smell
in a vessel that’ll niver reach port again. An’ all
excep’ me swore they’d desert when we got to
Vladivostock —if we ever got there.
“Up along Formosa it come on to blow as
though the Devil had pulled all the strings of all
the winds at wance, an’ when the wind fell, lavin’
a heavy sea, the sun was hid in a thick white mist
—white as the face of a dead man—an’ the cor-
posant rode on the masthead at night. Have you
ever seen the corposant? Man, ’tis an awful
thing—a great blue ball of fire with a little fierce
spot in the midst loike the eye of a devil. An’ it
glows, an’ glows, an’ makes the men’s faces all blue
against the dark sea racin’ beyant the sidelights.
An’ one of our deck hands swore he saw the face
of the nigger grinnin’ out of this wan—he was a
superstitious sort of chap. Oi’m superstitious too,
but Oi wouldn’t be froightened if all the ghosts in
the sea come out of it. Oi’ve seen a good many,
too.
“So we butted through it with the deathlight
on the fore, an’ at the dark hour before dawn, me
an’ Captain Grain bein’ alone (on deck on the
bridge we were) he spoke to me.
“ ‘Those swine below near hanged us, Mick,’he
says.
“ ‘ Yes,’ says Oi.
“‘By God! there they are, some in the
stokehole, an’ some in their bunks, an’ Oi have
their lives in my hand.’ He looked up at the
deathlight, an’ Oi thought by his eyes he was mad.
Oi think now he was. Mad or reckless—’tis the
same thing, wid p’raps a sloight bit of difference
on the Day of Judgment. He alone knows now,
but p’raps some day when Oi go where he’s gone
he’ll tell me amid the flames.
“‘D’ye hear the breakers?’ he says. Oi
listened, an’ Oi heard them plain.
“ ‘ What are breakers doin’ here?’ Oi asked
him.
“ ‘ Hark !’ says he.
“A boom like the echo of a field gun in the
hills tould me we were right on top of whatever it
was. ‘ Captain, for the love of Mary ! ’ says Oi.
“‘’Tis the Albalone Rocks,’ he said.
‘Dhrowned loike the swine ye are ! ’ he went on,
glarin’ down as though he saw the men sweatin’
at the furnaces deep in the belly of the ship.
“‘You’re not goin’ to wreck her,’ Oi said.
An’ ‘ Wait an’ see,’ says he. What was Oi to
do ? Captain Grain had tould me to wait. Oi
waited.
“ She sthruck loightly on wan wavefall, an’ rose
again on the send of the next. Then we shot in
against a broken wall of white, wid teeth of black
stickin’ out of ut an’ bitin’ through the plates.
‘There!’ laughs the captain. ‘The dogs—Oi’ve
dhrowned them.’
“ ‘You’ve dhrowned us all,’ says Oi.
“‘What the Hell does it matter?’ he sneers.
‘ We’i\none of us fit to live. Who heaved the
nigger o'ser ? ’
“ Wid that he cloimbed down from the bridge,
an’ Oi sat down an’ buried my head in my hamds,
cryin’ at his words. D’you think ut was fair to
say that to me that had done what Oi did for him ?
Oi may be a bad wan—Oi may be a bit quare in
the head —Oi had a touch o’ the sun wance, you
know. But Oi think that was ingratitude. An
he thought so too, because, at the end, he looked
at me, him bein’ bound an’ ready—‘Ye poor, mad
divil,’ he said. ‘Oi was hard on you too.’ Those
were his very words—would Oi be forgettin’ them ?
“ The seas broke over the Sarah Springhall an’
flung the men off to their death as they come on
deck. Oi called out to two of thim that Captain
Grain —God forgive me, Oi thought he was dead—
had dhrowned them for their talk in Singapore
about the nigger, an’ one, George Paston, cloimbed
up on the bridge, an’ Oi tould him all there was
to tell, an’ laughed in his face as the bridge swung
over an’ spilled us off, an’ the sea took us. Oi
think the Captain must have gone overboard as
soon as he reached the deck.
“Thirty-wan men there were on board, an’
seven only came up on the beach aloive, for there
was a beach behoind the ring of rocks. An’ of
that seven Oi was wan, and George Paston was
another. There was only six at first. We found
each other by callin’, an’ Oi tould them all what
Oi’d tould Paston—what the Captain had done,
an’ what a brave man he was, an’ what dogs they
were, an’, loike dogs, they snarled at me. But
George Paston kept them off me. * He’s half mad,
boys,’ he said. An’ below, in the grey dawn, we
saw the bodies of some of the rest washing up an’
down in the foam on the beach, with their hair
streamin’ loike seaweed, an’ their faces loike wax.
“One body lay high up between two little
rocks, an’ when we went down to ut we saw ut
was Captain Grain. As we looked he moved a
little. Oi went to take him up, but the rest pulled
me away. ‘To Hell with him—chuck him back !’
they cried. But George Paston stopped them.
‘No; wait till he can feel ut,’says he. By-and-
bye he opened his eyes. Then the others tied his
hands an’ feet, an’ carried him, me cryin’ behind
them, to a high place over the sea. An’ here they
laid him down on the crackly, wind-dried grass.
“ * Now,’ said one, ‘ we’ll pay Captain Grain for
24 men.’
“ ‘ I’ll pay for my sister’s man,’ said another.
“ ‘And I for my brother’s son,’ said another.
“‘And I for a 10-years mate,’ said George
Paston. ‘ An’ Mick will pay for all—and the
nigger. Take him up, Mick ! ’ #
“Oi lifted him, an’ he smiled at me out o’ his
battered face, an’ said what Oi tould you.
“ ‘Oi can’t,’ Oi said, an’ laid him down again.
“ ‘Then you go, too,’ said George Paston.
“ ‘ Don’t be a fool,’ said Captain Grain.
“Well —not a sound out of him as he fell. It
was a horrible distance, but he was a brave man.
Oi should have shrieked. Oi can see him turnin’
over an’ and over now, an’ the waves leapin’ up
to meet him.”

iMea/is
a
Chance.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SO it seems, looking hack, as if Chance did all,
That the scroll was not written beyond
recall
Long ere we knew.
That our love was not part of a pattern planned ;
Blind Chance but lifted a listless hand
And gave me you.
As I stood, so it seemed, on the beach of Night,
Some soft thirtg, wonderful, helpless, white,
Passively sweet,
Meek spoil that the somnolent Ocean gave,
Chance-flung on a dead, chance-lifted wave,
Lay at my feet.
No ! Destiny marked us each for each ;
Fate flung you, helpless, within my reach ;
But I was wise.
This strange sea-wrack before me thrown
I might have passed, unknowing, unknown,
With blinded eyes.
But did I not in the Infinite
Some soft thing, wonderful, glimmering white,
Strangely discern ?
And did not you, in the far Sea-drift,
To the shore where I waited your eyes uplift
And toward me yearn ?
Was it careless Chance that drew my feet
Beneath the low moon your soul to meet
By the Ocean’s rim ?
I had always dreamed of you, lost, alone,
And, but half-recalled, I had always known
A secret dim. . .
And, somewhere afar, or perilous-near,
Your low voice calling I seemed to hear;
But ah ! your name?
Yet I knew that this loneliness was not all,
And eternally craved, and at last my call
You heard—and came !
For in lives long dead I knew your eyes ;
And my love is but that I recognise,
Remembering.
And I could wait—as in lives before—
Till the loyal years to me once more
Your soul would bring.
Thus, thus, I remember your dear eyes shone !
In the twilight time of the ages gone
Did our love begin !
On no hazardous currents of aimless seas,
But the long sure surge of the centuries,
You drifted in !
N.S.W.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
The Killers.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I HIT some big fishing in October last.
Picture 1 was a squad of killers rounding
up a black whale inside the heads of Twofold
Bay(N.S.W.). A mountainous sea was running
in from the nor’-east; a wet, white sky and blind-
ing rain squalls kept us moist behind the ears.
Across the hills of water plunged the narrow-
beaked whaleboat; behind throbbed a 50ft. launch,
lifebuoys at hand, emergency spears and harpoon
gun stowed for’ard. At the wheel stood a hawk-
eyed youngster from Eden, who has steered
through the death-wash of a dozen black whales.
Everywhere ahead the squall-rent sea was white
and full of ravenous hollows eager to engulf us.
The half-castes in the boat pulled like devils.
Mardoo, and Greig the harpooher, taunted them
with the word “ slowbacks,” and they responded
by shooting farther ahead of the motor-driven
launch.
Where were the killers ? One man hinted that
they were sulking near the South Head and
wouldn’t work. It was stated that a new-chum
half-caste had struck one with an oar a week or so
before —a criminal offence not likely to be
repeated in the presence of an Eden whaler. For
the porpoise-like whale-sleuths of Twofold Bay
know their own worth, and the slightest insult to
their dignity is never forgotten, as the men from
East Boyd know. Without their immediate assist-
ance there would be little or no work for the
score of men who live by the bones and oil taken
from the wandering cetaceans.
A hundred yards ahead through the sheeted
rain the water became suddenly flurried, as
though a marine ploughshare were slicing the sea
in halves—it was the wake of the killers travelling
in triangular formation in front of the boat. They
sighted the launch, and were gone in a flash. But
not for long.
A hoarse, siren-like bellow filled the salt-way,
unutterably mournful and sad, and—“ Look,
look, for the Lord’s sake ! ”
I glanced beyond the harpooner’s pointed finger
and saw It through the grey sheen of a three-
fathom wave, trudgeoning from side to side,
rolling its giant shoulders like a wounded ship.
And the cry that 'came from its vitals suggested
the trumpet of the Resurrection.
The killers were everywhere now, like dingoes
around a hurt steer, • charging in under the ex-
posed fin, snapping, rending the great black sides
with torpedo swiftness. A pair of veteran killers,
nicknamed “Jim” and “Barney,” shot under
the heaving mass; a moment after the water
grew red where their jaws clung and severed huge
strips of blubber from the cetacean’s side.
“These Eden killers on’y want a stockwhip
behind ’em,” shouted the captain as “Jim ” and
“ Barney ” rose to the surface rending their strips
of blubber to pieces.
The black whale rolled and vanished until the
relentless killers worried it to the surface. And so
the fight went on until the harpooners finished the
hard-driven leviathan with spear and toggle-iron.
Whale-anchors were attached to the dead ceta-
cean to keep it from drifting out to sea. The
great head sunk far into the water. Within 24
hours, the accumulating gases would again lift it,
balloon-like, to the surface. It would then be towed
to the trying-out station at Kiah, East Boyd
The whalers’ quarters are far more comfortable
than any shearers’ huts I ever saw. The tucker
ranges from boiled schnapper, trevalli, baked
bream and mullet to mutton and fresh beef and
hunks of Bega cheese.
The whaling crew, mostly half-castes, laughed
heartily over certain portions of Bullen’s “Cruise
of the Cachalot.”
“Ef Bullen ever helped to cut up a right whale,
or a humpy or a finback, he’d know better than
say they feed on pilchards,” said the harpooner,
huskily. 1
I pressed him to explain.
“ it’s this way,” he continued. “ When a
whale’s feedin’ on britt—it floats like a prawny
weed on the water—a few fish get entangled, an’
the whale strains ’em through its jaws. Chaps
like Bullen sees a few fish in the beard, an’ think
it’s been eatin’ em. Rot! Now, ef Frank had
ever helped to cut up a whale, he’d never have
made that statement in the ‘ Cruise of the
Cachalot.’ ”
I hinted that Bullen’s books had passed muster
throughout the world without adverse comment.
“ That’s so,” grunted the harpooner. “There
are about six people, between South Ameriky an’
Norfolk Island who’ve looked into a whale’s
gullet. I don’t know how many there are up
North. You’ll admit, though,” lie said dryly,
“ that the critic fellers don’t go whalin’ between
drinks.”
After tea Captain Davidson showed me a sheaf
of bone and oil receipts from various Australian
firms, which proved beyond doubt that the old
harpooner Greig was responsible for the lives of
187 black or right whales.
And this happens in Australia where men are
always writing about cows and sheep !
ALBET DORRINGTON.
The Thing.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WITH a hunch of diaper tucked
beneath his chin the heavy person in
evening dress violently attacked a stack
of French peas.
He had come in late, and even at this stage his
second pint of wine was under Percy’s care.
The room was half-filled with diners.
The man sat near the door. Far down the
room, near the street windows, another man sat
with a woman. The woman, rose-cheeked with
wine and frocked in conspicuous clothes, found
her greatest pleasure 'in frequent sips from her
glass.
The stout man’s gaze was every now and again
upon the woman. Her back was turned to him,
but her companion, sitting against the wall, could
view the whole room.
The pea-eater ate and drank with no regard for
the nerves of his neighbors. He kept on with his
meal, stolidly, and commenced his second pint,
every now and then letting his gaze wander to
the couple at the top of the room. He was a
quick feeder, and arrived at his brandy while the
woman was eating a pink ice.
Shortly he opened his case and took out a fat,
black cigar.
Percy dashed eagerly up with a taper, and the
man sat back and blew up a cloud of pungent
smoke.
Presently he took his bill, and slipped it into
his vest-pocket, putting a coin on the table for
Percy. Percy immediately seized upon the stout
one’s coat and hat, and lay in wait for him by the
doorway, full of doglike attention.
The large man got up and walked down the
room towards the couple.
Percy watched, with a sparkling eye, and
people half-turned in their seats to covertly re-
gard the monster who strode among them.
He came up to the couple, his eyes upon th,e
man. The man suddenly knew fear, and his
fingers stole towards his wineglass, but not to
carry drink.
Gooch, the fat man, stepped up, and the woman
noted the quick pallor of her companion. She
turned, very swift and still, and without shrink-
ing. Gooch took the cigar out of his mouth, the
tip of it burning bright crimson as he did so. He
bent his face to the woman’s, with his hands be-
hind him, as though to disclaim any intention of
personal violence.
The woman—not moving from him, for there
were so many others in the room ! —caught at the
edge of the white table-cover, and moaned, under
her breath, very softly.
Gooch bent down over her, and deliberately
blew his great mouthful of cigar-smoke into her
face; and she still sat, without moving and with
a fixed smile on her mouth. And Gooch smiled
broadly, too, and stood a moment, looking down
at her. Then he looked across the table at the
man and smiled at him also, and walked away,
back to where Percy waited near the door.
The man looked at the woman in a ghastly
silence, but with the flesh of his lower jaw quiver-
ing and twitching.
Then “ The butcher ! ” he said, snarling.
The woman, yet smiling, stared at him, and
then burst into a little peal of laughter.
What was almost a sigh of relief ran among
those who had seen.
“ A joke !” murmured someone, and the side-
ways watchers went on with their eating.
But the man got up, shaking.
“ Come on ! ” he said, “Come on! Let’s get
out o’ this place ! All these people ! ”
“O,” she muttered, “ you fool ! Why didn’t
you laugh ? Why don’t you laugh ? Or break his
neck ? ”
The man took no notice, but turned in haste and
groped clumsily for his own things, leaving the
woman to appeal to a waiter for hers.
She spoke again ta the man as he stood, waiting
and trembling.
“Coward ! Coward!” she purred; and
‘ Thing ! ’ and passed in front of him, down
between the other people, with her head high and
a brilliant color burning in her cheeks.
Percy opened the doors for them, still doglike.
“Good even’, Mrs. Gooch!” he breathed, as
she rustled by.
The Thing went out after her, with his shoul-
ders hunched up near his ears, and his hand shak-
ing as it slid along the bannister.
Percy followed through the doors, and leaned
over the stairhead, gaping.
